THE RED PAGE.
Edwin Arnold, a Lond. publisher, announces
his foundation of another “Colonial Library” at 2s. 6d.
and 3s. 6d.‘, and attacks Us with six novels abreast. There
is room for improvement ot the 2s. 6 d, covers—in a crude
yellow paper, with a distressingly ugly decoration.
“ Anything is good enough for the Colonies” no longer
with the $1.50c. American book on sale here at
3s. 6 d. —often well illustrated, and bound in attractive
cloth a book that publishers have taken pains with.
Nor does the matter of the Arnold books rise
far above mediocrity. Mr. Pane's Wild Oats, by
Charles Eddy, tells pleasantly enough the story of a
London discount broker’s social downfall and rehabilita-
tion. God's Scholars, by Charles Marsh, is an excellent
study of character in a Norfolk (Eng.) coastal village.
More exciting is The Longshoremen, by George Bart ram
—a story of Sussex (Eng.) smugglers in the earlv nine-
teenth century. The people have bold individuality, and
the plot develops well, despite some dull patches toward
the close. This is part : of, .the philosophy of the prince
Of smugglers—
All you heed to manage women is a way with you.
Rap out at 'em now and then—always do it when
there's no reason for it—throw a tankard at ’em if they
answer baok—then coax and pet ’em and chuck ’em
under the chin,-tell ’em about their eyes and hair,
praise their fancy in ribbons, and give’’em plenty of
money to spend, and take care that one day’s never
the same with ’em as another. A woman will stand
anything except meanness and a dull life—they ’re the
things that she can’t endure. Keep her interested—
that's the way. ”
The River of- Vengeance, by P. L. Oliphant, is the worst
of the batch, and bad consistently. It opens with a
shameless parody of “ Ouida,” and continues with a
shocking travesty of Russia.
Give me not too much finish. Let me he
To cold perfection strange, if so I must
Refine away the ardour of the soul
And catch the plague of ■wordy nothingness.
Let me not learn the trick of cunning terms,
That in a careful anguish mince along,
If so I place a harrier to truth,
Or hate the springs of natural eloquence,
Evblyn Phihmby in The Century,
~ln The Independent Review (N >v.) H, Ellis
generalises from the birth statistics of Britain, America,
and Australia, and concludes that “the Anglo-Saxon
race ib losing its reproductive power.” the Anglo-
Saxons are approaching a period when they will begin to
die out, and in some parts of the English-speaking
(world have already reaehed that point.?’ The article
gives additional proof (if proof were needed) that “the
decline of the birth-rate in N.S. Wales” is no isolated 1
phenomenon due to “ artificial cheeks,” but is the symp-
tom of a world-wide tendency.
SOME SYDNEY STATUES
The unveiling of the bas-relief that adorns the
facade of N.S. W. Art Gallery turns one’s thoughts to other
statuary work in and about Sydney. We have bronze
statues of Governor Bourke, Queen Victoria and her
Vonsort, Captain Cook, Governor Phillip, Thomas Mort,
and Dr. Lang, in addition to the numerous marble
imbecilities of our public parks and gardens. We have
atatuary, of sorts, on market, bank, and office buildings ■
we have the unhumourous grotesques that fill the Post
Office spandrils, and those other graven images that fail
to fill the niches of the Lands Office—and then there is
Dailey 1
Lord Rosebery once suggested that if the restless spirits
that possessed the Gadarene swine were to enter into the
statues of Edinburgh, and if the whole stony, brazen
troop were to hurry, hustle and huddle headlong down
the steepest place near Edinburgh into the deepest part
of the Firth of Forth, Art would sustain no serious
1055... Most of our statues have the Demon of Ugliness
Strong within them, though he, unhappily, drives them
not to their destruction ; but Australian Art would be
the gainer if some of them were converted into useful
road-metal, and the remainder summarily consigned to
the lime-kiln or the melting-pot.
£
A few of the Lands Office effigies may be taken
as an illustration. On the Gresham-streetside there is a
disproportionate Parkes with one wooden arm in a sling
and at the other end an irate Deniehy muttering a male-
diction on his trousers. Round the corner we find Went-
worth in a much-creased waistcoat, holding a pen that
might well be mistaken for a pahn-branch typifying, in
mediaeval fashion, the posthumous martyrdom to which
he has been subjected. Near him is John Robertson—
not the bluff and emphatic knight we knew—but a bland
and saintly John, timidly, and with unaccustomed mild-
ness, explaining the provisions of his famous Act
Above Wentworth stands J. S. Farnell in a frock
coat made on the block principle, similar to that
adopted by the Noah family in the Arks of our
childhood. One, however, seldom noticed the solidity
that characterised the Noahs’ garments, because the
base served them instead of fegt, which were always
strangely wanting. But it is not so with this statue'of
Farnell. It has both feet and legs, and, because of the
altititude of the niche in which it stands, these feet and
legs are painfully and conspicuously apparent, pro-
truding from the solid mass that forms the coat. The
sculptor, evidently, never gave a thought to the position
his statue would occupy, and neither he npr the others
appears to have considered what effect the inevitable
foreshortening would have upon their work.
It is undoubtedly an impossibility for a Hculptor to
achieve anything like an artistic success when his
subject is a full-length portrait-statue of any disiin-
guished, but physically commonplace man in ordinary
modern dress. Phidias himself would have failed had he
been set such a task. As a matter of fact, he seems to have
recognised the difficulty and shirked it, for in the famous
Parthenon frieze he clothes his horsemen in short cloaks
and helmets only, though we are tolerably certain that
the Greek cavalry of the time of Pericles were attired in
something more than this. But Phidias knew too well
the limitations of his art to make any concessions to
mere realism. Our Lands Office sculptors have not fol-
lowed his example, and the result is apparent in their
statues. It is a sorry spectacle to see, even in effigy, an
estimable middle-aged gentleman exposing himself to the
weather, and trying hard to look dignified while con-
scious all the time that his coat is a shockingly bad fit
and that his trousers even a statue, I think,'must feel
the ignominy of those trousers !
J*
In dismissing the Lands Office statues, of which
the best is bad, attention mav be called in passing to the
demented explorer labelled “Stuart,” standing hatless
under an apparently bright sun, and essaying a difficult
exercise in some novel system of physical development—
if one may judge from the position of his hand. The
ridiculous puppets in fancy dress that encumber the
Bridge-street faqade are absolutely beneath notice.
The statues on the Colonial Secretary’s Office are not
works of art, but, unlike those of the Lands Office, they
fill the niches in which they are placed, and, if looked at
merely as so much architectural detail, they are effective
and not altogether uninteresting.
Of our other statues, the monument to Phillip, in the
Palace Gardens, is undoubtedly the worst, because of its
pretentiousness. It is not a group, but a series of five
figures, and the principal one, in the insecurity of its
cheap, melodramatic pose, and its general air of absolute
inanity, resembles nothing so much as one of those
wooden effigies that were wont to adorn tobacconists’
doorways in the days gone by.
The statue of Cook cannot be called an unqualified
success; nor can that of Queen Victoria—spoilt by its
stagey, off-with-his-head attitude and its badly-crumpled
“ property ” train ; and criticism turns in horror from
the “ job lots ” with which the Botanic Gardens and the
Centennial Park are studded. The more one thinks of
these objects, the less one thinks of them.
The Markets statuary lacks simplicity and boldness ;
the effect aimed at is frittered away in a multiplicity of
details, and the contrast of the white marble and the
yellow freestone is unpleasant. This last defect is to be
noticed, too, in the marble statue of Queen Victoria
beneath the Post Office tower, and more markedly, per-
haps, in those somewhat lifeless statues of Birch’s on the
Australian Joint Stock Bank in George-street. How
much better with regard to colour is that graceful bronze
figure poised above the pediment of the Exchange !
S
The statue of Sir Richard Bourke is, perhaps,
the only one of which we have reason to feel proud. It
might take its place among the best portrait statues of
any country and suffer little by the comparison. The
sculptor was fortunate in being able to clothe his subject
in military uniform, and yet the fine effect of the work as
a whole is in no wise due to the dress, or even to the
pose : but solely to the arrangement of the long military
cloak. This was the one stroke of genius, the superfluous
thing necessary, as Voltaire has it, to transform a matter-
of-fact bronze figure into a work of art. Deprived of his
cloak, his Excellency Sir Richard Bourke would be as
little worthy of notice in the Domain as his predecessor,
Governor Phillip, is in the Palace Gardens, though he
would be always infinitely less ludicrous.
Next to that of Bourke comes the statue of the -Prince
Consort, in Hyde Park. It is dignified and impressive-
somewhat too much so, perhaps—but, nevertheless, it is
a really notable work.
Of the Gallery statuary it is unnecessary here to speak ;
for, with a few exceptions, it consists mostly of presen-
tation works of no great merit. Two piece's, however,
may be noticed, viz., Birch’s “ Retaliation ”—a fine study
of action—and Andreoni’s magnificent bronze, “ Who is
without sin among ye, let him cast the first stone.”
Of the many casts from the Antique, one may be per-
mitted, in passing, to express the opinion that they are
entirely unsuited to a Gallery such as ours. They are in-
teresting and valuable, but they should be in' a place
apart, not scattered about oromiscuously, to the destruc-
tion of their own effect and that of the pictures surround-
ing them. An exception might perhaps be made in the
case of the Venus de Milo, incomparable, and lovely even
in the lifelessness of plaster. Incomparable, one may
well say, for her only possible rival, the world-famed
Venus de Medici, is, after all, merely an ideally pretty
woman, fresh from .hb Dath, and altogether too human
not to be coquettishly ashamed of her nakedness ; but
the other—call her Venus, or what you will—is beyond
question a goddess ; a superbly and triumphantly beauti-
ful goddess, serene in the unmindful consciousness of her
divinity.
£
Sculpture is a purely decorative art, and it is
never farther from its ideal than when it seeks to
emulate the painful realism of a wax-work show. But
Governments and fatuous memorial com-

IRISH
The Irish-speaking folk say that the English
language compared with the Irish is as stiff canvas to
f“PP' e s , llk - A great picture may be painted on the can-
faK artlst > lmt the silk is in itself a magical
1 u at gla,| , ces > and shines, and darkens with all the
subtle hues and shadows of life and of the kingdom of
KwiV’u • “ ls onl -}' the masters of style who can write

is as close as the breath, as deep as the heart, as strange
fiL 6 S^K U ’u and , as lntimate > and yet mysterious, as the

™L™. Urmur 0f s t rea ms, the cry of the birds that pass

song of the sap in the trees, the whispering of the dead in
tneir graves.
• 1 r*; ad the other day of how a scientific man had
indented a machine by means of which he could hear
p ants growing. This is merely the re-discovery of a lost
; ... ih ? oelts knew all about it long ago. Have they
not the story of the blind giant who used to sit on the
battlements of his castle in the morning and listen to the
grass growing? Surely they should know all about these
things. Consider the advantages they had in the
egmning. They came to Europe after the disappearance
of neolithic man, who left behind him some- cartoons on
*£° ks > , bu 't n ° literature. They were the first of all extant
races to look upon the Western World. They walked
amongst shadows in the Dawn of Time. Small wonder
that they have been called the Twilight Race.
It is in Irish that the heart opens like a rose, and flashes
come from “ that little, infinite, faltering, eternal flame
that one calls; oneself.” This is the result of an inborn
mental attitude—a legacy from the days when speech
was used to express, without reticence or circumlocution,
the thought that burned in the brain. In those fine
days all Nature, animate and inanimate, responded to an
appeal addressed to her in Irish. When a poet was in
trouble, or one of his friends was dead, he called with
confidence for the sympathy of the skies, and the sun

I have before me a book, recently published, by
Eady Gregorv. Its title is “ Poets and Dreamers.” It
introduces to me a poet, of whom I am ashamed to say,
I never heard before. Raftery was his name. He was a
wandering bard, like Abou ben Zeyd, “ a singing man of
the singers, the world’s guest and a stranger.” He lived
in Connaught in the early years of the last century, and
travelled over the country from house to house singing
songs of his own composition in Irish, and playing upon
a little fiddle. When a couple were linked together for
life it was a marriage but not a wedding if Raftery were
not there. He was blind like his predecessor, Homer
and was always poor, but he had the heart that goes all
the way without complaining. Says he :
“I am Raftery the poet, full of hope and love ; with
eyes without light, with gentleness without misery.
“ Going west on my journey with the light of my
heart; weak and tired to the end of my road. lam now
and my back to a wall, playing music to empty pockets.”
He is described as being a thin man—who ever heard of
a wandering fat poet?—not very tall, with a long frieze
coat and corduroy trousers. He died at seventy years of
age on Christmas Eve in the year 1835, and was buried in
Killeenan. When he was alive he “travelled Ireland
challenging all the poets of that time. There were hun-
dreds of country poets in those days, and a welcome for
them all.” Here are a few translated extracts from an
elegy he wrote on a fiddler friend of his by the name of
Thomas O’Daly :
“The swans on the water are nine times blacker than a
blackberry since the man died from us that had pleasant-
ness on the top of bis fingers. His two grey eyes were
like the dew of the morning that lies on the grass
“He was the beautiful branch. In every quarterthat
he ever knew he would scatter his fill and not gather Ha
would spend the estate of the Dalys, their beer and their
wine. And that he may be sitting in the chair of grace
in the middle of Paradise....
“ No flower in any garden, and the leaves of the trees
have leave to cry, and they falling on the ground
There is no green flower oil the.tops of the tufts since
there did a boarded coffin go on Daly.”
The fine effort of imagination in regard to the swans
m the foregoing, does not find the soft place in my heart
so surely as the reference to the prodigality' of the
deceased, and the delightful suggestion that God should
reward him for the same. But there was nothing mean
about Raftery. In another of his poems on “ The Rising
Again of Ireland” he tells how, when she has risen”
“ ships will be coming from France and Spain, and from
all the countries ; and there will be no rent on the land
and every poet will he given a fee of twenty-one pounds ’’’
How exact he is in his statement! It makes one think of
the measurements given by St. John in his description of
the New Jerusalem. Exit Raftery. He was a wanderer
on the face of his own small patch of earth all his days
but his name is held in more kindly remembrance than
the names of many a Connaught magnate of his time who
rode royally after, and to, the dogs.
Lady Gregory spent three days in a Connaught
workhouse, taking down old stories and legends from the
lips of the inmates. She says : “As I listened, I was
moved by the strange contrast between the poverty of
the tellers and the splendour of the tales. These men
who had failed in life, and were old or withered, or sickly
or crippled, had not laid up dreams of good houses and
fields, and sheep and cattle ; for they had never possessed
enough to think of the possession of more as a possibility.
It seemed as if their lives so poor and rigid in
circumstance that they did not fix their minds, as more
prosperous people might do, on thoughts of customary
pleasure. The stories that they love are of quite visionary
things ; of swans that turn into king’s daughters, and of
castles with crowns over the doors, and lovers’ flights on
the backs of eagles, an<j music-loving water-witches and
journeys to the other world, and sleeps that last for seven
hundred years.”
Compare this with Carlyle’s picture of an English work-
house and its stolid, squalid, helpless, hopeless, apathetic
denizens.!
Here is part of a translation from an Aran (West of
Ireland) ballad, and I would be pleased to know where
you will find anything like it in English. It is called
“ The Grief of a Girl’s Heart’’—
“ O Douall og, if you go across the sea bring myself
with you, and do not forget it; and you will have a
sweetheart tor fair days, and market days, and the
daughter of the King of Greece beside you at night..
“ It is late last night the dog was speaking of you ; the
snipe was speaking of you in her deep marsh, it is you
are the lonely bird through the woods ; and that you may
be without a mate until you find me..
“ O, ochone, and it’s not with hunger or with wanting
food, or drink, or sleep, that I am growing thin, and my
life is shortened ; but it is the love of a young man has
withered me away..
“ When I go by myself to the Well of Loneliness I sit
down and go through my trouble ; when I see the world
and do not see my boy, he that has amber shade in his
hair.
“It was on that Sunday I gave my love to you : the
Sunday that is last before Easter Sunday. And myself
on my knees reading the Passion ; and my two eyes
giving love to you forever. . . My heart is as black as the
blackness of the sloe, or as the black coal that is on the
smith’s forge ; or as the sole of a shoe left in white halls ;
it was you put that darkness over my life.. . You have
taken the East from me ; you have taken the West from
me ; you have taken what is before me and what is
behind me; you have taken the moon, you have taken
the sun from me; and my fear is great that you have
taken God from me.’’
There it is in an alien tongue. Imagine what it must
sound like in its own Irish !
#
The Twilight Race is, in Ireland, making a
brave effort to rescue its rich and noble language from
the maw of Oblivion. But the hard, unsympathetic
Spirit of the Age says—“ What is the use of it? Will it
help in the development of Commerce? Is there any
money in it ? ” And we must reply to these questions in
the words of the old mournful refrain—
“ Seagan O’Dwyer a GHeannu,
We’re worsted in the game.”

Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIG TIG ID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen—ln answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt , with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very dilfi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLLEV
GENUINE BELTS give GENUINE RELIEF.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Exiles From Dreamland.
Where the ebb-tide of the sunset laps the floating
peaks in gold ;
Where the moon-glade, molten silver, on the
fields of sea lies cold ;
Where the columns of the rainbow in the cliffs of
mist are set,
We, the exiles from our Palace, see its splendors
mirrored yet.
Ah, that we once more might dwell there—snatch
a golden hour to spend
At the looms where dreams are fashioned, where
their shining colors blend —
Till the Gates of Morn swing open, for the Fates
will have it so,
And the gleaming looms are broken, and ’tis time
for us to go!
So we leave it, looking backward on the Palace
that we knew,
Going slowly, going sadly, bidding all our dreams
adieu;
While the vision-haunted towers, and the peals of
fairy bells,
Fade from sight and die from hearing as the last
carillon swells.
One long lo_.k ! Flash back the sunshine, Oye
walls of Fairyland !
Make the vision one to treasure when those walls
no longer stand.
Then, farewell! The splendor passes as it reaches
greatest worth,
In a blaze of brighter glory than our sun may
bring to birth.
VIO.
RETTLEDRUM.
Wine and Rosea.
The wine is red
As my hot blood. A star
Glows in its heart as burns a star in mine;
And it is said
The God of Things That Are
Spins in their orbits all the stars that shine.
So let us rest!
While, sounding sweet and low,
Your crooning song my troubled thoughts com-
poses;
And from the West
Soft breezes sweep, and blow
The heavy scent of red, red lover’s roses.
Ah 1 melody
Of rose-scent and guitar !
Ah ! voice and fingers that make song of all!
How sweet ’twould be
If God Who dwells afar
Would bear us, singing, to His Festival;
And in the West,
So rich in red and gold,
Bid us go '.ree to gather dew-wet posies,
Where Love is blest
And none grow soured and old,
But dream and dream among the red, red roses.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
“Little Love.”
Little Love woke with the sun in her eyes,
Woke an ■ looked over the ridges
With a thought in her heart that was sweet with
surprise.
“ Ah ! how golden the sun on the far wattle lies
Where he shall come over the ridges.”
Little Love leaned from her window to see
The dew of the morn on the roses.
“How fair that olosed bud on the morrow shall
be ;
I shall beg him to reach up and pluck it for me,
And crown me his queen of the roses.”
Little Love sleeps with the moon on her eyes,
The moon that rose over the ridges.
How wondrously still, and how whitely she lies—
Hands clasped o’er the bosom that scarce seems
to rise
To the breath of the breeze from the ridges.
Little Love sleeps so waxen and cold,
As white as her own snowy roses ;
A lover to waken her needs must be bold—
Ah ! Love when he comes to his bride will be told
To cover her coffin with roses !
Q.
M. FORREST.
Voiceless.
Out of the long, lone night,
Out of our earth-homes low,
The voice of the voiceless dead
That lie in a restless bed
Rises all filled with woe.
Softer than falls the rain
Fell on my heart their thought—
Wrapt in a winding sheet,
Bound at the brows and feet
We hear, but we answer naught.
Faces we dearly love
Weeping above us come ;
But still in the earth we lie,
Still without word or cry,
Stirred to the heart, but dumb.
Not though they strive and toil
Lone in a friendless land,
May we from prison leap
Out of our seeming sleep,
Comfort in voice and hand.
Oh, for a tongue to speak !
Oh, for a word to say !
Wrapt in an icy sheet,
Bound at the brows and feet,
Wait we the Coming Day.
N.S.W.
CHAS. E. HOWELL.

Eight Years’ Dyspepsia.
A LONG PERIOD OF SUFFERING.
A WELCOME FRIEND IN TIME OF NEED.
The Case of Mire. 8. BROWN.
(BY oub reporter.)
If you are imbued with any desire to gain
an insight into the methods adopted by
people whose efforts are attended with suc-
cess, you cannot do better than read the
following narrative, which was related by
Mrs. Selina Brown, of No. 5 Little Edward-
street, Pyrmont.
“ One day,” said Mrs. Brown, “as I was
talking to a young girl at the front door, a
strange and overpowering sensation came
over me, which caused me to have a fit, and
I was taken indoors and put to bed, where
I was lying unconscious for three days. A
doctor was called in, but he said that he
could not account for the sudden turn I
had taken. He prescribed some medicine,
which did not have any beneficial effect,
as three weeks later I took another fit in my
sleep, after which I was so feeble that I had
to keep to my bed for several days, and
again the doctor was brought to my bed-
side.”
“Previous to taking the fits were you in
good health ? ” asked the reporter.
“ I was not at all well, as you may gather
from the fact that for eight years I suffered
from indigestion,” answered Mrs. Brown.
“ For a long time I seemed as if I had no
use in my body to work at all. I felt
languid and depressed in spirits, and did
not care whether I lived or died ; neither
could I sleep well. It was a perfect misery
for me to go to bed, a 9 my mind was so un-
settled that I felt as if I wanted to walk
about all night. Just as I thought I was
falling asleep I commenced to think about
some troublesome subject which generally
occupied my mind till morning, and when I
got up I was too exhausted to do anything,
and with a taste in my mouth that made
everything seem bitter. The only sleep I
got was always accompanied by dreadful
dreams, so that I never got any proper rest.
The sense of hunger was a stranger to me
entirely, but in spite of that I took a little
food occasionally, or else I should have
starved.”
“ Did the food do you any good ? ”
“ I am sure it did not do me much good,
if any, for I was continually getting weaker
and thinner, and the worst of it was that
eating always gave me such awful pains in
the stomach, where windy spasms made me
feel as if I wanted to tear my clothes off, no
matter how loose they were. A heaviness
in my chest also troubled me greatly, for as
each breath was drawn a smothering sensa-
tion came over me, and at the same time I
suffered acutely from pains under my
shoulder-blades and about my back. Every-
thing possible seemed to go against me at
once, as I was often incapacitated from
going about the house owing to my feet
swelling and my limbs aching, across the
loins especially Unless I was very careful
and slow in my movements I became so
giddy that I could not stand, while extrema
nervousness was another torture. I was
afraid to move in the dark lest something
would happen to me, and if anything gave
me the least excitement my nerves shook to
such an extent that the vibration of my
limbs was quite visible. Little did I think
that something even worse than these ail-
ments was jr store for me, but my experi-
ence goes to show how a person’s constitu-
tion is gradually wrecked, and what fright-
ful results are likely to follow. After my
sudden seizures I was in a more hopeless
state than ever. My head pains were so
bad that I could not bear anybody to speak
to me, and any food that I took was always
vomited. The medicines and powders which
were given to me were of no avail, and at
length I crawled out of bed and went to
another doctor, who told me I ought to go
to a hospital. Having three young children
I did not like doing so, and instead of going
to a hospital I came home.”
“ Were you wise in going agauut the
doctor’s advice?”
“It turned out to be the best thing I
could have possibly done, as a neighbour
shortly afterwards came in to see me for the
purpose of reading a cure performed by
Clements Tonic. When she had finished
the storj r she exclaimed : * That is just how
you are. Why don’t you try it?’ 1 said,
‘I believe I will.’ When my husband
came home he thought I was going
to the hospital, but I said, ' I’m not
going, Bring me home a bottle of
Clements Tonic, and I will try it.’ On
my word of honor, I felt a slight im*
provement when I had drank only half a
bottle. Just imagine my delight after, the
way I had been disappointed with all other
medicines, especially as I had long since
thought that I was doomed to die, because
I could not get relief. But Clements Tonic
soon eased the terrific pains in my head
(which extended right down my neck), and
when, in a few days, they were gone alto-
gether I could get about a little, for I also
felt more vigorous than I had been for a long
time. The first good night’s rest I had had
for many a month was obtained through the
comforting influences of Clements Tonic, and
when this grand experience had been re-
peated several nights in succession I felt
quite new, for I was happy in the belief
that I might yet recover my health.®2very
tissue in my body seemed to be strengthened
by the wonderful medicine I was taking,
and for sharpening my appetite it was just
the thing. Before long I sat down to my
meals like everybody else in the house, and
I could always take a fair share of whatever
was placed on the table. The neighbours
were intensely surprised to see me up and
about once more, as they had entertained
little hope of ever seeing me well again, I
had no trouble in keeping my food down,
and every day I found greater relief from
the symptoms of indigestion. The. pa ns
finally left my stomach and shoulders, and
I could breathe without gasping as I used
to, for the oppression had disappeared from
my chest, aud with returning strength my
limbs acquired freedom from all aches.
When I was taking my doctor’s medicines 1
had no desire for anything, but Clements
Tonic gave me a b'ght-hearted inclination to
see everything. By its continued use I
forgot what nervousness was, as I knew no
fear, and my nerves were as steady as a
rock. I was never a stout woman, jet my
diseases had dragged me down considerably,
but C ements Tonic was my sole medicine
till my lost weight was regained, and, re-
markable as it may seem, that is all I
required, for I was then quite cured of
everything. None of my former ailments
were left benind by Clements Tonic, and I
am only speaking the truth when I say that
my life was saved by that medicine. If you
like you can publish my story in any way
you deem fit, as it may alleviate the sufferings
of many more.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Selina Brown, of 5 Little Edward-street,
Pyrmont, Sydney, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of four folios, and
consecutively numbered from one to four, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic; and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements —which I give voluntarily, with-
out receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Pyrmont, this
tenth day of March, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
JOS. MoDOUGALL, J.P.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 31, 1903.
Pbioe 6d.
BEFORE
G. H. Reid : “ What! darnce with you 1
I wouldn’t associate with the likes
of you! ”
“I was not prepared for the apostacy and treachery to
their vital principle, shown hy a majority of the members
of the Labor party of Australia. I wish to say this to the
Labor members of the late Parliament, and the Labor
members of the. next Parliament, that if I could win a
thousand offices by abandoning my feeling of bitter hos-
tility and contempt for their unfaithfulness to their
principles, I would not do it.”-
Mr. Reid at Paddington.
AND AFTER TAKING A GENERAL ELECTION
G.H.R.: “ISN’T IT MY TURN FOR A DARNCE NOW?”

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 31, 1003.
To the Federal Labor Party.
Thb recent Federal ejections make it urgently
necessary that the Federal Labor party should
reconsider its position. The old situation has
become anomalous, and it will soon become
untenable. So far, the Labor party in the Aus-
tralian Parliament has occupied the same position
as the corresponding party does in the N.S.W.
State Legislature ; and that pos tion, if the
N.S.W, case is any criterion, is one which is
almost certain to end badly. The party has
professed to hold itself aloof from the most urgent
question of the day—the fiscal question—and to
have no opinion, as a party, on the matter where
a strong opinion is most wanted. It was supposed
to be ready to vote either for helping or ruining
Australian industries if it got its price—said price
to be paid in political concessions. It was
understood that it wasn’t looking for office itself,
but was to be a mere appendage, on certain con-
ditions, of one or other of the two remaining par-
ties, to both of which it was more or less hostile.
It was never to be the dog—only the tail. This
was commonly recognised as its position, and
while it was a weak party the position was under-
standable. But now it ranks second among the
three factions which make up the Australian Par-
liament, and next election will probably place it
first. It can’t be the tail and thus shirk its
responsibilities much longer; it must look for-
ward to being the whole dog, or at least most of
the dog. It is impossible that the biggest party
in Parliament—as the Labor party promises soon
to be—can always profess to have no opinion upon
the most urgent of political questions, to have
no financial policy, to be in no way responsible
whether the finances go well or ill, or whether the
official departments are administered this way or
that. It will have to take up the White Man’s
Burden—the whole of it.
* # * *
The Labor party is now a good half of the
crowd which keeps Premier Deakin in office.
Therefore it should demand half the Ministerial
portfolios. It should take half the responsibility
of administration, and half the blame of failure,
when there is failure—and half the salary. It
should show that its leaders can administer depart-
ments,'and draft Bills, and lead the House, and
encounter financial emergencies, as well as any
John Forrest or Pumpkin Smith or other incon-
siderable character. Out of respect for itself and
for the people who sent it into Parliament it
should do this. ,As things are, two armies
do the regular fighting and a third reserves to
itself the right to fire on either of the others.
This may be profitable for a time, but it isn’t
war This triangular attitude has been tried in
NSW, and after temporary success it promises
to’ end in disgrace. The N.S.W. Labor party
supported Reid and Lyne and See in return for
valuable concessions; that is to say, because
thev gave it those concessions it allowed them to
ruinously botch the finances and make the
State a disgrace and a mockery. If it had
definitely joined one side, and insisted on its
full share of the portfolios, it might have taken
the concessions instead of having them granted
to it, and it wouldn’t have needed to connive at
disgraceful public frauds as the price of them. It
might have kept the Government straight, and
given the country honesty, solvency and pro-
sperity in return for concessions. Then the State
would be grateful to it, and Labor in N.S. VV.
politics wOuld be a power and something to be
respected. Just now it is so little respected that
it will probably, unless it reforms, meet with
something not far short of extinction at next State
elections.
a C
The view The Bulletin takes in thismitter
can be best understood by comparing the relative
situations of N.S.W. and Maoriland. The two
States commenced their Parliamentary Labor
movement about the same time, and under very
similar circumstances—also under very bad cir-
cumstances, though those of Maoriland were the
worse of the two. But the Maoriland Labor
Sarty didn’t decide to hang fire halfway between
Jjssell and the Ballance-Seddon combination.
It merged itself in the Liberal party, with the
result that the Government became a Labor
Government, and, for the best part of 13 years, it
has remained so. The Labor element had the
courage to provide itself with fiscal principles and
a financial policy. It took the responsibility when
things went wrong. It took, personally, the job
of putting things right. It proved to the world
that a Labor Government can also be a perfectly
safe Government. It proved, by administering
the State departments and running the State
finances, that it had as much political intelligence
as the best of its opponents. And when it wants
reform it enacts it; there is no necessity for
buying each reform by winking at some new
swindle or conniving at some old sin—by keeping
this incapable in office or that “drunk” in
authority. It doesn’t require to purchase con-
cessions for its own class by allowing the whole
State to be swindled ; under the steadying influ-
ence of responsibility it legislates, and legislates
well, for all classes, and therefore the scoff about
“class legislation ” is hardly ever heard now. The
results are, that it has got far more concessions
than the N.S.W. party has done. Also, it has
collected official salaries for many years. Yet,
while Maoriland is many miles ahead of N.S.W.
in democratic legislation, its doings never fill the
outside world with the alarm that the much less pro-
gressive N.S.W. Labor party does, mainly because
it doesn’t present the alarming spectacle of power
without responsibility. All the Maoriland Demo-
crats have lost in doing this is the satisfaction of
calling themselves a Labor party, and that sacri-
fice is more of a gai r ' h m a h'SS.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Democrats adopted, instead, the
policy of aloofness and irresponsibility. The
idea worked reasonably well for a while, but
a dry-rotting process was inevitable, and it is now
so far advanced that the whole system is tottering.
For a long time the nominal Government has
very seldom had much real power of its own,
therefore politics have degenerated into a scram-
ble of tenth-rate men —the sort of men who are
content to accept office on such terms. There
has been no man of anything like the calibre of
Richard Seddon, George Turner or C. C.
Kingston in N.S.W. State politics for many
years. There isn’t one who seems to stand
on the same level with Browne of Queensland,
with Trenwith, or Isaacs —there is hardly even a
Propsting or an Allan M‘Lean. The Labor
party holds the balance and makes or unmakes
Ministries—that is to say, it has the power of
choosing between two incapables. Meanwhile it
has never been in office ; it has never learned how
to administer public affairs; it has no financial
policy; it hardly even attempts to draft a Bill.
Its business consists in demanding concessions
which often cost money, and in leaving the
Government to find the money, and on this basis
it finds itself called upon to wink at the dis-
reputable means by which the Government finds
the money. It has done this until the wink has
become a habit. It doesn’t get many concessions,
and it has to wink at some iniquity in return for
each concession. It has grown sleepy because it
hasn’t much responsibility to keep it awake, and
the Government has grown demoralised—partly,
at least, because its soul isn’t its own,
* * * *
The Australian Parliament is young as yet, and
the evils of the triangular system haven’t had
time to develop, but if the system is persisted in
they will develop. The object of the Federal
Labor party should be to coalesce with the
Ministerialists, and to replace such men as
Forrest and Fysh with rnen of the stamp of
Watson, Trenwith and Kingston. The com-
bination would force the Tory element out of the
Ministry, and make the Ministerial party more
democratic than it has ever yet been. _ It would
probably make it so democratic that it would be
all a Labor party, only without the name, and the
name is better dropped in any case. It would
relieve the Ministry of its present necessity to
take in weak Tories —a necessity arising out
of the fact that strong Democrats remain
aloof, posing as a third party, which is in
Parliament yet not wholly of it. It would enable
Labor men to get Ministerial experience, and it
would relieve them of the stigma of aiming at
power without accepting its burdens. The pre-
sent system, under which the Labor party
demands old age pensions and such-like expensive
reforms, without having itself to devise the means
of finding the money, is demoralising all round ;
the one party is apt to ask recklessly because it
hasn’t to find the means, and the other party is
apt to finance recklessly because it never knows
what new demand will be sprung upon it, and it
has only the obloquy of imposing the taxes
while someone else gets the credit of securing
the reform. Again, a coalition would force
the Labor party to find a definite financial
?olicy, and no party is complete without one.
t would teach the Labor party to take a wider
interest in legislation, and avert any risk of it
petrifying into a mere class institution which con-
fined itself to questions of pick-and-shovel interest.
It would destroy the Tory fictions about the
dangers of Labor legislation ; the present Federal
Labor party contains men of a high order of
intelligence who are well-fitted to destroy that
ancient bogey by practical demonstration. It
would put an end to the gibe that the Ministry is
a poor-spirited combination holding office on
sufferance, and the Labor party a sordid combina-
tion selling its support for a consideration.
* * * *
Already, though things have so far gone pretty
smoothly, the impracticability of the present
system has shown itself. If there had been a
coalition Government a few months ago, the votes
of the Labor Ministers would have made it certain
that the clause bringing railway employees within
the scope of the Arbitration Bill would not have
been excised from that unlucky measure. But
because there were no Labor Ministers, the
Cabinet was filled up with ancient Tories like
Forrest and Fysh, and because of that element
in the Cabinet, the clause was excised. The Labor
party had no redress against the Tory Forrest
except by selling itself bodily to the Tory nigj- -
a- vocate, Reid. Counting the strong Democratic
element in the Protectionist party, and tne
wholly Democratic element in the Labor party,
it was a House with a powerful Democratic
majority. Yet by reason of the foolish triangular
system the strongest Democratic element m it
was reduced to considering whether it woulan
sell itself to a Tory leader, in order to pumsli
the partially-Democratic leader who had gone
back on it. Under the Maoriland system the
original Arbitration Bill would simply have been
carried; but the balance of power, as held
by the Australian Labor party, though a
powerful element for destruction, isn t good
for constructive purposes. And this sort or
trouble will arise again and again, it the
present system continues —it will keep rising till
the best men quit the Cabinet. To a man like
Turner, with Watson and Trenwith as col-
leagues, the drafting of a progressive policy would
be easy. The same Turner, with Forrest and
Fysh as colleagues to harass him by giving a lory
bias to the Cabinet, and Watson as an irrespon-
sible outside boss ready to give him notice to quit
because of that bias, will one day find the position
intolerable. It has already become intolerable to
Kingston. It is the sort of position that, some
day or other, will become intolerable to every
man who is worth retaining, and the control of
Federal politics will drift into the hands of supple
inferior men who can stand any sort of position
for a salary. And by the time that end is
achieved, J. C. Watson, who might have learned,
in office, to be one of the best Ministers Australia
ever had, will probably have wilted down into
another tired M'Gowen. There are two ways of
managing an elective monarchy. One is to make
the best man king and let him run the show. The
other is to have any inferior sort of man as king,
and let the best man yell outside the palace and
threaten, if the king doesn’t do the right thing, to
destroy him when he comes out, and make a worse
man king in his stead. Also there are two ways
of being a great novelist. One is for Dickens to
write “David Copperfield” himself. The other
is for Dickens to give Kenneth Mackay the
job of writing it, and compel him to be a literary
genius by the threat that, if he isn’t one, the job
will be taken from him and given to John
Haynes, of Sydney. In each of these instances
the man who stands by represents the Labor
party, and it is, on the whole, a very close resem-
blance.
* * * *
For all which (and other) reasons The Bulletin
reckons that the Australian Labor party should
aim at a coalition ; should widen its policy so far
as to enable it to absorb all the democratic
elements of the Government party ; should insist
on supplying half the Cabinet, to the exclusion of
the worst Tories now in office ; and should aim at
being the dog—not merely the tail. Then the
degrading spectacle of Geokge Reid coming
round to buy the party will be seen no longer. It
will cease to be for sale.
The Intolerable Press Situation.
When Australian Democrats have recovered from
the blast of Foreign-trade shrieks with which the
last fortnight has been filled, it is up to them to
sit down and quietly consider how the Australian
press situation affects them. In every State that
situation has a character of its own ; and nowhere
is the character good. In N.S. W. and Victoria it
is the worst. Queensland feels the want of a
democratic, national daily least of all. For one
thing, the Worker covers much ground there;
the platform influence of the Laborites and other
Democrats is greater than it is anywhere else;
and the provincial press is, on the whole, more
virile, independent, and powerful. South Aus-
tralia’s position is not so good as Queensland’s, but
it is better than that of Victoria or New South
Wales. In the Advehtisek it has an Opportunist
journal with Liberal tendencies. Bonython’s
paper would like to go on the straight, democratic
path ; but its boss is in politics, and is mixed up
with a dozen local institutions, and isin “society,”
and wants to make enough money to buy an old
English estate; and so the democratic path is
often too hard and too narrow. The Advertiser
won’t fight; it is desperately afraid of making
enemies; and so it lets things slide. Thus, though
it is a professed Protectionist, it allows Freetraders
like Glynn and Poynton, not to mention Speaker
Holder, to get places in the Reps, without a
challenge. If it did fight, the chances are that it
would do it very much as the Melbourne Age
does, though it is not easy to imagine the Daily
Bonython being quite as unscrupulous as the
Daily Syme. What Syme is it is hard to say.
He is a, rich man, and his friends are mostly rich
men, and he doesn’t want either his own or his
friends’ wealth affected. Yet he was a Liberal,
and he wants to seem a Liberal still; so he puts in
an antidote with every dose of poison now, and
neutralises its effect. Bonython really gets
much the same result in another way; but
he lets the people do the mixing. Syme
also has ambitions. Bonython’s ambitions
are personal; Syme lives through his paper and
hungers to see it victorious. And so, even
though his dose does nothing either way, he
fights to make the people take it, and sticks at
little to gain his end. He makes a thousand
enemies where the Adelaide man makes one ; but,
on the other hand, he is feared, and the other
isn’t. So dissimilar, they are yet. almost equally
unsatisfactory from the Democratic and National
view point.
• * • •
Sydney’s position is different again. Adelaide
won’t fight at all, and Melbourne won’t fight fair ;
but Sydney fights on the wrong side. In its daily
press Australian Nationalism has practically no
expression. Herald and Telegraph are both tied
to the Foreign-trader’s wheel, and the Evening
News hangs on behind. The situation is worse
now, in fact, than ever it was before. Until lately
only the Telegraph fought with both hands and
feet, determined to get victory for its side any-
how. The Herald wanted the same victory,
but “Old John Fairfax” had left it some
scruples and some clean traditions, and “Mister
James” kept them alive. Unfortunately, the
drought or something killed them ; anyway, they
are no longer in use, and to-day the paper which
once was proud of sitting in the place of judge,
descends to the tricks, the perversions, the twists
of the most disreputable, beer-fed, and beer-fee’d
Eolice-court advocate. And by plundering the
tate to have these city papers poured into the
country at little or no expense to the proprietors,
these “great” metropolitan journals have just
about strangled the provincial papers, and made
almost the whole of them poor, anaemic little
things, with a soul in keeping with their bodies,
and with influence in keeping with both.
Every now and then when a harder pinch than
usual is felt, there is an isolated movement to “do
something.” Occasionally something is actually
attempted. Adelaide, Sydney, and Melbourne,
in that order, have lately had new daily paper
prospectuses put before them. In Melbourne,
one new paper has, in fact, materialised j and
another is talked of. But with no daily press to
show the public how badly the new organs are
needed, the progress is appallingly slow. Meet-
ings may be held, but, at best, only a handful of
men can be got to attend ; and the existing papers
will either not report the proceedings at all or
simply misreport and ridicule them. The Mel-
bourne papers do the former ; the Sydney papers
have done the latter. For new papers to take the
Fat Man’s side money can still be got; enough
was got in Brisbane, the smallest of the Eastern
State capitals, to lately put the Daily Mail on
its feet. But for a paper which will take the
democratic side the capitalist seems to have
no funds; and if he had, he would not be
warmly welcomed, because of. the fear that,
before long, he would become the same
as the present proprietors who started on the
same side. If, however, the capitalist is not to be
encouraged to go into the business, co-operation is
the onty alternative. The Labor unions ought,
even of themselves, to be quite able to supply the
funds. They have now—in N.S.W., anyhow—
no need to build up large strike funds ; legislation
is doing effectively what the strike used to do
ineffectively. Without any undue strain, then,
they ought to be able to supply even the whole of
the necessary capital. But the movement must
not be confined to any one State. The newspaper
must be a better newspaper than those which now
mislead the people ; and to be that it
must share the cost of production with
others. It must have a cable service ; and
no one paper can afford a cable service. If
a Sydney paper attempted to supply one,
it would, compared with its local competi-
tors, carry a handicap of £20,000 or £25,000
a year; for if the present Australian cable
service costs £25,000 a year, none of the Sydney
papers pays more than £3OOO a year of the
total. The same with other services, literary and
otherwise. It may look easier to establish one
paper than six ; but it will be a great deal easier
to keep six papers alive than one, and when this
is realised it ought to be a good deal easier to get
capital than it has been so far.
• * * *
For The Bulletin’s part, it would be better
pleased to see a crop of papers that would not be
bound exclusively to the Labor party. The
Labor party will not concern itself, as a party,
with more than a comparatively few things, and a
daily paper must concern itself with almost every-
thing. But there is no very good reason why the
Labor organisations should require exclusive
possession of a paper. In fact, the paper that
goes out with the brand of “Labor paper” upon
it is not so likely to make converts to Laborism
as the paper which, while advocating all or most
of the things which theLaborite requires, has views
upon other things—views for which others besides
Laborites will read it. Even, indeed, if it did
not advocate all that they seek in its leading
columns, it would be an enormous gain if it would
report them fairly when they speak. The
situation, then, is that the Democrats and
Nationalists of Australia have practically no
daily press, and they remain handicapped
and helpless because no one section is
strong enough to establish a press of its
own. And, so far, each section has been too
exacting to think of working with the other
sections; it has wanted its press to speak for it
and nobody else ; and the result is that, demand-
ing all, it gets nothing. It is time for Democrats
to be less exacting ; to dwell less upon their small
differences and more upon the large principles
they have in common ; and they will find that of
the latter they have quite enough on which to
build a successful paper in each State. Co-
operation among the democratic sections in each
State, and co-operation among the Democrats of
all the States, seems the only way out of a posi-
tion which all Democrats must feel has become
intolerable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Trenwith, the Slayer of Symeism.
Symeism in Victoria is dead. It may resurrect if
Syme mends his ways, but at present it is very dead.
The power of the Age, which was sufficient to make
and unmake Ministries, to foist on the people non-
entities and crush outbrave independent men, has
been shattered. David Syme, glorying in all the
strength of his political machine paper, started
out to put down William Arthur Trenwith,
who, as Minister for Railways in the second
Turner Government, resisted the claims upon
the taxpayers’ money for land taken for the
construction of the Warburton railway line.
Several times it looked as if Syme had accomplished
his work. He taunted and aggravated Trenwith
to such an extent that he made certain indis-
creet remarks about several of Syme’s em-
ployees, one of whom overwhelmed him with
law costs in defence of a slander action.
The final struggle between Syme and Trenwith
came over the Victorian Senate election. Tren-
with, knowing the odds against him, resigned his
safe seat in the Legislative Assembly, and came
out for the Senate. The Age put forth all its
strength to crush him. It declined to allow him
to advertise his meetings in its columns, and
totally ignored his existence as a candidate.
Trenwith’s courage, character and ability
appealed to the electors, and they put
him at the top of the poll with over 100,000
votes to his credit. So to-day Symeism
lies badly smashed, while Trenwith makes a
triumphal entry into the Federal Parliament.
Trenwith’s fight against Symeism brought out
all the best characteristics of the man. All his
life he has been doing battle against heavy
odds, surmounting disadvantages and difficulties.
Character is the wage paid by Nature to man for
the labor of overcoming difficulties, and in this
respect Trenwith has been handsomely rewarded.
He has been 35 years in Victorian politics, and no
other man in the community has a straighter
record, and no other man, except George I urner,
could have won 100,000 votes. Trenwith’s life is
an interesting study. He was born of a boot-
making father and a book-loving mother at
Launceston, Tasmania, in 1847. At the age ot
seven he was learning his father’s trade and
feeling the influence of his mother’s books, which
filled two large boot trunks. He never attended
school in the ordinary sense. His mother had been
ateacher, and all helearned hegotfromherat home.
She died while he was still a child, and his father
married again. Young Trenwith was unable to
get along with his stepmother, and at 13 years o
age he left his father’s home, taking with him Ins
brother, aged 10. For some time he earned a
living for both At 17 he was elected one ot the
committee of thrr 'old Launceston Working Mmi 3
Club, co-operating with men old enough to be ins
grandfathers. Before he was 21 he married an
came to Victoria. Shortly afterwards he became
a member of the National Reform
those days, led by Graham Berry and 1
Lalor. This league had for its object the reto
of the Leg. Council, and Trenwith was appointed
lecturer and organiser. Soon afterwards he \


selected to organise the workers in the boot
trade. His success frightened the employers,
who determined to suppress him. They were
no more successful than Syme has been. In
one of the posters issued by Teenwith he
gave a list of the men who were working for
less than union rates, and referred to them as
“ scabs,” defining a “ scab ” as a man who was to
his trade what a traitor was to his country. The
employers put up some of the men to go for Tren-
WiTH for criminal libel, and the case was for two
years before the courts. At last a jury was found
to convict. The verdict, however, was upset on a
number of legal points. In time Teenwith be-
came president of the Trades Hall Council and in
1889 he entered Parliament, where he was regarded
as a sort of monster. Gradually he lived this
down, and when he went into the Senate fight
carried with him the assistance and good wishes
of every member of the House. In Parliament he
had only just overcome the prejudices against him
when he was blinded by cataract. He now has
the use of one eye only. It was while suffering
this affliction that he put up such a sterling fight
for Protection during the tariff debate. Much
discussion and many conferences have made
Teenwith remarkably ready. As a debater
he is without equal in Victorian politics. His
great strength, however, is also his weakness.
The consciousness of his powers makes him disin-
clined to prepare himself for great efforts. He is
inclined to rely upon his impromptu thoughts.
He is somewhat given to mental laziness. It is
sometimes said that Teenwith has modified his
views, but the fact is that the other fellow has
modified his views so that they now accord more
closely with Trenwith’s.

No Truck with the Labor Party.
The Piebald-Australian Foreign-trader who has
been talking about “great successes” which he
knows he hasn’t achieved, is already suggesting
that the Labor party is not bound to the Minis-
terialists. And that is true enough. All that
binds them is that they think the same way upon
many things. But the Foreign-trader, knowing
his George Reid, knows that if all that
stands between Reid and a union with the
Laborites is a difference of opinion, it is 400
small an obstacle to trouble about. Geoege
Reid’s elasticity isn’t everything, though ;
apart from the fact that Reidites opposed the
Labor party in every possible constituency, and
that that isn’t the sort of thing to suggest friend-
ship, there are other things in the way. George
Reid is not the whole party. Men like Bruce
Smith, Simon Feaser, Gould, J. T. Walker,
William Knox (who left the Ministerial party
because it combined with the Labor party), Skene
(ditto), Glynn, and Braddon have to be reckoned
with. The Tory members of Reid’s party when
he.was State Premier didn’t worry over his Labor
alliance; they knew that mostly he was only pre-
tending to satisfy the Laborites, and, anyhow, they
had the Legislative Council to throw out anything
that might accidentally get through the other
House. Now Reid can’t play the pretender any
more. He would have to do things for the
future; and that would not suit the Tory section
of his party. And the section that isn’t quite so
Tory is pledged to alter the tariff, while the Labor
party is tired of the tariff, and wants to get on
with social legislation. Union between these two
is, therefore, just as impracticable. Reid, there-
fore, has no present hope. He said he wouldn’t
have any truck with the Labor party ; he said he
couldn’t conceal his loathing and contempt for it,
and not for a thousand Premierships would he
touch the Unclean Thing; and Australia took
him at his word and gave him followers that
make any trucking very difficult.

The Consumptive’s Christmas.
As Australia plunges into the hot and enervating
summer, all who can afford to do so seek in Tas-
mania, on the tablelands, or at seaside resorts
some relief from the sun. Especially do the
politicians shut up their talking shops and go in
search of coolness, and it is quite safe to say that
not one prominent Parliament man, in his retreat
in Tasmania or on the mountains, gives a thought
to that particularly helpless section of the people —
the consumptive poor—whom he has left behind
to gallop to death in the stifling heat; for, if there
were thought, there surely would be action. The
consumptive poor are a large class in Australia,
for the continent has long been a dumping ground
for the phthisical of other nations, and the moist
coast climate favors the growth of the disease
under conditions of poor food and overcrowding.
We now not only import consumptives, but pro-
duce them locally; and whilst most other
civilised nations are making great efforts
to cope with the White Plague, Australia
does practically nothing. In N.S.W. for
example, there has been founded one small sana-
torium at Wentworth Falls, which does excellent
work for half-a-score or so patients, and one small
hospital at Thirlmere, which, whilst not so good,
is still fairly good. Together they can touch but
the narrowest fringe of the evil. The great mass
of consumptives totter on to death in their own
homes, or are herded together at the Liverpool
Tubercle Farm, in an old mill building so thickly
crowded with patients that the air in the wards is
a high-grade culture of tubercle bacillus. A
lethal chamber would be kinder and not less sure
than this “hospital.” N.S.W. conditions in
regard to the consumptive are, by the existence of
this place, made a little more openly scandalous
than in the other States, but throughout Australia
the problem of phthisis is just as effectively
neglected—perhaps a little more dangerously, for
if the consumptive is to be allowed to die, it is
better, in the public interest, that he should die
quickly on a tubercle farm than slowly in the slums,
spreading his disease as he dies. But with the
coming of the summer, which makes things so much
harder for the consumptive, at a time when, as a
Christian people, we are supposed to be peculiarly
softened in heart and eminently inclined to show
good-will to our fellow man, cannot there be found
in any Australian State one Cabinet Minister bold
enough to insist on immediate action in this
matter? Something could be done in a week or
two without great expense. The military might
be requisitioned for tents to accommodate 100 or
200 patients on some high upland ; or the railway
department for worn out railway carriages which
could be converted into little one-patient rooms.
There is no time, and no absolute need, for an
extensive hospital ; a tent settlement would serve
purposes of cure better than the finest stone or
brick building ; and tents could be set up, drains
cut, and a commissariat arranged in a week. No
better work could keep a public man from his
holiday.

The Referendum.
Now that it is certain that N.S.W. has voted by a
huge majority for cutting down the Legislative
Assembly to 90 members, those politicians who
faked the Referendum should sit down and view
themselves very seriously. On the evidence it is
more than probable that the electors would gladly
have voted for a reduction to 80 members, or even
to 70, if they had been allowed to express them-
selves ; and the politicians who deliberately stifled
the voice of the people by refusing them the chance
to vote for any smaller number than 90 may
consider that the people have a very solid
grievance against them—a grievance about which
the people will relieve their feelings at next
election. The N.S.W. Labor party was against any
reduction, and it was largely responsible for
limiting the Referendum to prevent the people
giving full expression to their opinions on the
subject ; which, considering that the Referendum
is one of the sacred principles of the democratic
creed, was an especially disastrous move on its
part. The result goes to show how very far the
N.S.W. Labor party, in its present sleepy and
degenerate condition, has got out of touch with
the people, and should be a warning to it to get
back with all speed to the purer democracy of its
early days. Probably, if the people hadn’t been
muzzled—if they had been allowed to vote freely
on the question of the reduction of the over-
grown State Legislature—they would have
voted for a further cutting down by the
abolition of the useless Legislative Council, and
the fact that they were muzzled on this question
is another serious grievance. So far as the pre-
sent Referendum has gone, it tends to show that
the policy of trusting the people is a safe policy.
Probably if a vote were taken on the question of
the abolition of borrowing, it would prove that
therein also the people are a great deal wiser and
more courageous than their rulers. At all events,
the experiment is worth trying. If the Labor
party will not become a definite anti-borrowing
institution, it should at least get back to its old
habit of trusting the people, and get out of its
new habit of being a mere ditto to the disastrous
John See, and should insist on a popular vote upon
the question. That Referendum would be one of
the most interesting episodes of N.S.W. history.
It would dish all the bogus “reformers”
and the vague, non-committal economists. The
Tory daily papers which live on shadowy
diatribes about “ retrenchment” would be brought
up with a jolt against a hard, definite fact. A
clear-cut issue would be brought into State poli-
tics, and it would make John Stinson sit up and
Joey Carruthers fall down insensible. N.S.W.
Labor party, which believes, or used to believe,
in trusting the people, can hardly refuse, if there
remains with it one shred of its ancient principles,
to take up this question.

The Jabberwock Goes into Figures.
The Sydney D.T. is still demonstrating at great
length that it and George Reid and Dry-Doggism
won a Great Moral Victory on a recent Wednes-
day. “ The success of the Labor party, ” it says,
“is undeniable; but it is not broad-based success.”
The Barrier and the Darling together have not as
many electors as any one of several metropolitan
districts won by Foreign-traders ; therefore, says
the D.T., as those two districts elected Laborites,
the Laborites have one seat more and the Foreign-
traders one seat less than they are morally entitled
to. On the same principle consider this :
Foreign-trade. Labor.
Seats. Voters. Seats. Voters.
Riverina .. .. 18,131 Newcastle .. 35,633
Macquarie .. 20,459 W. Sydney .. 25,789
Werrima .. .. 21,077
Totals 59,667 61,422
Here two Labor men represent more than three
Foreign-traders; and clearly, according to the
D.T.’s own rule, there is a Foreign-trader too
many, and, therefore, the Foreign-trade success,
even in N.S.W., wasn’t “a broad-based success”
either. The D.T. oughtn’t to make rules which
are liable to fly up and hit it like that. But in
the matter of faking figures the D.T., acting in
the Jabberwock interest, can do a lot better than
even that. With regard to the Senate election it
prints this pretty little table, so beautiful in its
simplicity that it seems a pity to have to brand it
as a hollow fraud and a painted lie: —
Opposition. Ministerial. Labor.
Votes polled.. 1,066,931 778,433 605,501
Seats won 5 4 10
So “Labor polled only a little more than half
of the number of votes polled by the Opposition
and won just double the number of seats.”
This is monstrous, of course. It not only
shows that the Labor party’s isn’t “a broad-
based success,” but it also shows the injustice of
the whole Federal compact, since ‘ ‘ equal repre-
sentation in the Senate has turned things upside
down.” And the Jabberwock points to these
figures and says that figures can’t lie. “You
asked for figures and not fancies,” it says ; “ there
they are.” All that is wrong with them is that
they are stuffed Jabberwock figures, and not the
real thing. There were three Foreign-trade can-
didates in N.S.W. ; and their scores are all
counted. There was only one Labor candidate ;
and his score is counted alone. By this pretty
little trick the Opposition figures, compared with
Labor’s, are multiplied by three. If the
Laborites had put up two more unsuccessful
candidates in N.S.W. they might have added
200,000 votes to their record. On top of that
interesting little exercise in Jabberwock arith-
metic the D.T. reckons in as Oppositionists
the Queensland National Ass candidates who
took great pains to assure that State that,
though the} 7 cordially approved of George Reid’s
colored-labor programme, they were dead against
the alteration of the tariff to which Reid is
pledged so far as he is pledged to anything. Also
the votes cast for the “anti-socialist four” in
Victoria are likewise credited to the Foreign-calico
cause, even though the candidates were in some
cases not Freetraders, and in all cases were dead
against Reid’s policy of re-opening the tariff
question. From bottom to top, then, the D.T.’s
figures are stuffed, and are no more like the real
figures than the D.T. is like an honest, truthful
paper.

A Bishop.
This incredible slab of nonsense is taken from a
sermon recently preached by Bishop Langley at
Bendigo :
Socialism in some of its manifestations was clearly and
distinctly marked out as one of the means by which men
were saying, “ I am Christ.” It was a method that was
being adopted by many men, and accepted very largely,
by which they desired to cause a social revolution
throughout the world, in order that some of the anoma-
lies of life that God had allowed could be remedied, and
that all men might be made prosperous, if not by Act of
Parliament, by a social revolution, such as he had referred
to. The State was to bring about this prosperity. Men
were to lose their individuality in the State, which was
to step in and regulate everything, even to the minutest
detail. . . An attempt was being made to graft socialism
upon Christianity, or Christianity upon socialism, but the
two were distinctly antagonistic in character. They
could never be amalgamated, as they were distinctly
opposite in their purposes. Christian socialism could
never come to anything, because when a man became
a Christian he ceased to be a socialist, and when
he became a socialist he ceased to be a Christian. It was
necessary, in order to understand what socialism aimed
at, to go to those who spoke authoritatively on behalf of
socialism. It was regarded by socialists as robbery to
hold property; it was regarded as absolutely wrong and
dishonest for any man to possess anything. In fact, the
condition of Australia, when the aborigines held posses-
sion, was exactly the true ideal of socialism. It was a
perfect socialism, except that the aborigines had kings to
rule over them. Socialism meant the destruction of the
family life, as its aim was to abolish family life, and
introduce a state of things by which marriage should be
a civil contract, binding just as long as the parties agreed
to live together.
How such an incredibly ignorant person came to
be a bishop may be fairly regarded as one of the
world’s mysteries. Still, the most ignorant
bishop is expected to tell the truth, and not
merely be the mouthpiece through which are
circulated the silly lies that his wealthy
parishioner tells him when he invites the gaitered
one to dinner. A course of reading in the way of
political economy might do Bishop Langley a
considerable amount of good, and again it might
not. His idea that a Socialist doesn’t want to
own any property—that he aims at getting rid of
all his assets, and reducing the whole world to a
state in which nobody, not even a Socialist himself,
will possess anything— is such a wild conception
as to suggest that Langley is beyond the benefits
of literature. And his idea that the old Australian
aborigine didn’t own anything, and was therefore
a real Socialist, shows an amazing lack of informa-
tion. The aboriginal owned all the property he
could get —even liis wife was property. He repre-
sented the doctrine of the sacredness of property
to an extent that even the bishop hasn’t reached.
Socialism has crept into the bishop’s existence
and so disrupted the doctrine of property that he
can’t even flog hia “missus.” She doesn’t
belong to him now.
G. H. Reid and the Labor Party.
G. H. Reid has gone on a trip to Westralia for
the purpose, it is rumored, of interviewing the
newly-returned Labor members of that State,
some of whom are reported to have Freetradeish
sympathies. Mr. Reid sees now in a coalition
with the Labor party his best hope of office, and
is preparing to declare himself the only genuine
White Australian Radical friend of the poor man
on the continent. A month ago, or less, he was
saying at Paddington
I am prepared for anything which Mr. Lyne may do,
but I was not prepared for the apostacy and treachery to
their vital principle, shown by a majority of the members
of the Labor party of Australia. I wish to say this to the
Labor members of the late Parliament, and the Labor
members of the next Parliament, that if I could vvin a
thousand offices by abandoning my feeling of bitter hos-
tility and contempt for their unfaithfulness to their
principles, I would not do it.
But all that is over now, and possibly Mr. Reid,
by a Pickwickian process of reasoning, convinces
himself that, whilst he was pledged not to sink his
bitter hostility and contempt to the Labor party
to win a thousand offices, that does not bind him
not to sink those feelings in the pursuit of one
office. More probably Mr. Reid does not reason
about the matter at al\ and simply applies his
familiar top-dog rule to the situation. He ex-
pressed loathing and contempt for the Labor
party before the elections ; that was to try to get to
office. Now, after the elections, he is just as ready
to express affection and reverence for that party ;
again the end, which is office, justifies the means.
And the Labor party is not likely to let any
squeamishness as to Reid’s personal insults, or
a contempt for his time-serving and unprincipled
political methods, stand in the way of using
him if that seems expedient. But what
that party has to keep in mind is that the
Reid programme at the general elections was
definitely against the White Australia policy and
the Immigration Restriction law. It could hardly,
therefore, with any possibility of giving to the
transaction the appearance of an honor-
able compact, agree to go now with Reid,
either on its policy—in opposition to
which he fought the elections—or on his own,
which is contrary to the most important plank of
its platform. An honorable arrangement with
the Deakin party is possible ; it would only be a
continuance and a development of relations which
were productive of good in the last Parliament.
Or if, by some strange development, George
Reid should come to friendly terms with Premier
Deakin and form with him a coalition Govern-
ment, the position would not be dismaying to the
Radical cause, for that would prepare the way for
a genuinely democratic Kingston-Watson ad-
ministration. Absolutely the only dangerous
development of an involved political situation
would be the success of Reid’s efforts to “ make
it up with ” the Labox party—the party which a
month ago he could not mention without a shudder
of loathing.
There is a side issue in John Haynes’ humorous
N.S.W. secession movement which would be
worth considering if the secession movement had
anything serious about it. Supposing N.S.W.
did claim a right to leave the Commonwealth, and
supposing the claim were allowed (both of which
ideas are too absurd to be worth considering), it
would be a sadly shorn and diminished N.S.W.
tha,t climbed out. If N.S.W. could, by any pos-
sibility, prove a right to leave the Union, then it
must prove also the right of any part of the State
to leave N.S.W. At present Australia is one
political whole by the will of the majority
of the Australian people. If it can be hqld
that any fragment of Australia has a right
to break off because there is a majority for seces-
sion in that district, it can’t be held that the
seceding, district has a right to drag with it by
compulsion districts that don’t want to secede.
The Barrier, which votes Labor, and a great part
of the Riverina which votes Protection, and
probably part of the North would have to be left
behind when N.S.W. seceded; just as Virginia,
when it quitted the American Union, had to
leave West Virginia behind it. This is an aspect
of the case which doesn’t appear to strike John
Haynes and co. Their idea, apparently, is that
the rest of Australia has no right to dictate to the
N.S.W. minority, but that Sydney has every
right to dictate to the Broken Hill and Riverina
and Monaro minority.
