A red-hot Tory gospeller named Rivett asked
a Wodonga Congregational audience, recently, if
Christ would be in favor of a White Australia.
Since Stead, some years ago, set the fashion of
asking what Christ would do under given circum-
stances, the average sky-pilot has mixed Christ’s
name so indiscriminately with politics that a
number of electors begin to think He is a pro-
minent politician like Alf. Deakin. Rivett,
of course, said that if Christ came to Australia
He would be shut out under our present nefarious
laws, which seems to The Bulletin rather an
exaggeration. If Christ went to England nowa-
days He would be told He was an alien and could
only be regarded as a citizen after five years’ resi-
dence, and after paying heavy fees and promis \g
to live permanently in British territory. If He
went to India He would be booted round by every
British jack-in-office and told that He Was quite
unfit to have any share in the political manage-
ment of the country, that being the exclusive
privilege of the British invader. Butiif He came
to Australia the Deakin Government would let
him in cheerfully ; He would only have to pass a
test in some European language and even the
most blasphemous parson would hardly dare to
say that the Omniscient couldn’t do that. Of
course the Argus would denounce Him as a
socialist and Derham would speak severely about
Him, but that is all.
♦
In combating, and for the time being defeating,
the Victorian Government’s education retrench-
ment proposals, Theodore Fink made the best
speech of his life. Starting with the declaration
that Robert Reid was as wrong-headed as usual
when, as Education Minister, he wanted to run
the department on commercial principles (Reip,
like his firm, was a whale on commercial
principles), Fink laid down the axiom that a
nation’s greatest asset was its body of school-
teachers. He showed that Victoria, in giving
free education between the ages of six and 14, was
already more niggardly than many other nations,
and while in England education cost £3 per head,
in Melbourne it was done for £2. Fink was in
dead earnest for once, especially when he threw
the gibe at Kyabram that the proposed economy
meant ‘ ‘ the retrenchment of the intellectual facul-
ties—the starving of the greatest of all our
resources.” “ The saving in school buildings now
means a bigger expenditure on reformatories and
gaols later on,” said Fink, and he concluded a
straight, honest, vigorous denunciation of “that
bastard cry for economy started by the news-
papers of Collins-street,” by a sneer at the spurious
patriotism that put up pillars of gilt while the
State’s children had to go outside its borders to
get a thorough education. The immediate effect
of this declaration w,s that several erstwhile
whole-souled Kyabramites recanted, “I never
thought,” said Field, of Maryborough, “that
reform meant interfering with the education of
the children.” Premier Irvine, realising that his
majority was vanishing, caved in and postponed
his threatened retrenchment—the charging of fees
outside the years 6 to 13—until the country had
spoken on the question. Judging by the awful
odor now proceeding from the dead ’Pootra, the
country may be relied on to speak effectively.

That foul-minded Tory lie about the Petriana
was put into abusive verse by Melbourne Paunch.
Even while the rescued and dried and carefully
fed mariners were on their way home on boai-d a
big, safe mailboat, Paunch, the liar, dropped into
rhymed screams about how they were cruelly and
wilfully drowned by the Australian Government.
A sample:—
The South Australian Laborites couldn’t win the
Leg. Council seat which Sailor Guthrie got out
of in order to race for the Senate ; the vacancy
went to President Moulded of the “ National "
Ass—the Toriest “National” Ass of them all.
Yet in the Senate race the Asses were nowhere.
All the difference lies in the franchise. Compara-
tively speaking, the S.A. Council franchise is
liberal ; but it is streets behind the Federal
one. Another straight tip to Laborites and,
Liberals to pass on as much power as they
possibly can from the State to the Federal’
authority.
A Celebration.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Otago (M.L.) ex-Contingentera’ Club held its first
annual re-union and dinner at Dunedin last month.
Tickets os. Stokes’ mandolin band was in attendance.
This song wasn’t sung :
The Boer we killed is very, very dead ;
The grass is tall and rank above his head.
So we’ll drink unto his bones,
Left behind amid the stones,
And we’ll pass the liquor gaily—the liquor rich
and red.
We will bang the wild decanter on his tomb,
His ghost will take it for the Crack of Doom.
He is but a framework now,
And the coolie and the Chow
Have come to take his country while he slumbers
in the gloom.
And we’ll light the fragrant threepenny cigar
(He has gone where things like that no longer are);
O, we sent back his beard
As a trophy wild and weird
(At least the papers said so) to our native land afar.
If you go and hunt with care amid the loam
You may chance to find the ashes of his home ;
It made a cheerful blaze,
In those good old fighting days,
’Twas a pleasant sort of circus and a giddy hippo-
drome.
His land, perhaps, is something of a wieck,
It’s owned by Aaron and Melchisedeok ;
And they state that you and I
Need not trouble to apply
For the yellow man is coming and such trash as
we may trek.
Still we whirl the bottle gaily in the air,
And the beer of celebration froth with care,
And we blow the glad cigar.
And we twang the mad guitar,
For we saved the Rand for Moses, and we aren't
wanted there 1
J.E.
In vain they stretch beseeching- hands,
“ What is your Law to Life ?” they cry.
Australia answers : “ Life is naught,
We care not if ye live or die,
For this, thank ye, your colored skins,
We answer if ye ask us why.”
The Law : “ Yes, let them die.”
Premier Deakin : “Or else apply.”
Labor Party : “A-a-men.”
These aliens at the gates of death
Have seen us smirch the British name ;
They curse us till their dying breath,
We put this Christian land to shame.
Through all the world the story flies,
Our “ White Australia’s” evil fame.
Our Law : “ They cannot land.”
Federal Parliament : “ Upon our strand.”
Labor Party : “ A-a-men.”
This last verse isn’t by Paunch :—
The black and unimperilled crew
Were free to land—let Truth explain—
Protected by their bosses, who
Were under legal contract to
Deport their niggers back again.
A crafty statement of the case
The Freetrade journals roared in vain
Premier Deakin : That story of the crew’s distress—
The Public: Invented by the Tory press—
Labor Bbrty : Was just a lie, and nothing less.
All Together: A-d-men.

-
It is a weakness of poor humanity to rush
in crowds to be there when the early doors open,
so to speak, and gape upon the mimic loves and
sham-lights of stage princes and other titled per-
sons, who are mostly strong and beautiful, and
mentally gifted* or giftedly wicked. “ Monsieur
Beaucaire ” was staged for the first time in Sydney
last week, and filled Her Majesty’s Theatre
up to the police regulation limit. It is a romantic
story of a French prince, one of the Orleans
line, running loose in high society in Bath,
an English town where people of quality
went to drink liver water. This prince is
introduced in the play at the age of 33
years, and spilin’ for a fight with some of the
best blood and keenest blades in England. As a
matter of fact, hardly any of the Orleans line
could fight worth a frog’s leg (there were three
families in the Orleans business, and the best man
among them was a woman), but that’s a trifle to a
romance-maker. Prince Louis Philippe, alias
Monsieur Beaucaire, is whimsical, brilliant, a
gamester, an expert duellist, but, above all, the
fervent lover of Lady Mary Carlyle, the toast of
Bath. There is considerable grovel in the drama to
“men of parts,” “ ladiesof quality,” and to dukes,
but chiefly because it is a love story, told with a
sparkle of real passion, it justifies its hour on the
stage and the attention with which it holds its
audience. For the rest it is a well-painted picture,
out of the past of a hollow society, smooth-tongued,
insincere, affected, and very graceful in its
management of the artifices that conceal
artificiality. The prince with the butterfly surface
attributes has the heart of a volcanic fire when he
confesses his love. But on the stage they keep their
state and there is scarce any love-making Persons
of lesser quality get “ engaged,” and purr in each
other’s faces, but these two will “ contract an
alliance.” The stupidity of all high and mighty
Bathers in not penetrating the disguise of the
Hyperion is no compliment to their intellects,
but no one will yap at such trifles save for yap-
ping’s sake. The part of Lady Mary Carlyle
gives Miss Maud Jeffries little show but to look
dazzling. Julius Knight accomplishes Monsieur
Beaucaire, the paragon prince, as naturally as
such an ideal could be fulfilled. Messrs. Warbur-
ton, Wontner, Turner, and Stirling are good fits
in their respective roles of Lord Winterset, Major
Molyneux, Beau Nash, and Squire liakel. After
Miss Jeffries, there is no woman’s part worth
mentioning.
** * *
The Xmas Pantomime at Sydney Royal
this year is, in some respects, of higher quality
than usual. The book—by a well-known Mel-
bourne journalist, Mr. “Monty” Grover—is
smart, the local gags are humorous, and one or
two patter songs are decidedly clever. Moreover,
there is a fresh Australian note in it. The average
Pantomime book, written by some superannuated
Poverty-Pointer, is full of fool-politics ; this year
at the Royal the allusions are mostly national
and White Australian—never infected with dull
Tory stupidity. Where “ The Sleeping Beauty ”
is most open to criticism is in the color arrange-
ments of its scenes, which are often garish, and in
one instance —the Hall of Pillars—the tones
audibly swear at one another. Some of the effects
are, however, mightily ingenious, the Bush Fire,
the Snow Storm and the opening Cloudland scene
for examples, and there is no lack of variety;
quite a score of different stage pictures are shown
in the course of three acts.
* * * *
To tell a thin, but bright and coherent
story, the management has gathered: together
a great crowd of performers, among whom
the youngsters shine out most conspicuously.
The Little D’Artos (M‘Leans) dance and
fence right up to the glowing promise of
their bright red hair ; Eileen Boyd (whose voice
develops into a strong, clear alto) as a singer, and
little Cora Hamilton as a dancer, equal in grace
and charm any of the adult performers. Some of
the adults are allowed less scope than they
deserve; Fred Leslie, for example, is an excep-
tionally clever dancer, but gets no opportunity of
fanning the air with his legs. Miss May Beatty,
“principal boy,” might also be given a little
more work, with advantage to the piece;
as it is, she makes a vivacious feature,
and is well mated with Miss Olive Lenton, who
as “ principal girl ” acts and sings and dances
brightly. With more experience Miss Lenton
will develop into a fine soubrette. Another
attractive circumstance is the beauteous Miss
Elsie Moore in a glad smile and pink fleshings,
as a highly ornamental Fairy Boy. Opening
night, “Beauty and the Beast” did not end
until close on midnight, and some severe pruning
will be needed to bring it within time, and also to
allow a little more scope for the principals.
There’s a lot of unused talent among the principals.
And the young lady who plays the silent part of
St. George of England and names herself on the
programme “Miss Bubba Hammon’' —has she not
a powerful gift of humor which might be given a
chance to display itself? Only a lady with a
powerful gift of some kind could call herself
Bubba, and Hammon.
** * *
The latest development of melodrama
demands that the hero should be falsely accused
of several distinct crimes, should emerge from one
trouble only to drop into another, be pursued
through four acts and several gaols, and be
informed offhandedly in the last scene of the last
act that his character has been cleared, but
not necessarily offered any cash compensation.
The old-fashioned notion of falsely accusing him
of one murder only, and rescuing him at the foot
of the gallows by a pardon, granted by the
nearest policeman on the hurried testimony
of the comic relief man who keeps the
pie stall, is out of date. There must be
crimes, accusations, releases, and vindications in
wholesale quantities. “The Great World of
London ” supplies this want thoroughly, and
carries an excited audience breathlessly through a
hundred improbabilities to a conclusion wherein
virtue is triumphant and villainy is left with no
resource but to go into politics on the religious
ticket. Of all the scenes, that of the murder on
the express train is the most sensational, and the 1
stage mechanician deserved the applause he got
for its air of reality. As for the human
personages in the play, they are of less
importance than the train, but Miss Ada
Guildford and Robert Inman struggle bravely to
give an air of real life to the procession of
improbabilities, and Lawrence Dunbar makes a
sufficiently blood-curdling villain to satisfy the
most exacting galleryite. “ The Great World of
London ” was more than well received on Satur-
day. and will be continued until further notice.
* * * *
By way of a side-issue, the “Great
World ” is very rough on the alleged prosperity
of England under Freetrade. After enumerating
a long array of characters, who seem to have
hardly a copper among them, it lumps the rest
thus :
Salvation Army Officers, Discharged Prisoners, Male
and Female Tramps, Outcasts, Vagrants, ex-Conviets,
Waifs and Strays, Station Loafers, Newsboys, and
numerous other characters met with in the Great World
of London.
Judging by that list the drama seems to struggle
all through London and hardly meet one person
with the price of a bed about him.

Albert Gran’s Conservatorium demon-
strations at Sydney Palace last week were chiefly
remarkable for the good work of two or three
girl-students—that is to say, if Gran’s own efforts
are left out of the count. As a rule, though, the
performers had more brains than voice ; Miss Ger-
trude Lyndon’s, nevertheless, held out almost to
the end of a specially striking impersonation of
Clarice, in “Comedy and Tragedy,” but then
frayed a little at the edges. In “ Pygmalion and
Galatea ” Gran’s own Pygmalion stood out con-
spicuously.
** * *
The American Comedy Co. which Maori-
land manager George Stephenson has brought to
Sydney Palace, is good enough to have deserved a
great deal more preliminary dust. It gives a
clean and clever show, which doesn’t drag for as
much as a moment ; smart dialogue (though not
much of it), clever dancing (very clever, some of
it), and singing at least equal to the ordinary
comedy-company level, while the songs themselves
without the local “tacks” are a good deal above it.
It is all bright—and most of it new, even to the
overture and the music that you hear the end of
as you come back from seeing whether it’s rain-
ing outside or not. Of story you don’t expect
much, and you don’t get it. The piece
is called “Mama’s New Husband”; and when
it is explained that Mommer is the widow
of a very rich plumber ; that she, not to cast
any reflections oh her own age, hasn’t mentioned
the existence of-a 17-year-old daughter, and that
Popper is a gay old boy who has been flirting
with the girl without knowing she is his daughter-
in-law, the story is about exhausted. Chas. J.
Stine leads the co. as the New Husband—a large,
merry comedian ; supporting him are Miss Olive
Evans, the daughter, a soubrette of the utmost
vivacity, to whom falls the largest share of the
acting; and Miss Marshall, the awesome
Mommer, who reaches her climax when, unable to
any longer hide her daughter, she hysterically
admits to her gay husband an “indiscretion,”
sobs “ I have a baby,” and produces the deceased
plumber’s 17-year-old infant. So far as the piece
has any serious intention, it is a whimsical satire
on plays about “the woman with the past.”
Besides these three principal comedians, every-
body else in the cast does one vaudeville turn.
Among the best are the Bicknells, “ Dresden-
China dancers,” whose performance is very dainty
indeed. For another distinguished item the
chorus is responsible—a cinematograph ballet.
The house is darkened ; and on the stage
you have the flicker, the jerks, the darts of light
of the machine; but the “picture” is supplied by
the dancers themselves. Saturday’s audience was
an exceedingly well-pleased audience altogether.
** * *
The Tivoli might have relied upon the
Cirnocs as its chief holiday attraction —its Christ-
mas goose and plum-pudding, in a manner of
speaking—but out of the wealth of the stores
which manager Rickards has gathered in Europe
patrons were promised a song cr two from Mrs.
Sims Reeves, the lady who has taken over the
business of the still lamented English tenor. But
Mrs. Reeves was ill; and the man that went
to hear Maud invited into the garden had
to fill up with a demonstration of how to
get out of handcuffs or prison supposing
Maud’s father should mistake him for a burglar.
The performance doesn’t seem less mystifying on
the second time of seeing. To the previous list of
“turns” has been added one by James Harvey,
who is giving his imitations of John Sheridan
and other royal persons ; while M’Ginty has
opened up a new box of jokes for the special
benefit of the young man who has come down
from the back to get the grass-seeds out of
his constitution. And on top of all these attrac-
tions Mrs. Reeves is liable to appear at any
moment.
** * *
At the end of a warm Boxing Day Sydney
still felt enough curiosity about woman’s sinful-
ness to fill the Criterion with a pack that bulged
out of every door and craned its neck from every
passage. Whether it knew any more after it had
craned its neck is another matter. The young
woman in the case sinned, so far as could be made
out, first a little for love of the man, and then a
lot more for love of the che-ild ; and both that
man and that che-ild have been heard of before.
And of course, as hitherto, the lady got the sym-
pathy of the house. She ate, and wiped her
mouth, and said she had done no wickedness,
just as she did in the days of Solomon,
who had quite as large opportunities of
knowing the sex as even a modern
player or play-writer has. Thus she gets even
with Adam. Ever since the unfortunate incident
in the orchard, the lady has thrown the core of
every apple she has eaten at the man; and the
fallery has admired her for her aim. In Charles
lolloway’s production Miss Gresham is the
modern Eve, and C. R. Stanford the Adam. The
former masquerades as Ida Valette, the latter as
Captain Carlton, R.N. The lady’s is a thankless
part—the usual weary, sighing admissions ; the
usual weary search for the child whom the villain
has got rid of with the help of Charles Holloway,
who doesn’t look that sort of person. Stanford
is a quiet, reserved villain as usual. Miss
Gresham hardly gives the impression that
she “cares for neither man nor devil,” as
the authors make her allege. But that is
the authors’ fault perhaps, for they have
made her an illogical creature, anyway.
Holloway’s own performance is very good. He
plays seldom—this is the first time this season
that he has been seen in the lighter vein, and the
holiday-house enjoyed him better than anything
else in the show. The two young people whose
rough course of love winds in and out of the story
are, as usual, represented by Miss Holloway and
Vivian Edwards, who likewise do well, if not
better; and Miss Irby Marshall, J. P. O’Neill,
Hawthorne and others add to the general
pleasure. All through his season Holloway has
mounted his pieces with a good deal of splendor,
and even more money has been spent on “Why
Woman Sins” than on the others.

The name alone of Mel. B. Spurr ought
to be worth not less than £2OO a year to its
owner. For eye arresting affect on a hoarding
it ranks with the Gilead P. Beck of novelist
Besant, or the Philander P. Springbuffer
of a - Victorian journalist. Mel. is the short
for Melancthou in Mr. Spurr’s case, but the
full name does not fit him at all. It conjures up
visions of orthodoxy watching heterodoxy crackle
in the flames. Mr. Mel. B. Spurr, who looks too
good-natured to make anybody crackle, began
his Sydney season at the Queen’s Hall last
Saturday to a crowded audience. The hall
had been transferred into an artistic place
with decorations, and cooled with noiseless electric
fans. Mr. Spurr looks—when he is just himself—
like a self-possessed, keen, professional man. He
is a barrister, and practised for some eight years
before making a profession of what had been
during that time an amusement —society enter-
taining. He lias a whole gallery of different facial
expressions, and canmakehands, feet and shoulders
talk. For two hours on Saturday night he
entertained single-handed, speaking, singing, re-
citing, pianoforte-playing, or mimicking singers,
reciters and speakers. Programme was mostly
humorous, but punctuated lightly with thepathetie
and the histrionic. Mr. Spurr’s audience went
reluctantly into the night, after laughing all over
the octaves. There will be a matinee this (Wednes-
day) afternoon.
** # *
Melbourne’s only panto., the scheme
whereof germinated at the little Gaiety Theatre,
but was transplanted to the Bijou, turns out to be
a lively, well-dressed show with a harlequinade at
the end. “Little Red Riding Hood” introduces
itself as a purely local production in every
particular except the title. The costumes
came from Flinders-street, the author is a
young man who once committed a terrible
Australian melodrama at the neighboring Royal,
the scenery and other marvellous effects
were invented and designed on the premises,
and as no artists were specially engaged in
Europe at enormous expense, the maximum
charge for admission is only 3s. Compared with
two or three bygone pantos, at the same price this
is a comparatively gorgeous affair. At any rate,
the fixings are new and bright, not to say taste-
ful. Also the company in general has a well-
distributed capacity for work. Tom Perman runs
the theatre ; W. Perman manages the stage and
disguises his face in burnt cork ; Miss B. Perman
is the matured child whose grandmother inhabits
a cottage in the bush half way between
Wagga and the new Federal Capital where the
wolves come from ; several younger Permans
appear as boys, beasts and fairies, and the list of
other names on the programme is about a yard
long. They can all dance. Outside of the
successful young ballet, clever legs of both sexes
are numerous, and among the men who paint
their noses red —Harry Shine, Torn Dawson, Box
and Cowan —there seems to be more sense of
humor than one expects to find in professedly
‘ ‘ comic ” scenes. If Melbourne really needs a
pantomime at Xmas, the city may consider its
painful necessities adequately provided for. “ Red
Riding Hood” made a good impression the first
nigidw'ind will improve a lot.
On Saturday next (January 2) Ada Cross-
ley will sing her positively last song to the Mel-
bourne public. Her farewell warble is to happen
at the Exhibition Building, admission one “bob,”
and a shilling extra for “early door.” In con-
sideration of the fact that early door visitors are
expected to arrive some hours in advance of the
evening concert there will be a preliminary organ
recital and a brass band, also refreshments obtain-
able at moderate prices for cash.
* * * *•
Ada Crossley’s songs for her shilling pop
Farewell Concert at Melb. Exhibition Building
are to be chosen by a plebiscite. This is bad
news for poor but frivolous people who are
hoping to hear Ada for the first time on this occa-
sion. At her previous farewell concert, when the
programme was presumably arranged by good old
General Desire, the contralto sang “Nearer, my
God to Thee,” also “O Rest in the Lord.” One
of her encore numbers was the popular “O That
we Two,” which terminates with the word
“God,” and another of the extras was “God be
with You.” General Desire, now that he is too
old to howl on the field of battle, seems to have
become religious.
** * *
The “ Country Girl ” promises to get a
firmer hold of Melbourne than any similar piece
staged at Her Majesty’s since the “Eloradora
times. As a mere musical spectacle constructed
on light opera lines, this is one of the best, and in
its patches and streaks of comedy narrative there
is more than a faint color of truth as well as a
sparkle of humor. Some characters in the
“Country Girl” present a genuine appearance;
moreover, the Williamson people happen to fit
their parts naturally, and all circumstances are
favorable to an effective show. The stolid Ban-
tock, a very unadaptable comedian, is placed to
the greatest possible advantage as a Rajah who
tunefully enunciates every syllable in a clever song.
Miss Ghiloni is equally fortunate in the. cha-
racter of a brown, languorous princess
from Asia, whilst Florence Young imper-
sonates an actress without any trouble to
herself. Rose Musgrove plays a gilt-edged
milliner in a charming way. Geo. Lauri, as a
smart young man from the navy, is an almost
new Lauri .fooling under new conditions.
Reginald Roberts has improved his accent, and
the performance in every other respect seems “ up
to Dick.” This last remark is intended as a
delicate compliment to Dick, whoever he was.
* * #
Bland Holt’s latest achievement at Melb.
Royal is a song and dance drama of British extrac-
tion. The grief and bloodshed probably came
from London, but the scenery, topical allusions,
figures of speech, and democratic trimmings are
as full of Australian sentiment as a local sausage.
Albert Norman professes to be a N.S.W. farmer
of the cockatoo species who migrated to the west
in search of a gold mine. Baker is a boundary-
rider attached to the family ; also Bland Holt
and two volatile ladies from, the Sydney
Tivoli go to the west in order to drive dull
care away. When they get arrested for unlawful
behavior the two tempestuous females prove their
innocence by singing a giddy song in which the
entire police court joins heartily, and then the sad
case of Albert Norman is brought forward. Two
heavy Australian villains have previously inter-
rupted the gaiety of the Commonwealth by mur-
dering Norman’s wife (Harrie Ireland) and her
lunatic brother. The lunatic is an unknown per-
son whose death made no difference, but the
corpse of Miss Ireland gets her surviving relatives
into a lot of trouble. Her little daughter is struck
stone dumb at the sight of it, and her husband is
arrested for the crime. At this point Baker
comes in useful as an interpreter of dumb evi-
dence, and later in the evening he closes the pro-
ceedings by marrying Miss Ross in a bush taber-
nacle. The title of Holt’s amazing drama is “A
Desperate Game.”

The Boxing Night attendance at Mel-
bourne Princess indicated that a revival of “ Mid-
summer Night’s Dream ” was not wanted. It
deserved a full house, but the reward of merit was
not forthcoming. Therefore the beautiful show
will be withdrawn on Friday, and “Twelfth
Night” will come to pass.

Wirth Bros’. Circus came back to the old
spot on Dec. 26th, and camped out in green tents
under a blue and boiling sky, as though that specu-
lative entertainment were a picnic, or an annual
meeting of Latter-day Saints. At times like
Xmas week Wirths’ biggest tent covers a multi-
tude of sinners, not only for the reason that the
Circus is a collection of Unapproachable Novel-
ties (in an advt. way of speaking), but also on
account of its airy environn.ent. The audience
that mopped its brow at the first matinee
had the comfort of thinking it might
have been hotter elsewhere. Anyway, the
moist, inactive public looked several degrees
cooler than the mysterious Howard Bros., and
the flying farewell gymnasts, and Pagel, and the
tigers that walk in heavy winter clothing. Circus
items are mostly feats of honest labor. Miss
Mayazel, the new flexible lady who performs in
mid-air, excites feelings of warm regard, likewise
the dusky hurricane equestrienne, and the French
professor who carries a lion on his shoulders. 1 his
is the same amiable animal that lies down beside
the lamb, and smiles fondly at the hyena, and is
billed as a blessed fulfilment of Scriptural pro-
phecy. The millenium, seemingly, has started to
radiate from W.B.’s performing zoo.

In Adelaide, amusements set in with
great severity on the arrival of Christmas. Fitz-
gerald s cn-cus occupies its usual pitch at the Old
Exhibition grounds, and introduces the usual lady
leggier, besides the usual horses. There are also
some quite unusual dogs. The Tivoli, with sliding
roof, keeps fairly cool these evenings, and is doing
well with a variety show, which, with unnecessary
Jlrench-Americanism, it calls “vaudeville.” At
the Royal, the much-travelled Beatrice Esmond
has made her first appearance as the “dearest”
mother of that Little Lord who rose on stepping-
stones of a grocery to higher things. Collet
JJobson and others support the show. Also the
old and crusted “ Messiah ” filled its accustomed
place in the Town Hall on Christmas night, and
concerts open air and other —are everywhere.

S. Australian Government climbed down
over the increases in the railway freights, and either cut
them severely or abolished them altogether. So far as
the mines are concerned, they will pay 6d. per ton more
lor ore and ore-products, instead of 9d., and for coal 13s.
oa. per ton as hitherto, instead of the 15s. Id. asked for.
One or two of the weaker mines, which are making no
prone, want a pretext for closing down, and Jenkins
Iscariot got the tip that he was going to be the scape-
goat ; so from under promptly.

Sydney D.T. is astonished to find that 1903
iS!? rts I r ? ra Sydney were greatly in excess of those for
1902, and far more than for most years. Australia has
been told so often lately (by the D.T. and others) that its
troubles were all due to the drought, and that when
normal seasons ” returned everything would be all right
again and everybody would be jolly and fat, that it at
length believed the yarn. Now the fact has' to be
admitted that in the aggregate recent years, including
1903, have been above normal, and that the shortage in
the wheat supply has been more than made up bv the
abnormal mineral output and the higher price ruling for
wool. The truth is that the drain for interest is now so
tremendous that Australia is in distress in even a year
that is above the normal in aggregate prosperity.

The Freehold Assets Realisation Co. (Mel-
bourne) was formed to do the work of burying the
lamentable Freehold Investment and Banking Co. with
as much decency and despatch as possible ; but it finds
the business slow. According to a calculation made some
time afterwards, it started with liabilities to depositors
and others (apart from shareholders) of £1,616,012, had
paper assets of £1,289,163, and a paper deficit of £326,849.
The undertaking co. began to buy up the late lamented’s
deposit receipts at a heavy discount, and sell its assets as
it got the chance ; and the progress has been such that,
nowadays, the co. finds that it isn’t rushed with deposit
receipts at the old rate. Cash is therefore accumulating,
and so the directors now propose to distribute some to its
depositors at the rate of Is. in the £ of their receipts.
The proceedings are complicated, but the main features
of the progress which the winding-up has made are shown
hereunder (“B" scrip represents unpaid interest which
is only a contingent liability):—
“ A ” Deposit Paid
Receipts “B ” Scrip “A” Deposits for “A ”

The position, then, appears to be something
like this. While the Freehold Assets Realisation Co. has
reduced the paper value of its assets by £626,321,
it has reduced its liabilities to “A” depositors by
£704,697. and on “B” scrip (contingent liability) by
about £30,000 ; and it has increased its cash,
&c., by over £20,000. At the rate which it has been
getting for its assets during the past three years, the
remaining assets ought to be worth £192,000. And in
addition to that there is £44,092 cash, &c., less £7958
balance on profit and loss account—total £228134.
Putting the shareholders and the holders of “B” scrip
out of the reckoning, this would leave the co. with ft
present actual deficit (on the realisation basis beforemen-
tioned) of £428,840. On that calculation “A" deposits
would be worth about 6s. lid. in the £.

A sensible step is proposed by the Municipal
Association of Victoria. During the past ten years
£10,020 has been paid in premiums by councils to com-
panies for guaranteeing their employees’ honesty ; and,
although by the introduction during that period of a
very thorough system of audit, several clerks’ sins have
found them out, the total claims paid totalled £1621 • so
the companies have pocketed nearly £lOOO per annum.
The association now proposes a municipal joint guarantee
fund. The bogey of “ State Socialism " has been trotted
out, of course, to frighten unthinking councillors with ;
but the size of the co.s* profits is proving too much for
even a full-grown bogey to successfully wrestle with.
• • • •
According to Statistician Coghlan’s estimate,
N.S.W. will, in the year ending March, 1904, reap
25,750,000 bushels of wheat, being an average of 19-2
bushels per acre. The previous largest yield was
just over 16,000,000 bushels, in 1901; and the
average per acre has not been surpassed since
1860. It is reckoned that, of this, 18,500,000 bushels will
be available for export—roughly, about £2,800,000 worth
The size of New South Wales’ public debt may be judged
from the fact that it will take the whole of this surplus to
pay a year’s interest and charges. The largest danger is
that the phenomenal harvest will give an exaggerated
idea of the importance of the agricultural business or
gamble, or whatever it is, just as the phenomenal wool
returns of 15 or 20 years ago sang a siren’s song to the
West (N.S.W.), where practically every settler has since
been ruined or thereabouts. The fact that the 1901 har-
vest was up to that time a record, and that 1902 harvest
was only second to it for magnificence, and in 1903 the
very next year—starving farmers were calling for seed-
wheat and other charitable relief, shows what sort of an
unreliable industry wheat is.

* * • •
The most important feature of the close of
Broken Hill’s year is the tackling of the zinc business by
the B.H. Prop. The Prop., having more resources than
the other mines, has never rushed things ; the smaller
co’s., thrown into desperation by adverse turns in the
metal markets, have pioneered more than their fair pro-
portion of metallurgical and other changes. When the
Prop, takes up a thing, it is therefore pretty safe to con-
clude that it is a demonstrated success. It has in this
way hung off the zinc-extraction business for years, con-
tenting itself with laboratory experiments only. Now it
has definitely adopted a chemical process, and is installing
it on the mine. More than that, with the object of getting
a cheap acid, it is stated to have secured the right to use
the Carmichael-Bradford desulphurising process, and is
erecting a sulphuric acid plant a few miles away from the
mine, down the railway line. Hitherto these desul-
phurising processes have not done the great work that
it was said they were going to do. It was not originally
intended to save the sulphuric acid fumes thrown off •
all that was aimed at was to get rid of them, and so turn
the intractable sulphide ore into a smelting ore. The
development of the acid process for making a zinc pro-
duct suggested, however, the collection of the acid
fumes ; and so the processes (at any rate, the Carmichael
process) jump into prominence again. But, valuable
as zinc (spelter) is, and enormous as are the quantities
known to be lying in the dumps on the mine awaiting
treatment, shareholders need not expect to get verv
much profit out of the treatment—in the early future
anyhow. The trouble is that even the chemical process
adopted by the B.H. Prop, will not produce spelter All
it will yield is a zinc product—something that wiil still
have to go through the painfully-slow and expensive
zinc-smelting or retorting process, for which, so far. the
co - .uL“ a< ! e 110 Preparations in Australia. Zinc is
worth £2l a ton ; the stuff that the co. will produce may
be worth £5 or so. But the zinc dumpa are earning
nothing at present; and even a very small margin of
profit is better than that.
• • • ■
Broken Hill has got more disappointments than
pleasant surprises out of 1903. The year opened with the
lead market improving; from £lO 17s. 6d. it moved
rapidly to £l3 15s. in April, and then as rapidly turned
back, and in the latter part of the year has kept between
£ll and £ll 10s. At £l3 every main-lode mine could
pay ; with present appliances it is doubtful whether any
except the B.H. Prop., South, and Central (Sulphide Cor-
poration) can work legitimately and pay at under £l2
10s. Block 10 will, no doubt, be able to work at a profit
at £ll when it gets its new plant. The future of the lead
market is most uncertain. The normal price is believed
to be somewhere about £l2 10s. or £l3 ; the lower price
lately ruling has been the result of the boom in produc-
tion which followed the boom in prices of 1899-1900;
in the latter year the average was £l7 2s. 6d.
Twelve months ago it was believed that the accumulated
stocks had been about worked off, and hence the rise in
price was expected ; and yet the market toppled again.
Prophesying in the lead market is an unsafe business.
Nevertheless, the New York M. and E. Journal, which
ought to be an authority on the subject, says that
“ while there has been a large increase in the con-
sumption of lead in the U.S. during the last three years,
the production of domestic mines remains at a standstill.
. . . Three years ago production had considerably outrun
the demand then current and the stocks carried by pro-
ducers were very heavy. ... At the present time they
are really low.” That is hopeful, anyhow.
The other day there were big-headed paragraphs
in the papers—•“ Lead Wanted.” The N.S.W. Govern-
ment had, it was breathlessly announced, received from
its Commercial Agent in “ the East ” a cable that he had
just been offered £lO 18s. per ton for 250 tons of lead.
What all the fuss was about heaven only knows. Broken
Hill produces a good deal more than that much lead
every day of its life. Moreover, it has been sending both
lead and silver to “ the East ” for 16 years ; the late W. R.
Wilson first opened up that trade. Finally lead was just
then saleable through London at £ll 4s. Some Jap.
dealer must have been pulling the new-chum Commercial
Agent’s leg.
'** * •
Crowl Creek (N.S.W.), whose reverberatories
are now running steadily, starts the new year with J. O.
Armstrong as general manager. In milling, smelting,
and engineering work the new man has had probably
more experience than any other single officer at Broken
Hill. His last big work was the designing of the Block
10’s new plant, reckoned to be the most complete in
Australia, and probably the most complete anywhere.
• * • *
Golden Link, Kalgoorlie (W. A.), announces a
serious decline in its November return from the Eclipse
lease. From a previous average of 14jdwt. there is a
drop to 7dwt., or 295. per ton, while the tailings yield
4 - 73dwt., or 20s. per ton. This makes the gross gold
contents won 11 or 495. per ton. As the exten-
sion of the cyanide plant was not completed, only 1100
of the 4000 tons of tailings produced were treated. The
accumulated untreated residues now amount to about
20,000 tons. In his latest quarterly report (to Sept 30)
manager Haig stated that ore reserves above 100 ft. are
alone sufficient to keep the 20-head mill going for nearly
two years, and will yield a profit of over £lOO,OOO. This
mill treats about 4000 tons monthly at a stated gross cost
of 20s. to 255. a ton, and on this basis, and even
on the November grade of ore, manager Haig’s
predictions should be fulfilled. The drop in returns
has given holders a serious scare, but, unless
the manager’s estimates of reserves are wrong, the scare
is needless. The co. started in Oct., ’O2, with a clear
£20,000 cash in hand, and has since won 28,8000 z. of gold
with a considerable profit. The gold chutes are short but
wide, and, as development is proceeding, fresh levels will
be opened up long before the 100 ft. is depleted. The mill
is treating 9 tons per stamp daily.
* * * *
The 19 principal mines of the Kalgoorlie gold
belt are now all using Govt, water, the total consump-
tion for Oct. running into 16,623,000ga15., costing £4156.
The largest consumer was Golden Horseshoe, 2,852,000
gals., and the smallest Chaffers, 5000 gals The use of this
water, besides reducing' cost of reduction, will materially
reduce cost of upkeep and repairs, as mineralised salt
water, even when condensed, is very rough on pipes, etc.
* • * *
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), is still
being boomed hard. If the ore really assayed 290 z. for a
foot wide, it would be the richest chute on the Golden
Mile, but it doesn’t, although it has been published that
it does. That the co. has some fairly rich patches at the
150 ft. level in Dwyer’s leases there is no doubt, but a
patch doesn’t constitute a gold mine. Until some
reserves of ore are blocked out the thing must be re-
garded as a gamble. One or two crushings may even-
tuate ; but it will only be a flash in the pan, unless some-
thing really substantial is found meantime.
• • * «
Northern Blocks Syndicate, Kalgoorlie (W.A.),
floated last May, looks like a wild cat. A large number
of boreholes have been put down, and, although some of
them were alleged to have cut pay ore, further bores have
failed to confirm the optimistic reports. Prof. Liveing
recommends the Board to lie low and let its neighbors
do the work, but, as said neighbors, North Western
Associated, have been following a similar policy for years,
and are not likely to alter it, this programme is hardly
likely to prove successful. The late Oapt. Rodda was
always willing to let tributes ; but although several
parties worked the ground, no pay ore was discovered.
Under existing conditions prospects look hopeless.
• * * *
Splendid specimens of telluride from Mulgabbie
have recently been exhibited in Kalgoorlie, and are quite
up to anything found on the Golden Mile. The speci-
mens were gob at the 100 ft. level in Robb and Phillips’
claim, and, at this depth, the lode is stated to be 4ft.
wide. The outlook is so promising that it is quite
possible that, after the holidays, genuine mining will be
started in the district. A large quantity of gold was won
from the alluvial and from rich leaders ; but no capital
has been invested in the district. If capital can be
attracted, of which there is some hope, a new gold field
may be opened up. It is nearly 100 miles north-east of
Kalgoorlie.
* • * •
Kalgurli (W.A.) continues to open, out very
satisfactorily at the two lowest levels in the north drives.
At 1000 ft. the lode runs up to 30ft. wide, and is worth
30dwt., while, at 1050 ft., it averages about loz. for about
a similar width. Manager Moss has gone to consult his
directors as to increasing the treatment plant. The
share market seems, however, to have already dis-
counted the future to some extent. As the lode has
been followed almost to the North Kalgurli boundary
in good ore, it gives a speculative value to the latter.
Unfortunately the North is not flush of cash, and no
underground work of much consequence is being done
Down to 1000 ft. the ground is very broken and the lodes
distorted, and no work has been done in really settled
country. If money were available to properly test it,
the North Kalgurli might become a mine,
* • * *
Burbank’s Birthday Gift, Coolgardie (W.A.)
has been run to a standstill, and it will be an awful con-
tract to put it straight. Almost everywhere there has
been lack of economy ; while, although there wasn’t
stone enough for the original 30-head mill, the plant was
duplicated, and, since the duplication, only 20 head have
been running, and not always regularly. The under-
ground workings are also unsatisfactory. The mine has
now been taken over by Bewick, Moreing and Co., and it
will be interesting to know who will be commissioned bv
them to do the work. If Loreing tackles the contract
he may straighten things out, as he showed his ability to
unravel a tangle at the Sons of Gwalia. Shareholders
Sii J-° ge J any return for a year at least, as it
will take that time to open up fresh reserves.
* * * *
George Ridgway, the new manager of the
Princess Royal, is to consult the Adelaide directors as to
the policy to be pursued. The new man has been
engineer and shift-boss on the Great Boulder. T.
Hewitson is consulting engineer. • At the 500 ft. level the
crosscut must go about 500 ft. before reaching the lode—a
tremendous lot of unnecessary dead-work. It would be
almost cheaper to start a new main shaft, more especially
as a very long drive has to be undertaken before the pay
chute is reached. The question is—Does the probable
life of the mine warrant this ?

Williams Taney, Berringa (Vic.), pays its first
dividend as an Xmas box. The year’s profit was £4082,
£2193 being made in the latter half. The half-year’s
average was sdwt. Igr. per ton. Considering the amount
of dead work necessary through breaks and slides, the
profit on that grade of ore is very satisfactory. After
writing off £2448 from plant, machinery, and develop-
ment, £1035 is carried forward.

Queensland is chased by buyers these times.

The Wllfley Concentrator has now reached a
very exalted position in the Concentration industry. It
had long been recognised that older types of concentrators
were unsatisfactory in many respects, being small in
capacity, intricate in construction, costly in maintenance,
difficult of adjustment and required constant supervision ;
but the “ Wilfley,” while retaining all advantages that
may have been possessed by its predecessors, was superior
to them in the following important respects : It will handle
several times more material than any belt concentrator
made, without exception. It will save a higher percent-
age of the values than any other thble, and will catch any
float gold, amalgam, or quick-silver which may escape
from the amalgamating plates. It will make cleaner and
more valuable concentrates, thus saving useless expense
for transportation of valueless material, and also reducing
the smelter charges. It requires little or no attention
when once adjusted, and no expert is required, either to
adjust or run the machine. It handles the pulp with less
power and less floor space per ton than any other con-
centrator. It has no expensive belts to crack and wear
out and no complicated machinery to keep in repair.
The Sole Agents and Manufacturers for Australia are The
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Limited, South
Melbourne.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

BEAUTY AND THE BEAST.
“The desire to possess Beauty is keenest where refine-
ment is strongest. "—Partially-Forgotten Philosopher.
You may have five inches of smelling
apparatus and yet buy a bad rabbit. A woman,
with a nose like General Booth, was smelling
rabbits at Paddy’s Market on Saturday night.
After much sniffing she selected one, and gave it
to the dealer to wrap up. He took it, stooped to
get a piece of paper from behind the counter, and
deftly exchanged it for a bunny in the first
stages of decomposition. “Blime,” said the
vendor to his partner as the lady went off
satisfied, ‘ ‘ she picked the freshest bunny we ’ad.
Wonder what she’ll say when she unwraps that
chap.”
“N.”: Ex-policeman Crowe lately pla-
carded Melbourne with bills, signed with his
name, and formulating charges against the police.
He declares that £30,000 worth of property, has
recently been stolen in one way or another with-
out recovery. Then he goes on —“Melbourne is
infested with thieves and receivers known to the
police. Melbourne gaol and the detective-office
have been robbed. A detective’s, carriage-rug
was stolen, in the absence of his coachman.
The police are in league with thieves. Police-
men’s houses are furnished with the proceeds of
robberies. Honest persons are imprisoned by
police perjury.” And so on, including a specific
charge against the Chief Commissioner of Police.
This is, of course, more or less nonsense., but un-
doubtedly the under-manned, under-paid police
force is not giving satisfaction just now in the
detection of crime.
* * * *
“ Poneke ” : Wellington (M.L.) Ministers’
Association has been letting itself loose on the
immorality of that windy metropolis. Everybody
except the parson is going headlong to destruc-
tion. Trams, trains and ferryboats crowded
every Sawbath, birth-rate falling away, theatres
full and churches empty —in fact, things are
awful! At the same time the place is progressing
marvellously. As one Rev. Glasson thinks
“ adversity is necessary to persuade the people to
piety,” it is suggested that his screw be cut down
by, say, one-half, just to test his scheme. The
moral of it seems to be that a country gets on
best in its intervals of impiety.

A Sydney licensing inspector, who knew
that the court did not like to issue a license to a
single man, lately reported to the bench that he
thought the rule might be broken in this case, “ as
the proposed transferee is shortly, to be married,
and meantime is carrying on with his niece.”
Application granted.

“ Vul.” : The cable of the other week, to
the effect that five southern Hungarian women
had confessed to the poisoning of their husbands,
being aided and abetted thereto by the local
coroner, and that over 100 like cases of husband-
shifting had come to light in one district alone, is
enough to make all the world that is a husband
unwell. “ Every woman is at heart a widow,” he
remembers sadly. But where the local coroner
comes in isn’t so clear. The brisk rush of fees
resulting from such a wholesale quantity of late
lamenteds to be sat upon was probably some
inducement. Writer, after this, will always look
on the city coroner with eyes of grave suspicion,
and insist that he ought to be paid by the year
and not by the corpse.
» * » •
Bendigo’s health officer, since the
late rains flushed the drains, reports the city
should be clear of typhoid, but it isn’t. What
Bendigo, the Golden City, is going to do unless
the “late rains” are pretty frequent he doesn’t
say. All the main streets have badly-con-
structed open drains, and in many cases
the channels are formed of round blocks
of wood placed endwise. Through the
middle of the city crawls the noisome
Bendigo Creek, a large, snake-like
open sewer, whose bed is full of the
slush of ages. Melbourne’s champion
odorous suburb, Footscray, is wattle
blossom by comparison. The back
streets have no pretence to drain-
age, and the channels meander
anywhere. . The hospital this
week contains 37 cases of ty-
phoid as against an average of
three for previous years at this
season. The municipal coun-
cil’s time is mostly occupied in
bringing out singers, reciters,
and such freaks. The electric
trams lately started are in the
hands of an English company.
The Library and Mechanics’
Institute are to be closed this
month with an overdraft of
almost £1000; and the hospi-
tal and benevolent asylum are
heavily in debt. The Golden
City has become antiquated,
and smells like it.
“ Australis ” : In the discussion of that
threatened national calamity, a fourpenny rate for
a “pot” of beer, it is interesting to know what can
be given away with threepenn’orth. One leading
Melb. hotel supplies patrons with results of pre-
vious day’s racing, distances and winner’s clock-
ing ; programme of next day’s races, with the tips
of every paper published in the city; weights
carried by tligselected horses; fixtures of Warner’s
team; approaching turf fixtui-es to end of year;
gun fixtures; sports meetings to Jan., ’O4, and
boxing contests. An obliging circular also tells
one that “good and reliable information on all
matters concerning sporting, shipping, mining
shares, general conveyances,” &c., can be had —
presumably from the barmaid. And on top of all
this he gives a counter-lunch.

“ What are you smiling at?” “Jimmy made a joke.” “ How do you know it was a joke?"
“I’d heard it so often before .”
Death of a swagman in a gaol shed at
Molong (N.S.W.), t’other day, adds another to a
long list of country hospital scandals. The man
was picked up starving. There was no benevolent
asylum in the town, and the hospital refused to
admit travellers “unless in urgent need of actual
medical or surgical attendance.” So swaggie
John Harveson went to gaol, was accommodated
in a shed, and died of unsuitable food. The
Government, which provides so much of the
money for country hospitals, should take a much
greater share in the ruling of them and stop such
scandals. No hospital in receipt of a State sub-
sidy should be allowed to shut its doors on sick
poor,

“Brumby ” : Be that par recently about a
runaway motor-car in Oxford-street, Sydney.
Once saw a driverless cab dashing down the
same street. An individual chased it and
climbed up into the boot, only to discover that
the reins, usually fastened to the boot, had some-
how slipped down between the shafts, and were
trailing behind the horse’s feet, so he coolly
clambered across the roof of cab on to the dash-
board, and thence on to the horse’s back, where
he seized the reins and pulled the beast up. Also,
one day saw what looked like an empty cab
passing, with the horse at a trot. A couple of us
stopped the crock, when we discovered the driver,
incapably drdnk, hanging head-downwards from
the other side of the box. He had fallen from his
seat, and had been caught somehow across the
stomach by the reins, which were attached to the
side of his box. Got him down, and were accosted
by his inebriated pal, who woke up and fell out of
the inside of the vehicle, casually inquiring what
all the fuss was about. Old Nick looks after
his drunks without a doubt—excepting, perhaps,
odd times when he’s too busy at elections.
“J.F.D. A Sydney undertaker has
on show in his window three specimens ot the
almanacs which he has been distributing at Nmas.
One represents an old soldier pointing out a
grave to his children, and is labelled “Orphans,
In the second, ‘‘Memories,” a lonely-looking
young woman is sitting near a tombstone, lhe
third, “Christian Martyrs,” is a masterpiece;
it pictures a saintly-looking young woman
praying at full speed while a shabby old lion is
making up his mind to attack. Only one martyr
is seen. The other is probably underground—a
victim to the young woman’s early attempts at
making puff-pastry.
ONLY ONCE.
SHE: “So you don’t like Mr. Lothair.”
HE: “ Candidly , I think , he is an abounding cad.”
S HE: “ Oh, you only say that because you know
he is in love with your wife.”
HE: “Good Lord! ma’am, that’s no discredit.
Why I used to be myself—once.”
“Tommy J.P.” : Most of the women
voters of Broken Hill roused admiration by the
business-like way they set about recording their
first really truly vote. A few afforded amusement.
One lady arrived before the touts. She entered
the polling-booth at the Town Hall, and took a
chair near the door. Voters came and went, but
still the lady kept her seat, until at last she
became an object of suspicion to the rather intel-
ligent policeman on duty near the door. Robert
interrogated the lady. “Have you voted yet,
Madam?” “Voted, is ut? Why, man aloive,
they haven’t called me name yet!” “Oh, yer
name won’t be called. You lav ter go wup ta th’
table an’ see if ya name’s on th’ roll, an’ git ya
papers, an’ record ya vote.” Then the lady
snorted, “Well, well, luk at that now, an’me
been waitin’ here patiently f’rr th’ lasht two hours
f’rr me name t’be called. Now, I shan’t vote at
all. I’ll go shtraight home ! ” And she did.
UNDIGNIFIED!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John See having attributed the results of
the Federal elections in N.S.W. to the misrepre-
sentation and abuse of the Sydney daily papers,
the Daily Telegraph refers to the statement as
“somewhat undignified language.”
Of course we can’t expect the daily papers to agree
With everything that’s uttered by that noble merchant,
See.
The rift grows wider in the lute when Mr. See declares
That truth is quite a stranger to most articles of theirs.
It shows a lack of manners and of gentlemanly pride—
It’s born of simple boorishness, and quite undignified,
To have the “ hide”
To say they lied,
Is certainly undignified !
And tho’ we know the papers are conducted on a plan
That twists the acts and speeches of the opposition man ;
And that, for party purposes, they’ll often tell a cram,
And fill up many columns with an awful lot of flam—
To tell them that the world would be much cleaner if
they died,
Is reprehensible indeed, and quite undignified !
They can’t abide
To hear they’ve lied—
Such language is undignified.
Perhaps this land will know a time (tho’ distant seems
the day !)
When papers will be managed in a widely diff’rent way.
When honesty and fairness to their pages won’t be
strange,
And they will hold the man a rogue who’d try that rule
to change !
But that won’t be until the men that run ’em now are
fried,
And past remarking that plain speech is most un-
dignified—
Till they have died
Who now decide
That telling truth’s undignified 1
N.S.W.

Such a campaign of lying and slander as
the Sydney daily press carried on at last election
could only have succeeded through a degree of
stupidity on the part of the voters that is to the
reasonable mind almost unimaginable. But ex-
perience proves that almost anything in the way
of a lie can be foisted on the public, provided it is
told often enough. This is the story that a big Syd-
ney solicitor circulated around Darling Point. A
friend of his (name given) had a pet sheep (name
given) which ran on his lawn; a country friend
(name given) visiting him, remarked that the
sheep wanted shearing and shore it. For this,
as he was not a union shearer, he was sum-
moned and fined £5 by the Arbitration Court.
As a lie, for stupidity and clumsiness this beat
even the Petriana fiction and the Six Hatters
Exclusion mvth. It is such a wretchedly absurd
lie that The B jlletin is a trifle ashamed to point
out that the Arbitration Court in N.S.W. has so
far not had the sheep-shearing industry under
review and has made no rule at all about it; that
the Court has no power in any industry to inter-
fere with the friendly and unpaid service of one
man to another ; and that it has never summoned
or fined anybody for shearing anybody’s sheep
under any circumstances whatsoever. Still this lie
was firmly believed and used as an election argu-
ment by lawyers and professional men, and in
some cases it was hard to judge whether drivelling
idiocy or shameless mendacity was the explana-
tion.
* * * *
; An Australian Major, who, after the
S’African war, was invalided to England, re-
turned to S’Africa, and lately got back to Aus-
tralia, accounts for the hatred of his countrymen
over there:—
(1) The last few contingents were mostly a bad lot.
They didn’t go to fight; they went to loot. The stupid
reports in the papers encouraged that idea—the boasting
letters of early contingenters, I mean. (2) On the road
down, when the trouble was over, our men behaved dis-
gracefully many times; so often, in fact, that when
Australians were coming the guards were doubled as a
matter of course. All S’Afrieans were “ Dutchies,” and
all, friend and foe alike, suffered. They were going
home, and they didn’t care a damn. (3) Jealousy has
something to do with it —a good deal. The Australian
at the war kept one eye for the Boer and the other for a
Billet. He often saw the Billet first. (4) On the whole,
he 8 a much more pushing, wide-awake, up-to-date
person than the S’African. (5) And he has let ’em know
he thinks so.
The Law made another
exhibition of itself in Mel-
bourne lately when it de-
cided, by a Full Court con-
sisting of Justices Madden,
Holroyd and A’Beckett,
that the copper ripple-plate
of a battery was a fixture
on the soil, tenement or
messuage, and, therefore,
could not be stolen in com-
mon law, even though the
ripple-plate in question was
stolen and removed and sold
to a receiver. Also, the
law held that, not having
been “stolen,” it could not
be received, though it was
received. It is doubtless
good law, but it seems to
open up a tremendous num-
ber of possibilities. If a
ripple-plate is a fixture, a
battery or a house or a tree
is one also, and there is
nothing to prevent ill-regu-
lated persons detaching
these implements from the
estates they are fastened to
and removing them piece-
meal. Chief Justice Mad-
den publicly scoffed at the
Law, and commended the
Queensland code, which
provides that every inani-
mate thing which is capable
of being moved is capable
of being stolen.
The surveyor’s department of Mel-
bourne City Council is still helpless before the
dust nuisance. There was recently a cry to “oil
the streets, ” but that bubble soon burst. Next,
tar, from which the water had been distilled by
a chemist appointed for the purpose, was advo-
cated, and the fashionable east end of Collins-
street was decided on as the experimental plot.
The distilled tar was applied and covered with
sand. When the latter had been deposited by the
wind in all the dwellings and consulting-rooms of
the quarter, a second covering was applied, and
so on. But the summer sun began to wax warm
and the tar commenced to melt, so that it was
impossible to step from the tram without accumu-
lations of it adhering to the boots and being car-
ried into the adjacent palatial premises. Com-
plaints of spoilt carpets were numerous, and more
sand was applied—and blown off. Then blue-
stone screenings were put on top of the tar in the
hope of holding down the demon, and the old-
fashioned steam roller was rolled over it, but the
tar still finds its way to the surface or flows
towards the gutter, where a special channel has
been cut to receive it. Unless Irvine’s breath
can be used to keep the tar cool there does not
seem to be any remedy. Meanwhile, the doctors’
end of Collins-street would gladly give the respon-
sible parties the free run of its poison bottle.

“ F.” writes re the alleged unfinishedness
and practical abandonment of the soldiers'
memorial on St. Kilda-road (Melbourne): —
The wrongness of this allegation will be proved in a
week or two by the withdrawal of the scaffolding and the
official “ opening” of the memorial. To my knowledge,
the work has never stopped. It is not a Government job.
A notice-board states that the memorial is being erected
by a Vic. Mounted Rifle corps, and, presumably, it is
being paid for b.v monthly levies on the subscribers.
Which would explain why the contractor has proceeded
calmly and steadily with the stony task. He took no
risks. The moderately ugly monument appears to be
quite finished.

A Point Macleay (S.A.) half-caste lecturing
an Adelaide missionary meeting on the duty of
being good to one’s wife (Register, Nov. 25):
I have a wife, and everybody on the mission station
will tell you I have never said anything to her. I have 15
children. (Laughter.) I have had twins there. (Loud
laughter.) I tell all you young men that I hope you will
follow my example. (Uncontrollable laughter.)
Curious how the Truly Good always “comeat”
any little thing like that.
The honorary magistrate—especially the
Victorian honorary magistrate—is an unusual
creature, but two of him (of Prahran) were lately
more than usually unusual, and more wildly
honorary than the average. In this case a girl-
wife applied for maintenance. Her story, which
doesn’t seem to have been contradicted with any
energy, was that her husband treated her with
fiendish cruelty (one item in the charge was that
he knelt on her for an hour shortly before her
confinement, the result being that the child was
born crippled), and that he kept her totally
without money. Then the honorary Bench dis-
missed the case on the ground that defendant had
made a bona fide offer to take his wife back.
It seems a curiously irrelevant view to take
of the matter. No one had apparently denied
his bona fide readiness to take her back. In fact
her opinion seems to have been that he urgently
wanted her back so that he might hit her in a
fresh place, and that he was intensely and pain-
fully bona fide in the matter. The wife asked for
a little money and a cessation of the walloping
process and the bench gave her a stone —at least it
said that a house, probably of wood with a stone
foundation, was at her service. To the J.P. mind
a house is a cure for all ills, and a brick cottage
can minister to a mind diseased or repair a broken
heart.

A Grecian female oyster-saloon keeper in
Brisbane was recently fined for employing two
young girls 90 hours per week for a wage of 3s.
She “parted” just two-fifths of a penny per hour.
And yet Byron wrote “ Maid of Athens, ere we
part ” —as if she was liable to part at any moment.

The Sydney tram conductors, or some of
them, now merely announce the name of the street
at which they stop without mentioning the word
“ street ” itself. The idea is a good one. Afore-
time it used to be “Kn-ST-KEET.” The passen-
ger knew it was a street, and that was all. Nowa-
days he hears “ King,” and he has sense enough
to know it is a thoroughfare and not an oil-
painting.

“Grasstree”: “Periwinkle,” in his par re
picnics (Bulletin, 15/11/’O3), does not give fair
play to Methodists. He says they only give me
feed during the day, and charge a shilling for it.
But look at the excitement they provide ! Read
the record of broken legs, broken arms, children
ptomaine-poisoned, girls kicked by horses, run
over by buggies, or by trains, that have occurred
at Methodist picnics during, the last couple' of
years! If “Periwinkle” wants two feeds for a
“bob” with all this thrill thrown in, he is a
glutton.
Tho Convent of the Good Shepherd at
Perth (W.A.) had two days’ experience of an
impenitent Emily, of “evil fame,” and then sent
her back to the police as an incurable case who
wouldn’t work. Refusal to work speedily dis-
qualifies a lost sister for salvation at any
religious soul-saving establishment. Emily,
for her part, alleges that she was put to work
for 15 hours at a stretch, and had her arm
burned by a good shepherdess as punishment for
daring to talk during business hours. The lady
superior of the convent was called upon to appear
at the police court and refute the hot-iron story,
and the court decided that the refutation was
satisfactory, and that Emily was a bad lot. Pro-
bably the court was right, and there was no shred
of truth in Emily—still, just because the world is
full of Emilies, The Bulletin intends bringing
out with all speed its own patent and irre-
proachable convent or refuge, which will be
above suspicion. It will be surrounded by
iron railings instead of by walls, and will
have movable back, front and sides. All
work will be done with the front of the build-
ing taken out, so that the exact hours of labor
may be observed from the road. Meals will
be taken similarly, so that the quality of the
food may be inspected from the road. Religious
instruction ditto, so that the quality of the doc-
trine may be examined from the road. Confession
and penance will be done in the middle of the
road, so as to be open to inspection. The audited
balance-sheet will be hung on the gate, so that
passers-by can see how the money is applied.
People like Emily will sleep on the railings, so
that the public can see that they aren’t ill-used at
any hour of the day or night. The Scarlet
Woman, if that visible symbol exists, will sit on
the gate-post, so as to be open to inspection. In
fact, the telescope of examination will dispel the
breath of scandal, and the most violent Emily
won’t be able to circulate a coherent accusation.

N.S.W. Justice Walker :
He could feel no confidence that a man whose sense of
honor would not of itself make him a trustworthy witness
would become such by reason of the religious sanctity of
an oath.
And that being so, why does not Justice Walker
urge the abolition of court test oaths ? Nothing
more farcical, or indeed irreligious, can be con-
ceived than the swearing practices of our law
courts.

THE SIEGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JfIULL •panoplied in silken state
While trumpets boldly play their part
A woman comes who would besiege—
And take by storm—a poor man’s heart
She wages war with daintiness,
And cunningly she quells her foe
By craft like Joshua and his hosts
Before the walls of Jericho
Poor foe ! he deems himself secure
In mighty strength that naught can harm,
Nor knows that, pacing slow, she weaves
Around him a most potent charm.
So from his castle wall he scans
The splendor of her brave advance,
Smiling, serene, as one who eyes
The treading of some stately dance.
But, when her spells are fully wrought,
Once more her challenge rings on high—
He stands a space, still undismayed,
And then—surrenders utterly!
ALICE J. MUSKETT.
THE SLUMP OF GLORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was talking-, the other day, to an officer of European
experience, who informed me that in a short time most
of the Continental armies will be dressed in dirty-grey or
kharki. But the French Army rose as one multitude and
bitterly, and successfully, objected to the abolition of its
red trousers. A corpse in red trousers is a national hero
—in mud-colored moleskins he is carrion.
I hail the fact; I like to know
Before I start to sing
That men in butchers’ clothes must go
To do their butchering.
Who lured the Heroes of old times
To battle ? Bards bygone
In scarlet ballads, purple rhymes
And verse vermilion.
And young men rushed upon their doom,
And rode away to war,
All for the cussed snow-whi e plume
Of Henry of Navarre.
In that great primal Grecian day
When Heroes fought at Troy,
No Homer could have sung if they
Had fought in corduroy.
A man may die, and proudly die,
For home and beauty—if
In green and scarlet he may lie
When he is cold and stiff.
