A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 29, 1903.
My Dear Arini,—
The holy season of “geese on earth and good
fill to men ” is as specially associated with the pro-
duction of Handel’s “Messiah ” as with the Xmas
pudding. Sydneysiders filled tfieir Town Hall to
the number of 4000 on Xmas night to hear the
immortal oratorio. A sacred open-air festival
given at Sydney Cricket Ground on the same
night drew another four or five thousand. This
festival was arranged by Madame Christian and
Mr. Oliver Reece. The ex-star still shines some-
what from behind her convent walls, and she
“arranges” many musical performances, yet
never sees their production. There’s something
pathetic in it. One may imagine her waiting to
be told on the morrow “ how it all went off.”
For the past six or seven months the name of
Gerontius has been growing familiar to Sydney-
siders through sporadic newspaper - mention.
“The Dream of Gerontius,” a lyric poem, by
Cardinal Newman, realised in music, oratorio
form, by Dr. Elgar, was produced for the first
time on this section of the planet by Mr. J. A.
Delaney, leader of St. Mary’s choir, last week, at
Sydney Town Hall. Many persons had, months
ago, bought the tiny booklet, read the dream, and
then endorsed Gladstone’s comment about it
as one of the finest things in our language.
But these were of the elect, who are first over the
fence in the pursuit of culture. Others read about
20 lines, then, with a worried expression, glanced
at the end to see who finally got Gerontius’
soul, Heaven or , then looked to the title
page and said, “Good gracious!” wonderingly,
at the intimation, “ XII. Edition.” I had read
the poem years ago, and thought that Gerontius
took his dying too seriously Most of us do take
our deaths that way—a weakness of ours. Quite
lately I got hold of the score of the oratorio,
and Once, when in pursuit of material for a
t-ory military story, I was shown at fortified
Queenscliffe (Vic.) the barbed wire, spread over
the glacis of the fort to mesh a hostile invading
force. It was half-covered with wild convolvuli.
Had forgotten that memory and the sensation it
gave me until the sight of the score, bristling
with difficulties, recalled both. Mr. Delaney
fully deserve! the laurel and. wreaths and
bay-leaf batons and floral harps and other
appropriate symbols handed up to him. He
carried all the difficult, subtly-defended positions
without apparent effort, rushed his massed forces
up musical precipices with ease and brilliancy,
and raced them down pianissimo ravines as
smoothly as the water comes down at Wentworth
Falls. His detached forces, such as the soul
of Gerontius, the mezzo-soprano angel, and the
priest, also covered themselves with glory—the
glory of hard work admirably done (hard, too, in
the sense of being quite unmelodious).
Great Pluto—-how dismal was it all! Grand in
spots ; as, for instance, in the hymn of the Choir
of Angelicals, “ Praise to the Holiest in the
Height! ” with its strange, sudden close, as if an
iron shutter had dropped between the majestic
volume of sound and the ears that listened. I
liked best the demon chorus, “The Mind Bold
and Independent.” The demons jeer and shriek
and yell most fiendishly, singing “Ha, ha, ha!”
making noises like the whirring of owls’ wings,
and in effect asserting that they wouldn’t have
Gerontius’ soul at any price (the grapes being
sour) —no, not if it were offered to them with a
large, well-irrigated tract of country. Judging
by the voices, many of the demons were girls.
Judging by facts, a good many demons will be
girls.
Platform looked a great picture, with its hun-
dreds of black-and-white-clad men and white-clad
young women singers, who, when they fluttered a
fan apiece, had a far-off resemblance (from the
western gallery) to winged creatures. The red-
robei Cardinal, in honor of whose sacerdotal
jubilee the oratorio was produced, sat below
the platform. On his right were the white,
gleaming shoulders of Mayoress Hughes. Audience
was large and intensely appreciative of the
sonorous warring of celestial and infernal powers
and the very wailful special pleading of a saint in
the body and out of the body.
Mrs. Nellie Martel, who stood for the Senate
in the recent elections and didn’t do very well,
threatens, ’tis said, to issue a writ against a
Sydney weekly for publication of certain matter
alleged to be derogatory to her.
At a Beauty Show, held at Leeds (Eng.) re-
cently, a man and a woman were chosen, out of
many entries, as the highest types of physical
culture to represent England at the international
tournament in New York, at which the winners
will receive £2OO each. The woman’s age is 20
years; her height, sft. 3|in. ; weight, 1261 b. ;
waist, 24£in. ; bust, 33|in.
Physical culture is culting so strongly in Sydney
that it would be an interesting move of the schools
to hold a Beauty Show—or rather a perfect-form
contest —the perfect form as developed by this or
that method, and in opposition to the “erect
form ” that is corset-made. An Australian
Governor’s wife got a physical culture outfit as a
Xmas present this time last year. No name
accompanied the gift, and it was sent on to a con-
valescent home. The lady thought she was
already sufficiently cultured.
Their Excellencies the Rawsons, who will pre-
sently depart for Moss Vale, attended St. Andrew ft
Cathedral on Xmas day, and got plenty of Xmas
bush and flannel flower decorations and Athan-
asian creed. Can’t imagine the recitation of that
creed among Australian wild-flower surroundings.
One of the minor studies of these tea-room-
rushed holidays is the waitress who takes your
order and materialises the same. The rapidity
with which she does both rivals shooting the
chute at Manly. The tea-shop girl marries easily,
so perhaps men like being waited on with a bang
and a clang and a rush and a blush (I don’t know
about the last, but it rhymes with her leading
characteristic of rush). Yet papers like Home
Chokes and Woman’s Skite are always telling
girls that a man likes repose in his wife. “ Cheer-
fulness by all means, but no bustle or clatter.
If the tea-room girl marries a poor man and does
her own work, she can afford to get up late and
yet let him have his breakfast in time. Anyhow
the male tea-room browser likes the lightning-
change waitress even in the Cold Country. _ Last
year the chairman, at the annual meeting of
the Aerated Bread Company, deplored the fact
that so great was the demand in the marriage
market for their best waitresses that they had to
fill an average of four vacancies each week. It is
the practice of the company to present each bride
with a wedding cake, and this, in addition to the
presents sometimes made by customers, the chair-
man suggested, had considerably increased the
number of marriages. And the co., thinking of
its profits, contemplates in the future presenting
young ladies, on engagement, with a smudge on
the nose or a black eye.
N.S.W. Public Service Commissioner Delohery
has figured lately in the Law Courts in an (as yet)
unsuccessful attempt to prevent a financial insti-
tution building next door. Pa Delohery has
owned his present house in a fashionable street
for some years, and the co. threatened to put up
a house next door which would block up the bath-
room windows of Pa Delohery aforesaid. So, with
one son as barrister and other instructing him as
solicitor, the clan Delohery sought to suppress the
invading co. with the theory of “Ancient Lights”
(they are called “Auld Lichts” in Scotland), £in old
English idea which, under certain conditions,
gives to a landlord the light which comes through
his windows, and allows him to be visible from
outside while having his bath. So far the Court
has held that the doctrine of Ancient Lights does
not apply in Australia. Perhaps our lights are
not sufficiently ancient, or perhaps what we
require is a bit of shade.
The late CAPTAIN CARRINGTON, of the
s.s. Coogee.
That tragedy which overtook the steamer
Coogee in Bass Straits last Friday was the sort of
thing which would sound too improbable even to
put in a novel. There was a big Italian ship
drifting helplessly in the dense fog and the early
moiling gloom. Then the steamer, dodging
through the same curtain, passed under the lum-
bering craft’s jibboom, which raked the whole
length of the deck, killed Captain Carrington,
badly injured everybody else within reach, and
knocked mast, funnel, bridge, boats, deck-houses,
&c., into a heap. The Italians, though badly
encumbered with those' long names that the
Roman always drags after him, s 3ein to have done
everything that was possible; their own vessel
had a hole the size of an omnibus in the bow, yet
it towed the Coogee 90 miles on its homeward
way. The deceased Captain Carrington had made
1810 trips across the strait in command of the
Coogee, and was about the best-known and best-
liked shipmaster on the coast. There are many
ways in which a sailor can die, but to be struck by
a strange vessel’s jibboom on the high seas is about
the strangest death of all.
Four crippled English gentlemen and one ditto
English lackey—crippled by one French gentle-
man fighting against fearful odds, and with
(according to the book) only a light dress-sword !
The Frenchman is always brave and honorable,
the Englishmen (those he pinked) are bullies or
cowards, and a sixth (a Buke too!) cheats at
cards! Five Englishmen crippled by one
foreigner and paraded for the laughter of
a huge audience, which included the repre-
sentative of British Majesty glittering in
the managerial box! That’s an astonished
memory I brought from seeing first performance
in Sydney of “Monsieur Beaucaire” at Her
Majesty’s. French Consulate was fully repre-
sented in the dress circle and its smiles had no
reason to be on the wrong side of its face.
One may describe the story of “Monsieur
Beaucaire” with the much-abused word “charm-
ing,” then add “ froth.” In the book the heroine,
Lady Mary Carlyle, has a heart of chalk, in the
play a heart of gold. And the play, unlike the
book, gives you the happy (conventional) ending
which, because things seen are mightier than
things read, is the right conclusion and saves you
from that sinking feeling when the curtain drops
on the last act, and you pull out the hat-pin that
skewers your wraps together with a joyful feeling
that the princely Jack has won his lovely Jill.
One could easily be a Lady Sncerwell about
book and play. Booth Tarkington must have
written with his tongue in his cheek at times
but it is delightful to watch the men of fashion
and the fine ladies of Bath live over again for us
some of the conventional lies of their civilisation.
Their attitudinising, their deliberate struttmgs,
their low bowings and curtseyings and their
funereally slow minuets took up so much time in
a confessedly fast period that one would guess,
even if one didn’t know for certain, that they had
no automobiles or kitchen lancers. (By the way,
I’m not certain about the kitchen lancers.)
A passionate prince disguised as a barber is
very much the fairy prince of romantic girlhood,
and Mr. Julius Knight, who got a gladsome
greeting after his long absence, kept up
the pleasing illusion, and so struck sympathetic
sparks from his audience, despite the fact that
it was not all eighteen years old and feminine.
Miss Maud Jeffries, who also got a warm
welcome, looked very handsome, and filled her
rdle with the stately artificiality that the part
seemed to demand.

Among the wedding presents sent to a recently-
made bride were no fewer than eight belt buckles.
8.-b.’s are going to have a great vogue, say fashion
authorities. The Sydney Goldsmiths and Silver-
smiths Alliance is just now showing some par-
ticularly novel specimens. One is an Amen-
can-looking eagle of oxydised silver, with
turquoise eyes and a Declaration-of-Independence
expression. The American peacock charm is
another of the Alliance’s specialities, and so is the
peacock hatpin, showing the “eyed” feather
under a transparent disc. The Nellie Stewart
wish-bone ring is another novel circlet. And if you
want a diamond necklet, tied in a quaint bow and
fit for a fairy queen or just a nice human girl,
take a tram and more than your fare, and go and
get it. Enamel trinkets, in the form of flowers,
buds and stars, are also at the Alliance, and on
the invention of new charms inventors have ex-
pended much brain force. A very thin watch, for
a dress-coat pocket, is a good thing for a beau who
is particular about his fit and figure, and who
would perish if his clothes bulged.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“The Crossley visit to Gippsland was the
signal for high old times in that sleepy region of
fat cattle. Bairnsdale covered itself with glory
by giving the singer a ride in a bullock-dray.
‘ Our Ada ’ jumped at the notion, with a view to
photographs in the English papers, and accord-
ingly made her entrance into Bairnsdale behind a
span of eighteen beasts, with a driver glorious in
full bullocky rig according to the gospel of Rolf
Boldrewood. The bone-shaker was turned for the
nonce into a bower of greenery, and ameliorated
by armchairs.
“ Sale, which has large ideas, and looks upon
itself as the hub of fashion, woke from its
perennial sleep to make a joyful noise before Miss
Crossley. The mayor, a bustling Yankee, gave a
huge reception in her honor, and thereto came
from the hollow trees and wire fences all the un-
• numbered tribe who ever claimed remote cousin-
ship with the singer. She was presented with a
fearful and wonderful jewel, representing a native
bear, a kangaroo, an emu and a jackass gambolling
round the Southern Cross. Poor old Southern
Cross—how it is worked to death ! The
local talent sang, and the guest of the
afternoon applauded until her gloves split.
One damsel warbled ‘Out on the Rocks,’ and
wept in the evening when Miss Crossley gave it
as an encore. The concert was crowded at night,
and the audience yelled itself hoarse with delight
at each number, and addressed the singer freely
as ‘Ada.’ A motley collection of flowers was
given her, ranging from a many-guinea bouquet
from Melbourne to posies of geranium and wall-
flower tied up with sprigs of pepper-tree.
‘ Ourada ’ gathered them all in with the same
all-embracing smile until they became too heavy
for her, and were dumped on the platform. After
the concert the ‘ swagger ’ contingent carried
Miss Crossley off to a small and select supper,
where they asserted freely that she was a jolly
good fellow until the small hours of the morning.
“In the Crossley boom, there has been one
omission. Ada hasn’t been asked to xJ an f a
vegetable. Viee-Regals, Melbas, and Dukelets
have held spades when spades were trump
local gardens, but not Ada. As soon as
Waxellency succeeded to the ‘ Stomngton .
and the dependencies thereof, Curator G .
secured him for a spade job, and the y. r^an A onK
man did his duty for all he was worth. A
the select feminines who have spaded, t I
formance of Lady Tennyson ranks highest. °
ripped off he* gloves and dug like a gardens.
She loves gardening.

“ For the sake of Ada Crossley and W. Peter-
kin’s star parts in the Philharmonic ‘ Messiah
performance, a great throng packed into tne
Town Hall Christmas night, and steamed in sym-
pathy. The beautifully-prepared (expression is
used without prejudice) contralto wore white, all
a-quiver with silver points, and comet-tails run-
ning in the direction that makes for the elongated
effect. A large chou of the shade of the purple

“ The Ballarat relatives of Miss Florence Towl,
one of the first fledglings to flutter Europe-ways
from the Marshall-Hall Conservatorium, are
delighted with her successes in Germany, ohe
has been jilaying in opera, and her flattering
press notices would make a long day’s reading. ■ 1
wonder does she still make grimaces ? In the old
student days, her singing was often marred by
the sauerkraut faces that occurred in the
strong, soulful passages of her German songs.
“ Another Marshall-Hall student, Miss Kate
Samuels, is now ‘Madame Benda’ (hats. off,
Bendigo) to the concert world, but in private
circles, Mrs. Wesley Mills. She met and married
in Leipzig the American of that name. He is
a professor of physiology at Montreal, and a
throat specialist of renown. Presently Madame
Benda makes an American concert tour.
“ ‘ Madame Benda 5 is a reasonably good name,
but when the great Ballarat or Geelong star
comes along and calls herself Madame Bally, or
Madame Gee-gee, this habit of naming singers
after their birth-towns will fall into disrepute.
“Beautiful to observe the qualified artists ex-
changing compliments in a sub rosa manner at
a recent show. So naive to smile their admira-
tion at each other in full view of the watchful
audience, or to murmur ‘Fine!’ with a confir-
matory nod. This sort of thing sets a seal of
approval on the boisterous applause, and the
crowd considers itself more than ever a good
judge, when sister, or brother artists murmur
congratulations.
“ Collins-street has lost its dear old beau —Pat
M'Caughan. Melbourne had no greater damdy,
and no more critical connoisseur of feminine
charms. His eye ever kindled at sight of a neat
figure, a smart foot, or a bright face.
“ The John Cookes, of frozen-meat fame, have
taken the Governor’s summer residence on Mace-
don Heights. The charming little roost yawned
for a summer occupant.
“ The most exploited fashion of the day is the
belt with the tails. The resourceful She finds her
cravat come in handy as an appendix. Some of
the tails spread out into fan shape, a few hang
straight down with chenille bobs or drops at the
end. The most elaborate are the chintz silks,
piped and boned and fur-tailed. The boning
business becomes a nuisance. All the approved
collars have whalebone partitions that stab the
neck at every intake of oxygen. It’s very trying.
But there’s nothing for it. The nude neck sug-
gests a kittenish kind of coquetry that the proper
find intolerable. And, this hot weather, it’s
either no collar at all, or one girded up to the
ear lobe with whalebone.
“ Among the Christmas appeals in the Argus,
the other day, one turned up from the Lost Dogs’
Shelter. Like all the rest of these appeals, the
letter was footed with the full name and address
of the person controlling the institution. And,
after all, there appears no good reason why an
advertisement should not be hung on to the tail of
a lost dog.
“Labor member Bromley, last week, made
another casual attempt (and another failure) to
pick the lock of Puritanism that keeps Melb.
Public Library closed to the public on Sundays.
Bromley’s way of urging the claims of Common
Sense in this matter is quite unworthy of its im-
portance. He approaches the political Sabba-
tarians cap in hand, and humbly suggests that
those heterogeneous Tories shall consent to the
opening of the Library on condition that no em-
ployee be ‘ compelled to work on any Sunday
against his conscience.’ The amendment, if passed
in this form, would presumably prove inoperative.
The attendance of the staff—or a sufficient number
of the staff—can be secured by offering a cash
inducement to conscience-breakers. Lord’s pay
for Lord’s Day.—Yours affectionately,

street (Sydney) Chinaman is named William
Francis Xavier Hin Chin.
Deeming, Australia’s boss wife-murderer,
lived for a time in the building in King-street,
Sydney, in which Truth newspaper is now pub-
lished.
Who was the oldest voter at the recent
elections? Mrs. G. S. Smith, of Summer Hill
(N.S.W.) was aged 90, and there was a Viotorian
lady voter of 91.
No Hebrew minister officiating in
Australia is Australian born. Up to the other
day, and for 12 years past, Melbourne had one ;
but he has resigned.
John Watsford (born at Parramatta Dec.
5,1820) is the oldest and best-known of Methodist
ministers in Australia. He resides in Melbourne,
and still preaches occasionally.
The estate of Baronet Nicholson, formerly
of N.S.W. and Queensland, who died recently in
England at the age of 95 years, has been proved
for "probate purposes at £87,810.
Ex-Premier John Hall, of M.L., now into
his 80th year, has been going round listening to
Chamberlain, and reckons him up the best and
most convincing speaker he ever heard.
It was part of the general fitness of things
that the intensely grovelling address presented to
Gov. -Gen. Tennyson at Fremantle (W.A.) was
signed by Cadd, mayor, and Humble, town clerk.
A pious stock and sharebroker is some-
thing out of the common. Sydney Stock Exohange
boasts of one, who carries around a pocketful of
tracts which he distributes amongst the gamblers.
“ W.’ from Adelaide :
Nellie Martell, who tailed the poll for the Senate in
N.S.W., is a sister of ex-Senator Charleston who was
defeated in S.A. Two Camerons were thrown out in
Tasmania, while S.A. and W.A. each lost a Solomon.
The manuscript book of an eloquent
Senator got into the hands of the curious lately,
and they delight to tell how he has speeches
headed “impromptu” written out for occasions
expected to occur within the next month or two,
also smart retorts ready for jokes that he already
sees looming in the distance. The far-seeing man,
who can foretell the probable joke of next month,
has a big advantage in politics.

hospital for some weeks. A growth on the tongue

A. certain Queensland politician is reputed
to have once fallen in heavily over a take-down
game of poker. There was a put-up game wherein
the victim was to get three aces and bet heavily,
and another man was to get a full hand and
gather in the spoil to be afterwards divided. But
after big betting all round, bauds were shown and
the victim produced four aces and collected over
£2OO. How the fourth ace got into his hand is to
this day a myster *.
Julius Caesar advertises in a recent S.M.
Herald that he will not be responsible for any
debts contracted in his name. About 1950 years
ago Julius declared his wife must be above sus-
picion, and apparently he is still struggling to live
up to the theory.
The little Bendigo sect of “ Flitterers,”
which lately knooked off work and decided not to
live in houses any more, finds that it isn’t as
healthy as regular meals and a camp out of the
wet. Mrs. Flitterer lately died in a Vic. hospital
of typhoid fever.
The Marquis of Normanby, son of an ex-
Governor of three Australasian States, is in the
church, and has recently married money. The
title carried so little coin, that the present holder,
who is 57 years of age, had to turn Mulgrave
Castle into a boarding-school.
Adelaide has its athletic parson in C. E.
Doudney, who is one of the finest rifle shots in
the State, plays cricket and tennis well (especially
tennis), and is the best rowing coach procurable
in the holy city. His work is with old Dean
Marryat at fashionable Christ Church.
When the Rev. mystery, the Count Vay de
Vaya, was in Australia he was credited with being
that rare bird, a oleric without a collection plate.
But in Maoriland, it seems, he did get on the
collecting game, and bagged £l6O for an orphan-
age in Eastern Corea, of all places in the world !
Nicholas Willis, M.P., complained, at
a Federal dinner the other night, of the “ poor
character ” of the Protectionist candidates put up
in N.S.W.; and he was promptly reminded that,
at any rate, the Protectionist organisation would
not swallow the candidate he recommended to
it—Eden George.
The death is announced of Sir E. S. Dawes,
head of the firm of Gray, Dawes and Co., of
London. The name is an old one in commercial
Sydney. Sixty-three years ago Stuart Alexander
Donaldson, afterwards Premier, was in partner-
ship with William Dawes under the stylo of
Donaldson and Dawes.

been raised led me to the following discoveries.

kept by men named Beer, Brew, Glass, and
Brewin; north side by Drinkwater, Beverago,
and Drought. Am still exploring and expect to
make further discoveries.

On the Friday morning after the elections,
when Trenwith’s score stood at 90,000 odd, 10,000
ahead of the next best, a postcard was delivered
at the Age office addressed “W. A. Trenwith,
care of D. Syme, Age Office, Melbourne.” The
sender congratulated Mr. Trenwith, per medium
of Mr. Syme, on his great victory.
Squatter Angas of S.A. has given £lO,OOO
for the Christianising of Papua. Angas is now
80, and probably reckons that to Christianise
Papua will be a pleasing thing to Providence. A
more humble and reverend spirit might suggest
that Providence knew best what religion is good
for Papua, and provided that religion.
Sydney Alderman J. D. Fitzgerald has
cut short a trip through Japan because of per-
sistent police shadowing. J.D.F.—probably on
account of his fierce and desperate expression—
was taken for a Russian spy and dogged relent-
lessly on his trips. This proved so irksome that
he took the first steamer back to Sydney.

“ Molb.” digs up an old memory :
A Jay or two ago I was at Harwood’s old place,
Montreux, near Kew. To my mind, it is the nicest little
patch anywhere about. Harwood had socurud about an
acre, on a slope towards the north, with the moat
delicious view imaginable, in Melbourne environs, right
over the country to Heidelberg. The one-storey villa is
a perfect ideal, with its coachhouse, garden and lawn.
Such was the haven of the saving comedian, hurst, up by
the Boom smash. Here, on Sundays, he entertained tho
Broughs, Stewarts, Coppins, Grevllles, and ull the nicest
people.
A well-known Victorian city magnate and
station owner, who believed be had a thorough
knowledge of stook, on one occasion, when some
of his own sheep wore being drafted in bis pre-
sence, told the manager to get rid of them at
onoe, as they (the sheep) “weretooold when they
lost their top front teeth.” As a remedy for foot-
rot, he suggested “tin boots” filled with n. solu-
tion of his own prescription. The solos of the
front boots were to lie made thinner than the
hind ones, in order that they “ wouldn’t interfere
with the sheep when feeding.” And he actually
got quotations from several firms for the supply
of sheep boots. Also, he ordered said manager
to run all the cattle (about 1000) round every
morning to see if any had pleuro. Any that
coughed were to be isolated and treated according
to his instructions.
“ C.C." writes :
The members of the Tasmanian Leg. Council may be
only a job lot mentally, but there can he no doubt that,
physically, they would take a lot of beating. I know
most of them, and, although I would not hesitate to “ put
’em up ” to most men old enough to be my grandfather,
I wouldn’t care to enter the House of Dodery to look
for fight. Have a pleasant, though deafening, recollec-
tion of a night spent, after closing time, in a Hobart bar,
where two giddy cadets of the House of Dodery, whoss
combined ages were over 150 years, provided most of the
merriment. They wound-up some time in tho morning
by singing a duet, entitled “ We Never Went Astray,
with a wild fandango after each verse, and they were not
half so dodery on their ping as some of the tired youth
who looked in to see what the row was about. There
is some vivifying essence afloat in the Tas. Upper House
that appears"to grant perpetual youth to its inmates, and
when “ Alfy ” Page and the Hon. William Dodery are
seen frisking along arm-in-arm, the local undertakers
shed tears.
A Sydney paper retails a “ delightful little
incident” concerning G. H. Reid. When the
colonial Premiers went to assist at the Diamond
Jubilations, Gladstone invited them to Hawarden.
This scene occurred in the garden there :
The owner of Hawarden noticed that Mr. Reid, of all
the party in the open air, continued walking with his hat
off. He carried his light head-gear in his hand. Mr.
Gladstone, turning to the Australian politician, said,
“Will you not cover, Mr. Reid?’’ But Mr. Reid, hesi-
tating momentarily, replied in a voice just above a
whisper, vet within the hearing of some of the party,
•• Not in your presence, sir.” How that reply must have
impressed the grand old man, and how it indicated the
intense regard, amounting almost to reverence, which
Mr. Reid entertained for England's great statesman. Mr.
Reid paid a great tribute to Gladstone in that memorable
moment.
But bow much greater the tribute would have
been if Reid bad put his hat firmly on bis head
and gone straight away and imitated Gladstone
by abolishing all his Protective duties. There is
far more sincere flattery in imitation than in
offer tig a view of your naked head with a fly
standing on it.

Re one John Jones :
Modern Athens (otherwise Gawler) has a resident who
begun life with a millstone round ids neck. He was born
Jones, and his dad crowned the indignity by christening
him John. Hu left Knglnnd intending to come direct to
Adelaide, but the agents for tho boat and the captain
didn’t make any definite arrangements, and, John not
being consulted, they landed him at Sydney. But it
apparently made little difference, and J.J. stayed in
N.S.W. for seven years, and then drove overland to
Adelaide, taking 10 weeks on tho trip. He is chiefly
famed in his town for having lived 88 years “with a name
like that,” and for being instrumental in establishing two
churches and introducing Arbor Day to Pawler, and for
being its Mayor once upon a time.
Alexander Gordon, “Q.C., ' called to tho
Bar in 1841, and recently dead in England, prac-
tised half a century ago in the Equity Court at
Sydney. He was also esteemed as an ecclesiastical
lawyer, and was for many years Chancellor of tho
diocese of Sydney. Mr. Gordon was close on 90
at his death. A son (Alexander) leads in the
Bankruptcy Court, Sydney.
An almost forgotten figure in Austra-
lian life has just passed away at the age of 84
years. G. W. Itusdon was tho son of a clergyman,
and came to N.S.W. with his family in 1834. He
was engaged in sheep-farming until 1849, when ho
was appointed ngent for the national schools in
Port Phillip, and afterwards in N.S.W. and at
Moreton Bav. When Port Phillip was separated
from N.S.W. he became Under-Secretary in the
Colonial Secretary’s Office ; next lie was clerk to
the Executive Council, and finally clerk of the
Legislative Council, and clerk of tho Parliaments,
from which position he retired in 1882 on a pension
of £5OO a year. Ife was the originator of the Shale-
speare scholarship in connection with tho Melb.
University. At the Sliaks|Kjarian centenary
oelebration in 1804 the admirers of the poet were
divided as to the maimer in which he should be
commemorated. Harry Sullivan and James Smith
led a section favorable to a statue. This got so
far that a plaster model was made by Summers
and unveiled in front of the Public Library by
Sullivan. It got no further. Itusdon headed the
scholarship crowd, and succeeded, ftusden was
the author of numerous works on Australasian
history, one of which got him into very bad
trouble with John Bryce, of Maoriland. Br < a
sued for liliel atid got £3500 damages.

Aboriginalities.
BILL MAYNE’S STRATEGEM.
[For Thb Bulletin.]
Our tucker-bags were empty, and our stomachs just the
same,
When we reached a Barcoo station in the West,
Where a darkie chap was cooking—“ Yankee Tommy ’’
was his name—
Who was hated hy the swaggies like a pest;
For never had he given sup or fill or snack or bite
To a footman or a horseman calling there ;
But, in answer to a whisper for a feed, would threaten
fight,
And paint bright blue with blasphemy the air 1
“It is plain we must get tucker, get it any way we can,
For it’s eighteen hungry miles to Mulgadown,”
Said Bill Mayne, my mate in trouble ; “ and I think I
know a plan
How to work a point on Ham and do him brown.
It will show him that Australians know a thing or two or
three,
Though they’re humping swag along the dry Barcoo.”
And in hushed and wily whisper William then explained
to me
Exactly what he wanted me to do.
‘ You go up and strike the darkie," whispered William,
“Jfor a bite
Of bread and meat and other things as well,
And when he starts performing, you just challenge him
to fight,
And keep him busy sparring till I yell.
Mind he doesn’t get too near you, hold your distance,”
cautioned Mayne,
“ And keep his back towards the kitchen, see ?
Till I—never mind what I’ll do —till I coo-ee on the plain,
Then give the nigger best and follow me.”
Well, I reckoned on the whole that the job had to be done,
So I faced the black and irritable coon ;
And frankly and at length did I tell that nigger’s son
That he was a blot.upon the face of noon.
And as he charged, the very mildest thing he said was
“ Damn!”
The worst thing that he said I won’t repeat;
And for half-an-hour or more I was mostly dodging Ham,
And fought a losing battle with my feet.
I was getting very anxious when, at last, amidst the din
Of battle I could hear a faint “ Coo-ee 1 ”
And without a moment’s notice I exclaimed : “ Cook, IT]
give in 1
I can see that you are much too good for me ! ”
“ Waal, orlright then, take yer bundle an’ yer carcase
outer this;
And never cadge fer tucker here again.”
The dusky victor snorted with a grin upon his phiz.,
And I did, and followed Bill across the plain.
Well, you hardly can imagine my surprise when I found
Bill
A-camping close to water and dry wood.
Seven miles beyond the station at the bottom of a hill,
And surrounded by enormous wealth of food !
Loaves of new-made bread and brownie ; sugar in galore
and tea,
And a hot and juicy, appetising joint.
He had sneaked into the kitchen while I fought the cook,
you see,
And on our brother Ham had scored a point 1
% x 8.
A QUESTION OF AUDIT.
CHEMIST (to crushed assistant): “ Why, when I
was 18 I managed my boss's shop by myself,
and I took over £36 per week; and I had a
business of my own at 21.”
ASSISTANT: “ That would naturally follow. But
didn't he miss the money?”
“0.K.”: Wandering midst the solitudes
of Flinders (Vic.), a marine village with a scattered
resident population of 120 persons, I met a joke—
a motley joke. It was an announcement, written
on half a sheet of notepaper, pasted against the
window of an alleged ‘ ‘ general store ”: —
Wanted, the miserable Thief who stole 3 cabbages
from my garden to dine with me on Sunday.
The aged hermit of the store had either borrowed
his wheeze from the comic columns of a country
journal or composed it on his lonesome own. I
couldn’t see him anywhere about. The district
seemingly was deserted, except by me and six
dreamy cows on the grassy highway. I contem-
plated the joke at the window until the pathos of
it touched my soul. Poor little joke ! Staring
out on to the wilderness and craving the sym-
pathy of a world that wags in the dim distance.

“ Bunny ” : The first fierce foretastes of a
sizzling summer have recently struck Broken
Hill. A favorite house-costume for languid ladies
is a sash-cinctured nightdress. Why a sash? Well,
really I do not know—not having been game to ask
the reason. The young bird season is in on the
Barrier. A State school boy, asked why he had
been late two mornings in succession, said:
“ Please sa, I wiz kept to cut tup meat for the
young maggies an’ ta chew wup bread for the
littul cockatoos an’ par ruts.”
* * * *
A boy of 14 at Truro (S.A.) driving a
three-horse roller, the other day, was jerked off,
and landed on his back on the ground with the
heavy machine resting on one of his legs. Hold-
ing on to the reins, hs undid the traces of the
nearest horse, got the swingletree loose, and with
it levered the roller off his leg. Then he dragged
himself to the nearest horse, unharnessed it, and,
scrambling somehow on to its back, rode to the
hospital, where he was found to have several
bones broken !
* * * *
‘ ‘ Periwinkle ” in a frivolous mood :
Re “Polstan’s” query (8., 3/12/’O3) —recipe to “fix"
damper—a dish of necessity rather than choice ! Light
your camp-fire on a rock, or hard, even ground, three-
quarters of an hour before you unpack your flour. This
done, measure out five pannikins of the needful into
your billy, and add five pinches of salt. Paw the flour
well to distribute the salt evenly (wash your paws first),
then add water to make a dry dough. Knead well with
the knuckles. Pat out into a pancake shape, about half-
an-inch thick, on the nearest even-topped stump. Clear
away your camp fire a yard to windward, and dump the
dough on the hot earth where the fire was. Turn when
the bottom is brown, lift when the other side is ditto—
and eat hot. That is the way Boomerang Bill—chef
amongst the Overlanders when the kangaroo nibbled tus-
socks on the present site of Tub Bulletin offices—made
damper. Celery Bill, who rides a bike to the sheds,
makes damper thus, when he can’t cadge something for
his stomach : Half a bag of self-raising flour, skim milk
to make a dough, cook the mass in the camp frying-pan
till it rises in due ratio to the fall of shearers’ earnings
since the drought days, and eat till you burst. Throw
balance at gohanna—dogs won’t eat it! Can any
Bulletin gourmet tell me how to cook bandicoot—not
“ stoo ” —to resemble chicken ?
« * * *
“Ny”: Crows are not cunning—only
suspicious ! When a boy I used to snare them by
the score round the slaughter-yards, on Grange
Creek, Hamilton (Vic.), to sell to the Chows at
4d. apiece. The Chows used to split them open—
feathers, entrails, and all—dry, and pack them for
transmission (so tradition said) to China. My
plan. I cut a piece of fresh gristle into pieces the
size of dice. Fixed them, without touching with
fingers, to pieces of fishing gut, then fixed loops
of gut to splinters on the top-rail of yard fence.
Glossy-cloak takes the gristle—catch him, wring
his neck, and claim 4d. blood-money !
* * * *
It isn’t only the travelling judge who
wears much frill when out-back. Some of the
inspectors of post-holes, and cow-brands, and
things of that sort can lay-over even the
frilliest of the Judges. One of them, at a
small bush pub., began impressing the
township by bullying the groom. Then
he complained about the damper, mutton
and jam at tea-time, rang the bell after-
wards for the girl to move his chair to the
fire, and in the morning he complained
about the sheets on the bed being too
narrow, and left instructions for things to
be altered next night. There weren’t any
wider sheets on hand, so the maid tried
to keep them on by pinning them all round.
But that night a dust storm sprang up, and
the dust-laden hessian out of which the
ceiling was made burst, and the noise of the
inspector of post-holes roaring in the dark-
ness and trying to extricate himself from
the pins and calico awoke the next town-
ship.
* * • •
There is a woman knocking about the
North, between Townsville and Geraldton, whose
bush-name is “Annie Bags.” She carries her
swag, travels barefooted, dresses like a gin, and,
like her black sisters, is accompanied by a tribe of
dogs. It is alleged that she receives remittances
from England ; also, it is said she comes of a good
family, is well educated, and is, or was, a brilliant
pianist.

From “Hiphen” (more weird expectancy) :
I have read with astonishment remarks in The Bulletin
to the effect that the “ weird expectancy of the American
forest ”is shared by the Australian bush. Surely this is
not so? To my mind our bush is the very antithesis of all
that expresses futurity or expectancy. It brings home to
me everything that is old-time and prehistoric. It is
asleep always—dreaming in the past. Who can connect
gaunt gums and fire-scarred scrub with futurity ? I have
seen by the Gippsland Lakes forests of blackened tree-
fern, with kangaroo (the most prehistoric and least
evolved mammal extant) in herds among the sun-scorched
bracken, which appeared theatrically primaeval—a living
record of a dead past. Surely there can be no expectant
note in the greyness, the twilight, the hopelessness
(which an Australian must know and revere before he can
be Australian) ? No! there is no expectancy—weird or
otherwise—in our bush ; but if, perchance, i have read
my lesson wrong and it is there, it is that of one very
very weary who, helplessly, awaits the end, and expects
decent burial.
“H.S.R.” writes:
“Old Cove” (8., 3/12/’O3) takes exception
to my query (8., 12/li/’O3) as to any pastoral-
ists who are paying more than 15s. per week.
He says: “No man within 50 miles of
Bingara works for less than £1 per week.”
Now, 75 per cent, of the men on stations
within 50 miles of Bingara work for 15s.
or less. If “Old Cove” gave the name of
his holding I could easily find out whether
he is one of the exceptions. It is quite
easy to write and say “ I never pay less than
£l.” How many men does he employ, and
for how long? I know that overseers on
sheep stations and head stockmen on cattle
get £l, or perhaps more, or at least used to
till quite lately, but the general rank-and-file
of men employed on stations certainly do not.
And, what is more, very few men make £1
per week clear at the heaviest kinds of con-
tract-work, such as fencing and ringing, at
present prices. I will allow “ Old Cove ” from
the Pacific Coast to Mungindi, along the
Queensland border, and 100 miles south of it,
and he cannot find 25 per cent, of workers
getting £1 per week. The wages here (about
Emmaville) are £36 per year on stations, and
even now, at shearing, £1 per week is top
price for rouseabouts. Of course, the pas-
toralists are princes compared to the selector,
and I allow that the wage given is, perhaps,
as much as the circumstances justify.

“ Nymsie" : Can any Bulletin
reader tell me how long shell-parrots
(commonly “love-birds”) live in con-
finement ? Have had several pairs for
five years now. They nest and lay eggs
in a piece of hollow branch I have fixed
in their cage, but the young ones do
not survive. Also, what is the greatest age the
cockatoo (yellow-crested) has been known to
reach ? I knew of one which had put 32 years over
its poll.

“ A.K.C.” on the subject of Murray ling
(“J.C.” in 8., 3/12/’O3): —
A more delicious fish than cat-fish would be difficult to
find, in or out of the Murray. I know several fishermen
who religiously save them (alive, in wire-netting cages)
till sent for by regular customers who know a good thing
when they see it. Their looks are certainly against them.
Whiskers do not become a fish. But, as to their skin
being poisonous, one might as well say that cow-hide’s
poisonous because it isn’t swallowed with the beef it
encases. There certainly is a lot in imagination, even a
“ muddy lagoony smell ” (to quote “ J.C.”).

amongst bushmen, and a travelling chemist in
the north-west (N.S.W.) used to man'ifacture a
salve from the fruit mixed with some chemicals,
which was a good cure for horses’ sort backs.

“ War E.” : Mention of rabbit biting (B.
3/12/’O3) reminds me that the wild variety can
bite, and hard, too. Let “C.C.” get a rabbit in
a log, annoy him, and then attempt to pull him
out by the head, and “ C.C.” will know that for his
size bunny can bite about as hard as any beast on
earth.
A BACKSLIDER.
MAGGIE (to new acquaintance): “ What! YOU
eating pork! ”
“ Tag ” : All timbers increase in length
by annual growth on the top only. In saplings
the lowest limbs are invariably dead and tall off
as others grow on the top. Trees grow in girth on
the outside. Surveyors’ marks reopened after
being 50 years overgrown are not found to be
above the usual height of the axeman ; and wire
fences run into green trees are not disturbed.
The fork of a tree, where the limbs stand fairly
upright, does grow higher by the limbs growing
towards eacli other ; the under side also grows
nearer to the ground. Some of the endogens don’t
swell in girth—examples, the cabbage-tree, fern
and some bamboos. I have grown the cabbage-
tree 80 feet in height and almost the same girth
from bottom to top.

“ Rita Sunyasee ” discourseth in a general
way:
The so-called cynicism of the late lamented M‘Cooey
and “ Scotty the Wrinkler” was an inherited taint of
their environment. The Australian bush is typically harsh
in every aspect- Sit on the banks of the Murray at
dawn, fishing, and in the greyness hear
the rasping sounds of bush-life awaking!
First the kookaburra’s mocking
cacchinations ; then the white
cockatoo’s screechings afar;
followed by the pee-
wit’s piercing trem-
olo. Then the
harsh purr of
the cat - bird,
and the
q u i v e r y,
quakery,
husky call of
the wagtail!
As the day
warms hear the
harsh sounds of
insect life and
even the solitude is
harshness itself.
The note of the bush
is one of hopeless-
ness ; its inherent
slothfulness enters
one’s soul, and the
dregs breed harshness.
Once a fossicker has
found a speck, the
longing o’ercomes him,
in his spells of idleness,
to go forth to scratch for
a fortune, when the
stomach rumbles with
hunger; but the nomads
of the wallaby-ways never
feel like that—when there
is a crust available.
Under their seeming
harshness there
is a kindly trait,
often only ob-
servable to their
familiars, how
ever, and the
bush - Bohemian
is never a mean
man—in his old
age, at least.
Regarding his
failings—these are
all he has left for
us to identify him by, and
they are truly his own, and
they concern only him-
self. Regarding hyda-
tids—what bushman
hasn’t got ’em, how-
ever strong and long
his samplings of the
various microbe-
killers of malt
variety ? Dogs and cats may have something to do with
this curse of the Australian bush ; but all the way-bac
swamps, lagoons, billabongs, tanks and rivers teem wit
bacteria, more or less fatal to man, for which stoca,
native fauna and duststorms may be blamed, r -
sonally, I can picture no existence more idealistic tn
that of the swagman or swagwoman, but unfortunately
isn’t considered fashionable or respectable ! Yew 11 1
Bohemian would only keep himself clean (no excuse
dust-baths now the drought has broken), and boil n
drinking-water, methinks there is yet hope tor i
realisation of my dream of a summer mia-mia capital
the Commonwealth, and a swag-Government running t
affairs of the country, with its feet dangling in the wa
and its eye on the worm at the end of its fishing line.

“ Petramos ” ; It is a terrible thing fo?
the residents when a bobby is not a
power in the district. Where I live,
the local blue has been led astray
by divers persons—women of incon-
sequential character who rent a house
on his beat, and men who give away
free beers. More than once this
keeper of law-’n’-order has been in
bed whilst on duty, in a manner of
speaking, and the boylets, likewise
the manlets, of the district have him
under their thumbs. The other
night some beer inside the local Sun-
day-school superintendent was striv-
ing to get itself home to bed when
the cop. appeared. He was at his
post for a wonder and arrested the
sup. and marched him station wards.
“Loo’ ’ere, thish thingsh gone too
far, ” said the drunk; but the cop.
hauled him on. “ Jush you drop it,
me man,” said the sup. without avail.
Then he resigned himself to Fate.
“Orl right, orl right,” he said,
“ what about Kitty Lonlegsh ? Oh,
yer sly ole dog. ’Ow long since yer
saw thish street at night time, my
boy? You just wait till I see the
inshpector and I’ll tell him a few little
tales.” Then the cop. pondered deeply
for a moment and absently dropped the
Sunday - school man in the muddy
gutter and deserted him. Now no one
is afraid of the consequences of getting drunk,
and even small boys break windows, and their
big brothers create disturbances with impunity.
TWO TREES.
[For Thb Bulletin ]
Beside the chain-road they do grieve and sigh,
And ever tell
The farer with the swag who passes by
Their tale so well.
Between them, where the slip-rails used to be,
A space is brown;
They speak of it—of hands they used to see
The rails let down.
Towards the ruined makeshift, once a home,
They point—afraid ;
For ghosts of little bush-babes haply roam
Beneath their shade.
Sold out, the planter left them groping there,
All ill at ease ;
The man of opulence was asked to spare
Two remnant trees.
vie. Will Jarratt.

“ H.P.” from Maoriland :—
A native died in the thereabouts of Cabbage Bay
(Auckland), and it was desired to remove what was left to
Paparoha, a few miles away. The corpse was placed on a
litter and four mounted Maoris took each a corner. They
started off at a helter-skelter pace, followed by a yell"-
ing band of mourners. One of the horses became
restive and threw his rider into the mud, and the deceased

As soon as they awake, the performance is repeated. For
many years the Government has granted natives free
passes over the railways to attend important “ tangis,”
but legislation was passed last session blocking this
privilege. Considering the nature of the “ tangi,” it is
difficult to see any reason why it should ever have been
encouraged at the taxpayers’ expense.
THAT BIRTHRATE.
TROOTER “How many children have you?"
SELECTOR : “ Well, I thought I had 17, but the last time I run 'em in off the ranges there wuz
18. The missus says one o' them Brannigan kids got boxed along with ’em.’
“Periwinkle” : “ Kerani ” (B. 17/12/’O3)
need not have bulged his eyes in amazement at the
Argus par. anent a Gippsland schoolboy getting
a swarm of bees to settle on his waistcoat. Some
people can handle bees with impunity—especially
at brooding time; others can’t! A Corop(Vio.)
young woman—Miss Spiers—has a hobby for
keeping the honey-hunters. If she hears of an
ownerless swarm of bees anywhere within
two miles of her people’s farm, she goes forth,
brushes the buzzing mass from the fence-rail, tree,
or whatever it is hanging to, with her bare hand
into her apron, and carries it home as off handedly
as if it were a brick. She has never been stung
yet. Certainly she always takes care to have tho
queen bee in her apron, but there are people who
get stung in spite of that precaution. Other
members of the Spiers family can’t go bee-ing
without being assaulted every time.

To Mildura from Melbourne by rail takes
22 hours. There is a stretch of 70 miles through
a sandy desert; the rest of the distance between
the Murray and Bendigo is an endless monotony
of mallee scrub, with scarcely any settlement
The trains in dry, windy weather have frequently
to stop, on aooount of the bearings heating through
fine sand particles getting in. The cookies,
when they want the train to stop, light fires
alongside‘the line. There are no stations, and
f ew set stopping- places. The train leaves Spencer-
‘l™ day, a week at 7 a.m., and gets to
Mildura at o a.m.

The Inky Way.
“By Electrophone,” appeared over a speech for the
first time in London papers of November 4—it was Cham-
berlain’s speech at Birmingham. The recorders, con-
nected with an ordinary telephone wire, were fixed round
the platform ; in the Daily Mail office 10 reporters sat at
10 receivers, had their “ takes ” just as if they had sat in
the hall 113 miles away, and the last “ take ” was in type
within a few minutes of the end of the speech. The Mail
gives the times: —Speech started 8.10; compositor
started on first “slip” of “copy,” 8.22; received first
batch of telegraphed “copy” (sent in case the other
experiment failed), 9.23; Chamberlain finished, 10.5;
last word set into type, 10.20 ; paper on sale, 10.32 ;
last batch of telegraphed “copy” received, 11.37.
One of the reporters wrote afterwards: “The minor
rustlings and coughings of an audience were for
the most part eliminated, but their ejaculations,
their applause, their cries, came clearly through. Most
clear of all, and as loud as though he were only a hundred
inches away instead of a hundred miles, was Chamber-
lain’s voice. . . Not the muffled, attenuated voice of
telephones, enveloped in buzzings and cracklings ; it was
a voice that broke clear across the silence like silver
against black velvet.”
* • * *
Bendigo Advertiser, the oldest of the inland dailies of
Victoria, is now something over 50 years old ; its jubilee
birthday arrived on Dec. 9th. Only three of its Southern
contemporaries are older—the Argus, Herald, and
Geelong Advertiser —and none has suffered fewer
changes of owners. “Old Bob” Haverfield and A. M.
Lloyd founded the Advertiser ; the former has been dead
these 15 or 20 years, but the other is living still. They
had no capital, and after a couple of years the thing was
tottering. Angus Mackay, then Argus correspondent on
the field, took it up along with J. J. Casey (later Judge)
and “Jimmy ” Henderson. Henderson soon dropped out,
and David Stuart took Casey’s share in 1867. All the time,
however, the firm was Mackay and Co. Angus, of that
ilk, died in July, 1886. Old Dr. Nish, of the local Scots’
Church, preached the sermon at the grave—about an hour
and half of it. For a quarter of an hour or so the prosy,
learned old cleric discussed whether the man who was
dead ought to have been called “Mackee” or “Mackeye.”
Writer took it all down, with the crown of his hat for a
writing-desk. Next year Mackay's family bought out
the Stuart interest, and the whole concern has been
unadulterated Mackay ever since.
CONTEMPTUOUS CRITICS.
[For The Bulletin.]
The bard sat at his ink—
His daily ink ;
He did a heavy think—
His daily think ;
He rose up with a curse—
His daily curse.
From grinding out a verse—
His daily verse.
And what he said was this —“!!! * ?? those flies!
They’re so familiar—and so very wise—
They come and cross my ‘ t’s ’ and dot my ‘ i*s ’! ”

* » * *
The latest outbreak in the field of newspaper enter-
prise is the London Harmsworths’ daily, produced exclu-
sively for women. This is, as yet, a virgin field, and the
experiment should be interesting. There’s little reason
why it shouldn’t be a success from the start. Feminine
interests and the feminine outlook are widening steadily
every day, and a daily wherein the latest hat wdl be
judiciously blended with divorce news and a good u.iy to
use up cold mutton should meet with healthy support
from both readers and advertisers. The new organ is
called the Daily Mirror, and is printed on fine art paper.
This latter is absolutely necessary for the reproduction
of the photo, of the nobility’s pet cat—the British female
appears to have a strange hankering to know what the
nobility’s pet cat is like.
* * * •
Ambrose Winterton, “author and journalist,” was
recently sentenced to three months’ gaol by an English
court for literary fraud. He had obtained from the
Literary World £1 11s. 6d. for an article which was
prigged from an old Academy. Prisoner was a victim of
the cocaine habit and, it was said, took 120 grains a day
of the drug. The plea of mental irresponsibility secured
for him a light sentence. It does not seem to be too well
know n in Australia —judging from efforts made to vic-
timise The Bulletin—that to send a cribbed story or
verse to a newspaper which pays for contributions is an
effort to obtain money on false pretences. For purposes
of prosecution it is not necessary that the effort should
succeed.

Pathetic explanation by fortune-buffetted genius, who
does the editing and everything else on a Maoriland
news-sheet“ If the present issue of the Chronicle falls
short of its usual standard of excellence, it is owing to
the fact that circumstances necessitated a change in the
printing staff, for which no provision was made, conse-
quently the editor has had to turn-to and produce the
paper single-handed. Having been ‘ out to grass ’ for
some years, though once a fair hand at the business, he
has had a task to bring out the issue on time for delivery
to subscribers by the usual means, and any shortcomings
noticeable will doubtless be quite overlooked now this
explanation is made."
* * • •
That inveterate “ rag planter,” Joe Ivess, has launched
yet another paper in M.L., this time at Tihape, a
struggling hamlet on the northern main trunk line.
There is a paper there already, but Joe hopes to oust it.
The number of “ public educators” the natty little man
has planted has been lost count of years ago. Whenever
a new township is born the noble Joseph canters along
with his little boxful of fixings and a leading article on
“The Progress of the District,” sets up his office and his
type, and sells out soon afterwards.
* * » •
The Delta Advocate (N.Q.) is a living proof that the
old fire of Q. journalism is not yet extinct. Thus the
Delta on its critics: —“ Some anonymous, crawling
boundeis have used < heir own paltry gutter-begotten
gauge to measure cur motives, and on Saturday night
they placarded the town with an insulting notice to the
effect that we wished to depose Mr. Elliott in order to
put another in his place. Why do not these skulking,
anonymous cowards write their opinions to this paper ?
Failing sufficient intelligence to write, why do they not
call at this office with their insult? We can assure them
every satisfaction, and the liveliest five minutes they
ever experienced in the whole course of their crawlsome,
insanitary lives.”
* * * *
.J n e Strand Maoa. for July, Arthur Hill gives an
interesting account of that famous London official organ,
the London Gazette. According to Hill, the Gazette is
the oldest and the least read paper in the British Empire;
and is at once the smallest and largest paper, changing
from one sheet to a hundred as pressure of news demands.
It is the only paper whose word is law and whose
authority is accepted in the witness-box ; besides which
}? onl y paper in which certain persons are com-
pelled to advertise, and in which certain other persons,
if they wanted to, couldn’t advertise for love or money.
It yields a profit of £20,000 a year to the Government.
* * * *
Wellington (ALL.) a late-hour club has been
fashioned. Its membership mostly comprises young
pressmen, who assemble weekly (Sunday night) in an
oyster saloon, and discuss great questions -over a bunch
of watercress and some spring onions.
• * * *
Jack left the University with honors, and some of his
original brains. He tried teaching, coaching young
asses to be B.A.’s. Then he had temporary clerkships in
the civil service. After that he tried tea, and failed.
He’d been trying literature all the time, and he got
a letter of introduction to the editor of the oldest,
heaviest, and most respectable daily in Australia, whose
rule was to print nothing which might clash with the
advertising columns. It struck Jack that it would be the
easiest thing in the world to write nothing in leading
articles; but he found out. The heaviest paper also
desired only men with a University training. Jack had
that all right, and a letter of introduction as well, so the
editor decided to give him a show. He worked hard, but
his first two or three attempts were unsuccessful. Then
the Commonwealth loomed, and Jack sat up all night
and wrote an article on Federation. His ambition now—
his determination—was to write at least one leading
article for the great solid paper. He took the article
round to the editor himself. It ran like this ;
“ We believe we may safely affirm that all right-think-
iD£ Australians realise the fact that the willingness
evinced by the several peoples of the Australian colonies
to federate and become a single Commonwealth does not
arise from a wish to become less English, but from a
healthy desire to become, if possible, more Australian ;
in fact,’’ &c., &c.
The editor was pleased to see that Jack was improving.
He might possibly be able to use the article as a sub-
leader. It would require a little toning-down, but Jack
could safely leave that in his hands. Thus encouraged,
Jack took a rest, and, later on, tried again :
“ It is now four months, three weeks, and two days
since the outbreak of the war in South Africa ; and even
the most loyal and patriotic must admit that the progress
made by our armies up to date has not nearly realised
the sanguine expectations so freely expressed at the
commencement of hostilities ”
The editor wouldn’t have it at any price. It was re-
actionary. But Jack had grit, and, after awhile, he had
another shot at it:
“ As foreshadowed in a recent issue, everything seems
to point to one of two things—either a speedy termina-
tion of the war on account of the seemingly complete
demoralisation of the Boer forces opposed to General
Roberts, or a struggle to the bitter end prolonged into
months of irritating guerilla warfare. How far we are in
the right, time and the course of events alone can prove.
We leave it to our readers to ” etc.
That pleased the editor. He liked the style. Jack’s
work was beginning to show marked improvement, and
possibly, in the near future, the editor would be in a
position to offer him a place on the regular staff. But
Jack had to chuck it up. He said the strain was too
great—that he’d sooner say things for half-a-guinea a
column than say nothing for two. He’s writing now for
some democratic rag.
• • • *
What editor, holding sway in the rural realms of
inkydom, has not had frequent visits from the itinerant
inkslinger on the wallaby? A good-looking man gener-
ally, the very embodiment of the shabby genteel. Where
heslept last night is not his tale to tell, for he is on trade
intent. “A little effort that you might use,” he says.
An upward glance from the exchanges discloses the old
campaigner of last year to the local inkman’s gaze. He
takes the crumpled manuscript, traced with lead pencil
hieroglyphics, passes on a shilling, and the scribe of the
hoof-tracks touches his bald pate, bows, and seeks the
long tanglefoot with froth thick thereon 1 Rarely,
indeed, do the wandering one’s scribbles see the light of
type ; they are mostly old scraps, based on some par or
illustration noticed on a visit to the local library,
incoherent for the most part, uninteresting, and out
of date. Get the old itinerant’s tongue to wag, and you
will have yarns from the book of life, tales about his
salad days, his hopes and ambitions, his loves and losses,
his escapades, his triumphs, but never about his down-
fall. Generally there are some traces of refinement in
his character when it is looked at sideways in a strong
light. His clothes are usually the last possibility in the
way of greenery-yellowy habiliments. His hat looks
like a recently bombarded fort; he knows scraps of
Latin, and can often drink in several dead languages ;
and his habit of writing little smudgy pencil contri-
butions on venerable scraps of paper is quite incurable.
* • •
When Donald M’Donald contemplated leaving the
oountry for the metropolitan press, he first cast anxious
eyes on the Age. Chief of staff Martin at once summed
him upas a promising young man, and told David Syme
so. After an interview with the boss of the Age, David
delivered himself thus to Martin : “ I see no extraor-
dinary capacity aboot that young man. He may be
suitable for reporting ploughing matches or agricultural
shows, with which he has a good knowledge as an ama-
teur. But I couldna’ think of placing him on the
reeporting staff.” So Donald had to abandon the Age.
It was not long before he got a footing on the Argus, and
subsequent events have proved that David’s judgment
was not infallible.
THE “SUB."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The “sub.” sits in the office when
My manuscript walks in ;
He feels it with a frosty hand
And then he scrapes his chin.
He cuts it here, he prunes it there,
He boils the story down,
Until the blessed thing hangs like
Hog bristles from a crown.
I've loafed up in Darjeeling ; I
Have prowled around Maekay ;
I’ve seen the red pagodas lean
Towards old Mandalay.
The peacock-thrones, the tiger-room
Where Theebaw laughed and died,
dome fresh to mind as when we sailed
The Irawadi’s tide.
I’ve heard the gun-boats barking far
Beyond the China seas,
When junk-men faced the music in
Their quilt-like hyponjees.
I’ve met the razor-blast that blew
Out North on Labrador ;
It wasn’t half so cutting as
That small sub-editor.
There is no power in all the air,
No terror in the sky,
To match the zeal for boiling down
That boileth in his eye.
The Ganges thug-man in his pride,
His black malevolence,
Is not so gory as the sub-
Editor’s impudence.
N.S.W.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
THE WAILUG BARD!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’d rather have a single batd who’s full of fight and ale
Than half-a-score of singers white who’re always on the
wail ;
I like a bull-dog minstrel who can sing a song of Grit,
I’d sink your mis’ry-grinding bards to grind within the
Pit.
For nearly all this whining comes of courage waxen pale—
(Did Homer write a puling verse on heroes stout and
hale ?)
The world has weary cares enough to weigh on heart and
head,
Without the woes of sundry bards who ought to weep in
bed.
We all have made our blunders and we all must pay the
cost,
But only he whose pluck is gone is ever really lost,
There’s always room for fighters—in the world there’s
always war,
And your past can seldom sink you if you’ve courage at
the core.
Whatever will the women do—if men commence to cry ?
A man was meant to fight and live, or else to fight and
die ;
A singing man was meant to sing (and Song is yet divine)
But genuine song can never ring in poor degen’rate whine.
So keep your misery bottled up, and hold your wailing
down,
For scores have troubles worse than yours in ev’ry paltry
town ;
But, if your heart must weep or burst, then let it weep
alone,
And don’t get on the house-tops when you want to make
a moan.
I’d rather have a single bard who’s full of fight and ale
Than half-a-score of singers white who’re always on the
wail;
I like a bull-dog minstrel who can sing a song of Grit,
I’d sink your mis’ry-grinding bards to grind within the
Pit.

There is no doubt that if some beneficent being, witli the power to grant our requests, were to
ask U 8 to express our dearest wish and promise that it should ho realised, nine out of every ten of us
would, without hesitation, elect to become millionaires. The other one-tenth, too, would I e sorely
tempted to make a like choice, but would, probably, after severe mental conflict, conclude that a
million would be of little use without the capacity to enjoy the pleasures it would procure, and would
consequently decide that freedom from paid was the greatest boon they could ask. To tho’nine tomlis
nothing can be promised, but it is within the power of a large proportion of the remaining tenth to
realise their desire, the sufferings of most of them being caused bv
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders
Anaemia
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice

Bladder Troubles

Sick Headache
and a radical euro in any of these cases can speedily be assured. One and all of the disorders specified
are but the effect of the failure of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
to efficiently perform their functions. These important organs, when acting healthily, deal with,
and remove from the system, the poisons which inflict the pain and sickness.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition
of the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust-like sediment, or if the
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time
must be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some iess serious but
more painful illness will result.
The Liver. —ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or throe
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood,
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion,
Biliousness, Anssmia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys and
Liver was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, .these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many disappointments, the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length discovered. Warner's Safe
Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and, by restoring their activity, theso vital
organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poison*
the presence of which, in the system are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia. Lumbago, Back-
ache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply
by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures affected by Warner’s Safe (Jure
are permanent cures.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The three “ great ” Brisbane dailies man-
aged to pick one winner in twelve tries for the
Queensland Federal elections.
Victorian Assembly negatived, by 50 to
27, a motion by M'Cutcheon, M.L.A., to insert a
clause in the Electoral Expenses Limitation Bill
providing for compulsory voting.
After all the Age's jeers at the “ Comic
Four ” nominated by the Aegds, the Age’s serious
four averaged about 78,000 votes apiece and
the “Comic Four” of the Argus about 77,500
votes apiece.
An Australian writing from Birmingham
(Eng.) says that Freetrade in England is as dead
as Julius Caesar. Chamberlain didn’t kill it, but,
having a longer nose than most Englishmen, was
the first to discover its death.
“0.L.” tells of the quaint lie that won
The Macleay (N.S. W.) to Reidism :
Here the_ farmers voted against Protection, as they
consider it is only a matter of time when their corn will
be stopped from going to Sydney if the Deakin Ministry
remains in power.
The spectacle of Minister Bent and
Premier Irvine barking at each other in the Vic.
Assembly was presented to the astonished gaze of
Kyabram last week. This visible disorganisation
inspired even the crushed Peacock with valor, and
he struck the table with a book.
“Kalgoorlie: Frazer (L.), 5782; Kirwan
(O.), 2774.” And yet three years ago Ferguson,
then of the N.S.W. Labor party, was invited over
by the goldfields Laborites, went, had a look
round, declared that with Kirwan’s press influence
the task was hopeless, and came East again.
A disappointed champion of the alleged
“Victorian Four” strove to repair his broken
heart by inserting this advertisement in the
“ Missing Friends” of the Argus:
TjtAREWELL to prosperity in Australia, through
result of one. man one vote capped by female
suffrage.
The difference in the calibre of the Federal
and the State Parliaments is telling. The Mayor
of the city of Bendigo announced, the other night,
his conversion to the idea of wholly abolishing
the State Parliaments, and dividing their work
between the Federal Parliament and local-govern-
ing bodies.
A correspondent writes picturesquely on
a large subject:—
When that pitiable young man, Posterity, gets born,
he will probably use some very vigorous and uncompli-
mentary language about his conscienceless forefathers.
In fact, life for him will consist of one long pro-
cession of blasphemies and revilings,
as between swears he perspir-
ingly shifts his vast
debt - burden from
one jaded shoul-
der to the other.
Each morning
when he
awakes, after
a few hours of
uneasy sleep,
the vast pile
of indebted,
ness reposing
on his suffer-
ing chest will
cause him to
express a fer-
vent hope that
his ancestors
are frying in
the special,
railed-0ff,2000-
degree section
of Toph e t;
and, as he la-
boriously hoists
the load over
his spine and
starts out for the
day’s graft, his re-
marks will in all
likelihood be violent.
One usually pictures
Posterity as a patient,
Hindu - like person
staggering along the
dusty Nation-track with
his head down, but he may not be that sort of person
at all. He may be a kicker; and if he is, what will
happen—besides the language? I think I know what
he would do if the gift of pre-natal vision could be
vouchsafed Posterity; be would refrain from getting
born at all. And no sane person could blame him for
refraining.
The nearest approach to black-labor
divisions in Queensland are not in the central or
north country, but in the south—Moreton and
Oxley. Moreton was the only division in which
a colored-labor Senate candidate actually led.
North and Central Queensland, which know, were
solid white.
Tom Bent is credited with one wise idea.
He threatens a 10s. gun tax, which should have a
tendency to preserve human life from the depreda-
tions of the holiday young man whose gun goes off
when least expected. With an exemption clause
in favor of professional vermin-destroyers the gun
tax should be a blessing.
Bruce Smith polled the largest majority
fora N.S.W. seat in the House of Reps., his
plurality being 11,711 votes. Last election this
honor belonged to F. E. M‘Lean, with 5648
majority. The “ way he has with the women ”is
said to account for the lofty position of the tire-
some and mostly absent Smith.
During the up-north election excitement
the Queensland Laborites had cards printed which
they hung out at their opponents’ meetings, re-
questing a good hearing for the speaker. The
result was a shocking disappointment to some
papers and Oppositionists, who couldn’t get hold
of any disorder over which to work up some
badly-needed sympathy.
At the fag end of the late lengthy session
in Maoriland all big measures were pushed
through when a dog-tired House felt its brain
whirling round through sheer weariness. Amidst
the hurly-burly, Maoriland awoke one morning
to find it had voted £15,000 towards the erection
of a statue in London to the late Queen Victoria,
and it can’t remember how the thing happened.
Signs and portents of the Victorian
general elections : Irvine has resumed practice at
the Bar; Parliament has appointed a Board to
fix wages in the dressmaking trade; and the
Government holds out the offer to some of the
dismissed railway strikers that they may come
back. With the indications supplied by the
Federal poll, Irvine sees the necessity of hedging
on his anti-labor attitude.
The National Ass overreached itself in
S.A. It it had let Batchelor and Hutchison go
in unopposed for the city districts it would proba-
bly have got one of its own candidates in for the
Senate, but the contested elections in the
metropolis brought to the poll thousands of
Liberal voters who might otherwise not have
attended, and, being there, they voted for
M‘Gregor, Guthrie, and Story.
The result of the Queensland section of
the Federal elections should finally settle the
Black Labor party. The White Australia policy
was supposed to have been “sneaked in ” at the
first election, and the anger of Queensland, deprived
of its beloved Kanaka, was to give Reid a solid
following in that State. And the anti-kanaka
party wins every seat but one, polling by
electorates, and all three seats in the Senate !
Apropos Taylor, the negro cyclist who
goes round giving his “testimony,” some of the
parsons are making fools of themselves again.
One of them, the Rev. Gainford, preaching what
the D.T. calls “an interesting sermon,” said,
after repeating the daily paper lie and slander
regarding the Petriana crew—“ Our politicians
judge men by the color of their skin, while Christ
judged them by their hearts.” And yet, when
the Australian Government does judge men by
their “ hearts ” (the Rev. Gainford means charac-
ter, no doubt), and when in consequence it shuts
out a criminal and a thief, there is a howl from
all the Gainfords of the States. The poor blind
Gainfords can’t see that their alleged Christianity
is being turned into a catspaw by office-hungry
politicians and a dishonest press.

W. Sandford, defeated by Pumpkin Smith
for the Macquarie seat in the House of Reps.,
made a Federal iron bonus a leading plank in his
platform. After the election Carcoar Progress
Association expressed its opinion of the situation
in the following resolution :
That, as the result of the recent Federal elections has
proved that the working classes of Carcoar, with a few
exceptions, are opposed to the establishment of the iron
industry in their midst—an industry that would have the
effect of giving permanent employment—and as the
association, which exists almost solely for the purpose of
agitating for the benefit of the working classes, strongly
favors the establishment of the industry, the members of
the association, seeing their ideas are not in sympathy
with the class they desire to assist, decide to immediately
disband, and thus give the so-called workers an oppor-
tunity of finding work for themselves—provided they
want work, which this association doubts very much.
