W. M‘Millan at an educational break-up :
So convinced was he of the necessity for the strictest
discipline that he was of opinion that it would be a good
thing if every boy were compelled to go through a period
of military training.
Much, no doubt, can be said for the military
training of youth, but so many tailors and male
milliners play moustached warrior that it is hard
to persuade the public to take the subject
seriously.
Probably the most solid Protectionist vote
cast in N.S. W. on Dec. 16 was at the little agri-
cultural centre of Shark Creek, on the Clarence
River. Sixty-seven votes were polled there, and
every one was cast for the Protectionist Reps,
candidate, Frank Clarke. For the Senate, the
whole 67 were also cast for Protectionists Collins
and Griffith, and 64 for the other Protectionist,
Cunneen, while Henry Fletcher received the odd
three.
“ L.W.” : Some fool of a parson has
recently been asking with a snuffle would Christ
be in favor of a White Australia. This insane
habit of asking what Christ would do under
certain circumstances will yet land the parsons in
difficulties. Some day a Laborite will arise and
ask what would have happened to Christ under
Irvine’s Strike Bill had He attempted to deliver
the Sermon on the Mount while the railway strike
was on ?
During the “ increased grain freights ”
discussion on the Victorian estimates, nearly every
Tory in the Assembly, every blatant opponent of
socialistic legislation, every railer at Government
interference with private enterprise, got on his
legs and bleated about the necessity for assisting
the poor farmer. The wildest barracker for these
socialistic proposals was M ‘Ketchup, of St. Kilda,
absolutely the most hopeless Tory in the whole
House.
If there is one candidate in whose case
profanity is justified it is J. M. Chanter, who, in
a poll of nearly 9000 votes (not counting in-
formal), lost by jive —unless a recount upsets the
arrangement. All the local Tory interests rallied
round big landlord Blackwood, who has some
81,000 sheep and got about 4500 votes—nearly one
vote for every 20 sheep. The six men who were
too tired to vote for Chanter have a great deal to
answer for.
“ B.” writes :
The average virtuous Victorian has been reading the
accounts of blackguardism in the N.S.W. Assembly with
feelings of disgust and spasms of righteousness. Over
here we reckon we’ve only one politician who’d have a
chance in the Macquarie-street bear-garden, and that is
the cabbage-grower, Tommy of the Train. On purely
aesthetic grounds, we’re ready to part with Bent to
N.S.W., as it seems a pity that the Parliament of Owe’-
Sullivan, Crick, Norton, and other shining specimens
should be incomplete. And when we sell Bent, Vic. will
be able to pay for a few of those disabled engines.
Extract from a letter by George Pile, in
the S.M. Herald :
One would have thought that by this time the lessons
of the past would have been read by our people, and that
they, realising the effect of socialistic legislation, would
have been guided to have returned business men, men
with business knowledge, to the Federal Parliament at
the late election.
There is something magnificent about the confi-
dence of house-agents and the like that they alone
are competent to manage the affairs of a nation.
That overwhelming meeting of indignant
secessionists who, in the words of John Haynes,
are to give utterance to the “sweeping triumphant
voice” of N.S.W. for a bursting up of the union,
will be held in the School of Arts Hall, Sydney.
Said hall has about space enough to allow of the
swinging of a very moderate kitten, so apparently
: John’s hopes aren’t built on the same scale as his
yell. W hen a man calls on the indignant millions
to gather in a dog-kennel and assert their rights
he makes the ungodly laugh.
George Pile, the one-time owner of George
Pile’s dog, has been writing to the S.M. Herald
to tell the old story about the English capitalist
who was just going to invest £lOO,OOO in iron-
works here, but was scared off by “socialistic
legislation.” As usual, no name is given—the
name of the tattered person who declined to
invest £lOO,OOO here by reason of our socialistic
legislation never is given. The Bulletin, for its
own part, doesn’t believe this wild, weird person
had a copper to invest ; it doesn’t even believe
that there ever was such a person. If he existed
there would be some trace somewhere of the
£lOO,OOO works he established before we began to
have Labor parties and socialistic legislation, and
even if George Pile had 25 dogs he couldn’t find
those great industries.
SAVING HIS FACE.
“ He writes that I am a cad."
“ Tell him you will pull his nose."
“I will — where’s your telephone?”
When the Election Expenses Limitation
Bill was before Victorian Assembly, it was
amusing to see how the men of wealth fought for
freedom to bribe electors and buy votes for their
party. Fairbairn, M'Cuteheon, and the wealthy
retired baker, “Dave” Hennessy, tried all sorts
of dodges to get the Bill wiped out in the
Assembly. Men who have no other legislative
qualification than the possession of money are
naturally shocked to think that they will not be
able to use that solitary feature of their characters.
Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords is.
more a caucus than a legislative chamber. When
an important measure is coming before it, the
Landlords first meet informally, with no Hansard,
no reporters, and no audience, and decide what
they will do. Then they meet formally and carry
out their intentions, but the reasons, arguments,
compromises and deliberations are kept secret!
As these caucus proceedings constitute the real
business, the House of Landlords, on important
occasions, is really a secret and unreported
gathering.
Dr. Quick had a desperate fight for his
Bendigo seat with the Labor candidate, and
wouldn’t have won but for the break-away of some
of the Miners’ Association leaders from the Trades
Hall. Praed, general secretary of the Association
in Victoria, and other officials, worked hard for
him. Quick, who has never forgotten that he
began life as a mine-hand, has been the Associ-
ation’s lawyer for years, and often has forgotten
to send in the bill. Nevertheless relations between
the Miners’ Association office and the Trades
Hall are rather more strained than ever.
Provision embodied in the new Victorian
Elections Act:
Makes it an illegal practice for a paper to publish any
false or defamatory statement regarding the personal
character or conduct of any candidate, and renders it
possible for an aggrieved party to obtain a perpetual in-
junction from a judge of the Supreme Court to restrain
further publication of such statement.
Press slanders have been so unscrupulously used
lately to influence elections that a muzzling law
is inevitable. The newspapers may shriek about
the “freedom of the press,” and soon, but they
have brought the restriction on themselves.
Cabled that—
The British Admiralty is prepared for every eventuality.
No British warship is en route to the Far East, but it is
understood that Admiral Cyprian Bridge (the Oom-
mander-in-Chief of the China station), Rear-Admiral G.
L. Atkinson-Willes (the Commander-in-Chief of the East
Indies squadron), and possibly Vice-Admiral A. D. Fan-
shawe (the Commander-in-Chief of the Australian
station) are in possession of secret orders.
Presumably Admiral Fanshawe’s “secret orders ”
are to take the Australian squadron away North
in case of “eventualities,” so the Commonwealth
may possibly see the heels of the ships it is pay-
ing for a little sooner than it expected.
Some remarks by Bowles, the Freetrade
candidate at Newcastle (N.S.W.):
A committee was formed after the meeting to further
the interests of the Freetrade and Liberal party. Mr.
Bowles intimated that he had been accepted as the
party’s candidate, and chat probably other members of
the party would speak in the district in his support. He
had been promised a very strong vote already, especially
of ladies. He was president of the Christian Endeavor
Union, which had 1700 members, most of whom were
electors, and he had been promised their vote. The church
and temperance vote, he thought, would also be his, and
he had been promised the vote of the A.P.D.A.
And even with four separate holy snuffles to vote
for him he didn’t get in.
“ Demos ” from Melbourne :
I have not often been more profoundly moved than by
the meeting in the Melbourne Trades Hall on Friday
night, when the result of the elections was known.
Women formed about two-thirds of the crowded
assemblage. There was a Eureka Stockade feel about it,
electricity in the air, flesh and blood politics. The busts
of Higinbotham and Wilson Gray looked down placidly
from each side of the chairman. The grandeur of the
affair was in its sentiment being Australian, not Pro-
vincial. Victorian Labor has not gained in the Senate,
according to Trades Hall ideas, for that party put in
Barrett last time. However, there is Trenwith instead of
Invoice Reid, surely a big gain, although the Trades Hall
denounces Trenny as a “ treacherous political bounder."
The Trades Hall enthusiasm was over the advance made
by other States.
Colonel Templeton, the Argus Senatorial
candidate, got a nasty jar at Warragul (Vic.),
where he was explaining that white men couldn’t
possibly work in the stoke-hole of a mail boat
coming through the Red Sea. An indignant
elector rose up abd hailed the liquidating colonel
with: “ You know as much about that as about
politics generally. I have stoked on the Orient
line for three years, and here’s my discharge (pro-
ducing it). It’s all rot what you say. I can do as
much as three black men ; but 1 want more wages
than half a dozen. That’s the whole trouble.
And a purely theoretical colonel, who never stoked
in his life, could only change the subject hurriedly.

HEALTH
And How To Obtain It.
TUBERCULOSIS
OF TUB
BOWELS
CURED BY
VITADATIO
Canterbury Road, Mount Pleasant,
Tunstall, Victoria,
June 1, 1901.
MR. PALMER,
Dear Sir,—Last November I felt very unwell and had to
take to my bed. 1 called in a doctor, he examined me,
and said that I was suffering; from Tuberculosis of the
Bowels. He prescribed for me, and not feeling better I
decided to call in another who examined me also and said
the same thing. He also prescribed for me, and still
feeling no better I was strongly advised to take VITA-
DATIO, and after taking it for a short time I began to
improve, and eventually regained my long lost health.
I was three months in bed and suffered a great deal of
pain, and had it not been for VITADATIO I believe I
would have died ; but to-day I am thankful to say I am
in good health, and have gained a stone in weight, and
can honestly recommend to any person suffering as I did.
Yours faithfully,
ELIZABETH DENNY.
We certify that the above is correct.

From Allison’s History :
No nation can pretend to independence which rests for
any sensible portion of its subsistence in ordinary seasons
on foreign (who may become hostile) nations. And if we
would see a memorable example of the manner in which
the greatest and most powerful nation may come to be
paralysed by this cause, we have only to cast our eyes on
Imperial Rome, when the vast extent of the empire had
practically free trade in grain with the whole civilised
world, and the result was that cultivation disappeared
from the Italian plains ; the race of Roman agriculturists,
the strength of the empire, became extinct; the legion
could be no longer recruited, but from foreign lands;
vast tracts of pasturage overspread even the fields of
Lombardy and the Campagna of Naples ; and it was the
plaintive confession of the Roman annalist that the Mis-
tress of the World had come to depend for her subsist-
ence on the floods of the Nile.

T)URSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division of
the High Court of .Justice made on the 10th day of
August 1903 “In the Matter of the Estate of Caroline
Hicks deceased ” and in an Action between Elizabeth Isabel
Hicks (Spinster) Plaintiff and Alice Noakes Dengate
(Married Woman) Defendant 1903 11.2196 the following
Inquiry was (inter alia) directed namely (1) “ An Inquiry
who were the PERSONS ENTITLED by virtue of or
according to the Statute of Distribution to the ESTATE
of CAROLINE HICKS the Intestate in the Originating
Summons mentioned living at the time of her death and
whether any of them are since dead and if so who are
their respective legal personal representatives.” The
said Caroline Hicks resided at the time of her death
which happened on or about the Ist day of May 1898 at
No. 32 Park Road St. Leonards on Sea in the County of
Sussex and was one of the daughters of Richard Hicks
and Philadelphia his wife formerly Philadelphia
Richardson.
Now all persons claiming under such Inquiry (1) are by
their Solicitors on or before the 10th day of March 1904
to come in and prove their claims at the Chambers of
Mr. Justice Farwell and Mr. Justice Swinfen Eady at the
Royal Courts of Justice Strand London or in default
thereof they will he peremptorily excluded from the
benefit of the said Order.
Tuesday the 15th day of March 1904 at one o’clock in
the afternoon at the said Chambers is appointed for
hearing and adjudicating upon the said claims.
Dated this 18th day of November 1903.

A S’African paper tells this :
The other day, a young fellow found employment, after
seeking it in vain for weeks. He had excellent testi-
monials, and his appearance favorably impressed the
merchant who engaged him. On presenting himself
next morning to enter upon his duties, he was called by
the principal of the firm into his office and asked what
part of the world he came from. Innocently enough, the
young man replied “ Australia,” and was astonished at
the change that came over the merchant’s face. “ You
should have told me that before,” he said, sternly. I '' e
no ■ use for you.” After which brutal speech he
waved the disappointed youth out of the office.
What we fought for !

“ Bunny ” : Went into an out- back school
t’other day. Saw a class of kiddies (most of the
poor little beggars have never been within 300
miles of the ocean) learning geography with sand-
trays, claypans, basins of water, rocks, pebbles,
sketch-books and colored pencils. They simply
revelled in the lesson. Kindergarten’s a fool to
it. (Perhaps this is the true Kindergarten !) In
theold days so much was jammed and crammed into
them, so many lines, so many pages of “ jogafree,”
&c., that pupils (poor, persecuted pupils) became
marvellous memory machines—or physical wrecks.
They could recite yards and yards of damnable
dry definitions, but they did not know what they
were jabbering .about. Now the kiddies say less,
but do more. These bush-bairns can build you an
island, a lake, a cape, a mountain, a valley, a
desert, &c.—or make a very intelligent sketch of
divers colors on paper. Bless the new style.

Volapuk is, and has been for some time,
apparently dead, but a new universal language,
bearing the not very convincing title of Esperanto,
has sprung up to take its place. It is claimed for
it that anybody, literate or illiterate, cabman or
university graduate, of any nationality, European,
Chinaman, African nigger, or American Indian,
can learn to read, speak, and write it within a
month, while the grammar can be acquired in one
hour. It already possesses a library of some 200
volumes, including translations of “Hamlet,”
Byron s “ Cain,” and the “ Iliad,” besides many
scientific works, the latest edition being a com-
mentary on the fifth book of Euclid. Max
Muller and Tolstoy swear by it, and John Charles
O Connor, in T.P.’s weekly, devotes a page to its
praise, but he does not tell whence it comes.
“W.J.A.”: The versatility of the out-of-
work Australian is remarkable. I met a man in
Queensland whose bona-fide record runs as fol-
lows,_ his occupations being shown in order:—
Tourist, cane-cutter, timber-getter, gentleman,
groom, billiard-marker, typhoid nurse, under-
taker, Sunday-school teacher, bullock driver,
lodging-house keeper, bookmaker, engineer on
river steamer, aeronaut, hospital patient. Some
of his notes:—Hardest graft, cane-cutting;
easiest, bookmaking ; best-paying, boarding-house
keeping ; least profitable, Sunday-school teaching.
His longest engagement was as tourist (with
swag); shortest, aeronaut. (He forgot to
put water in the engine-boiler, and it
was all over in 10 seconds). He kept
boarders (cane-cutters) at Bundaberg, and
cooked everything in one utensil—a frying pan.
He first made tea in it, then cooked the chops,
and afterwards washed up in it. Being thrifty,
he, in the last days of the business, burnt every
fixture, partition and ceiling in the house for
firewood. The boarders left before he had time
to use up the flooring. When he departed he
politely returned the key of the remainder to
the landlady. She thanked him, and gave him
tea and cake. “So few,” she said, “paid up the
rent and brought back the key.” Hard times
drove him to Sunday-school teaching, and an
offer to escort a team of oxen to Hughenden
caused him to slide from grace. Being broken up
and unable to work, he is now in a Government
billet in Kanakaland.
Extract from a Fatman’s diary :
When we moved into the new house in Uppercrust
Grove—built out of the money saved since that ruffian
Kingston retired from the Customs—Birdie and I decided
that Jobson, the insolent groom, who wanted his wages
increased by 25., should be dismissed. As if 12s. 6d. and
keep—excellent keep, too, even if the servants do com-
plain about the kitchen butter and tea—wasn’t enough
for any working man. I’ve heard of a boy whose mother
was poor, so I got him for 2s. 6d. Of course, this was an
exorbitant salary for a mere boy, who eats such
a lot, but my generosity very often gets the
best of me. He had nothing to do but keep
the yard tidy, look after the horses, clean the buggy
and brougham, polish the knives and boots, work in the
garden, feed the fowls, attend to the dogs, and do a few
odd jobs about in his spare time. He’s a lucky boy who
gets a nice easy place like ours. But some are never
satisfied and don’t appreciate a good situation. This one
turned out to be lazy—so lazy I’m surprised his conscience
didn’t prick him when. Mrs. Smithers paid him on
Saturdays. He never got up before 6 o’clock—such sloth
I detest. If it was not that my arduous dutiesas sole pro-
prietor of a warehouse required more than the ordinary
rest I’d be up at daylight every morning. I like it, and it’s
a habit I am particularly anxious to encourage in my ser-
vants. Hence when Birdie informed me that Adolphuswas
not an early riser, I was much annoyed, and determined
to put him on the right track. Next morning I set the
alarm for 4.30. It certainly was trying to have to get up
after my hard work of the day before, but my dutv was
clear. I robed myself in the dressing-gown Birdie bought
me last Christmas, and, descending to the servants’
quarters, called Adolphus. I continued in the same way
for a week. Judge of my horror then, when Birdie was
informed by the housemaid that Adolphus had confided
to her that when he’d made sure I had gone back
to bed he simply turned over and slept another hour !
My moral sense has not received so great a shock since I
was forced to go to court and herd with common drunks
over a slight error of my shipping clerk. But my mind
was soon made up. I would administer a lesson not to
\>e forgotten. Accordingly, after calling Adolphus next
morning, I pretended to retire as usual, but, removing
my slippers, stole noiselessly back to his room, stealthily
entered, and removing the bedclothes with my left hand,
administered three hast, sound smacks with the other.
It certainly was an unfortunate mistake that I had
entered the wrong room. Nothing would console her,
and Birdie says the girl will tell all her friends and we’ll
have great difficulty in getting another housemaid. But
I comfort myself that my intentions were of the best.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Moat owners of starters in A. J.C. Summer Cup,
Saturday, had big lumps of the sweep laid them, and, as
a consequence, there were no eliminations; every oneof
16 carded stripped. Long Tom was most wanted, despite
his 61b. penalty, and, though others were freely backed,
he hardened to 5 to 2 before flag fall, while “ no money "
was Ikey’s response in many instances to inquiries for
the favorite. Bridegroom, T.M.S., Tatterdemalion, Little
Mary, and Fanfare were others weightily invested upon.
Donovan showed the road for some time, then Leader
(late Autocrat) joined him, and that pair led into the
straight, where they tired palpably. Cyanide and Long
Tom dashed to the front followed by Queenslander Ivan,
who galloped on Cyanide’s heels and came down, shoot-
ing mannikin Cornwell out of the saddle. Inside
the half - distance Bridegroom was level with the
favorite and a high-tension tussle resulted in Bride-
groom scoring by a neck amidst intense excitement and
mainly through Long Tom’s rider (Barden) easing a bit,
evidently under the impression he owned the race.
Fanfare was third. Hoisting of Bridegroom’s number
was the signal for a scene hitherto without parallel at
Randwick. Air was thick with hoots, groans and jeers
at the judge till long after the “ weight ” flag went up
and the noses had paid out. Some of the throng foamed
at the mouth. Big majority of people in the grandstand
and reserve thought Long Tom had surely won, but
those in members’ section (better placed) had no doubt
about the result, and wondered at the demonstration.
Time (2.52) is the slowest since the journey was increased
to lm. 6fur., and lsec. worse than Aurantia’s win last
year. -'
• * *
Winner (Bridegroom) of Summer Cup was bred
in Queensland, and is by King Olaf (son of Splendor) from
Tooloona, by First Lord (son of Yattendon) from Miss
Beau, by Clarion (son of Kelpie) from Lurline, by The
Premier. Though he won several times in Queensland,
Bridegroom’s previous best performance in N.S.W. was
annexing Hawkesbury Spring Handicap September last.
Subsequent to A.J.C. Spring meet astute Randwick
trainer, Ike Earnshaw, bought Bridegroom, and now
finds the spec, a more than profitable one. Since hia
comparatively poor showing in A.J.C. Salisbury Stakes
last month Bridegroom has improved remarkably.
* * * *
Air Motor was said to be going straightway to
the stud, but her owner gave the mare another chance,
with the result that, Saturday week, she annexed A.J.C.
Grand Stand Stakes (w.f.a.), and Saturday last appro-
priated Villiers Stakes (1 mile) in 1.39) (the fastest time
ever put up for the event). Air Motor carried top weight
(9.7), and beat Kingljck’s (last year) figures by )sec., with
51b. more in the saddle. She was bred at Kirkham by
Mr. H. MacKellar, and is by imported Ayr Laddie from
Goldsbrough mare Windmill, dam of Treadmill—M.L.’s
high-class performer, which ran second to Waikiki in
Auckland Cup on Saturday.
* * • •
Every one of six starters in A.J.C. December
Stakes (Saturday) was a maiden. Yitula faced the
barrier 6 to 1 on, and won easily. Provided he winters
well, Vitula should take a lot of beating in next season’s
young races.
* • • •
Biggest surprise of A.J.C. Summer Meeting on
Saturday was Transvaal’s remarkably easy manner of
winning the Hurdles. Stable connections were evidently
very confident, for nag was solidly backed and sent out
first favorite in slightly better request than Wollogorang
and Roodoch. Transvaal’s display of foot and jumping
was distinctly good, and in strong contrast to prior exhi-
bitions. Such a well-bottled-up good thing hasn’t been
seen over the sticks for some time.
• * * *
A.J.C. Nursery Hep., Saturday, was respon-
sible for downfall of a hot-pot in Lochiel filly Mangosteen,
who went out at 6 to 4 on, but finished second to Jim
Dorset (5 to 2 against), who was on 91bs. better terms
than when beaten by the filly the week before. J.D.
wore the filly down from the distance.
* * * •
Of 79 nags nominated for Carrington Stakes at
Randwick, Friday, this week, 23 are left in, and Pendant,
Sarto, and Idolise should have the winner amongst them.
Tatt.’s Cup (following day) 33 entrants have been reduced
to 17, of whom Bridegroom and Fanfare (on form so far
shown) look the pick.
• * * *
Praleon, who won at Randwick, t’other day,
starting an outsider, cost owner (Agar Wynne) £lOOO as a
yearling, mainly through her relationship to Grasspan
and Brakpan, but never finished first past the post till
occasion referred to. Possibly she’ll mend her ways now,
but it’ll take more than a race or two to get back any-
thing like a fair proportion of the coin lost on her.
• * * *
‘ * Marat ” : A Hindu put a pocketful of money
on The Hawker in the Juvenile Stakes, at Canterbury
Park, the other Saturday. When Bon Soir came in on his
own, Mustapha Khan went into a corner behind the stand
and prayed hard for the rest of the afternoon. Now and
again he stopped, to ask bystanders if The Hawker was
really beaten, or had his eyes deceived him. When they
assured him that his eyes were all right, he would have a
fresh outburst of prayer and lamentation. I think, by the
confident way he planked his money, one of his gods must
have given him the shapeless and unreliable griffin.
* » * ♦
The Sydney ex-politician who has raced of late
with such secondary success as “Mr. Marvel” is said to
be thinking seriously of giving the game best.
* * » *
Final forfeits for Y.A.T.C. Juvenile Stakes
(won by F.J.A. last year) saw that stable (Scobie’s) repre-
sented by Haut Nove, full brother to Sweet Nell, who
cost Mr: William Bailey 750 gs. as a yearling and hasn't
shown in public yet.
• * • *
Books had a great time at last Moonee Valley
meet-four of six races went to outsiders, three of them
very much so ; still some of the tribe insisted that they
lost heavily. Mo, according to himself, never wins, and
yet, though living on the fat of the land, he yackers not
neither does he twirl the long-handled shovel of honest
industry.
* * * *
Dingo and Nor’ West, who had fenish of recent
Takapuna Steeple between them, are full brothers.
Re fecundity of M.L. brood mare Rosalind
(Bulletin, 17/12/’O3). Breeder (T. Shannon) of last
Christchurch Derby winner, Roseal, handled all Rosa-
lind’s progeny, and says Dog Rose, a bay gelding by
Natator, was the best of the lot. At two years Rosalind
became the property of Mr. Redwood, and cost 37gs.
Following season she was put to the stud. Her first foal
(White Rose) was accidentally killed. Black Rose,
Alpine Rose, Sweetie and Redwood all won races. Awaru
Rose gathered a treble at Nelson Jubilee meeting. Royal
Rose performed well in Australia and M.L., and then
went to India. Yellow Rose is the dam of Heloise, and
Spring Rose of Rose Petal, a 3-year-old filly by Handsome
Rose. Last of Rosalind’s lot is a yearling chestnut filly
by Hillstone. Old age necessitated Rosalind’s destruction
last winter.
• • * •
Oldest nag seen out at recent Otahuhu (M.L.)
Trotting Club’s meet was Little Ben, by Ben Bolt—he
tops 16. Eleven years since Little Ben won Auckland
Trotting Club’s Cup, then worth £3OO. Said that Little
Ben’s sire’s dam was Sydney mare Bay Judy—a once
particularly good performer who beat all comers for a
long time.
* * * *
Opening (Boxing Day) of W.A.T.O.’s (Perth)
New Year meet saw a record attendance (12,000) for
Derby Day—3ooo better than last year. Tote results
showed considerable increase ; despite big business done
by an army of Moses, £9BOO passed through the machine.
Remarkable features were extraordinary big fields in
evidence and fact that absolute first favorites appropriated
five races, the other (Cubana, in Maiden Plate) just being
pipped on the post by Batolga, 15 to 1. W.A.T.C. Handi-
cap attracted 25 starters (biggest field ever seen in
Westralia), yet Meteorite opened at “ evens,” and, lining
up, odds on, made the running over every inch, and
romped home easily first. Opposition included such fine
performers as Santoi, Durham, Street Arab, and Aqua.
Bandolier made hacks of everything else in the Derby,
and qualified for the coin in 2.42 2-s—a record for that
particular section of the Commonwealth, Bandolier is
by Light Artillery from Queensdale.
* * * *
A “selling race” at a Victorian centre.
Three horses start in the race—Carmine, Whirlwind,
and Spring. Carmine is not wanted to win, and to
make certain that he won’t, he is weighed out about
71b. short. The owner of Whirlwind knows Carmine
is “dead,” and thinks he will deaden Spring and
back Whirlwind ; and he does. Whirlwind rapidly
shortens to 3 to X on, owner of Spring laying
most of the money. Owner of Whirlwind finding
market gone, and knowing also that Carmine is
dead and short weight, takes £l5 to 3 Spring, and
gives his jockey the necessary instructions to run any-
where but first. The three horses start, Carmine
leads all the way cantering—jockey can afford to be
reckless as he is short weight. Eider of Whirl-
wind looks round anxiously at the hard-pulling
Spring who seems anxious to have a cut in but can’t.
Whirlwind moves up to Carmine, but Carmine
canters in a winner by a neck. The rider of Whirlwind
jogs back quickly to paddock, pulls the saddle off his
horse and rushes to the bar to have a drink. Stewards
declare Carmine short weight and look round for second
horse. He is also disqualified for unsaddling out of place.
Spring can’t get the race as he did not complete the
course. Race declared off. All bets declared off.
Disgusted smart men get part of their money back, a
bookie having levanted with the rest. Left stating dis-
gustedly that the country is ruined by socialistic legisla-
tion.
* * * *
A grinning Maori jock, with a mouth like a
crater, and tent-flap ears, turned up lately at Masterton
trots, robed in a blue jacket, with the Union Jack pinned
back and front, and kharki pants. He won mid the wild
enthusiasm of the natives, but whether they were
cheering the rider, the Union John, or the dividend
wasn’t apparent.
* * * *
George Hirst has a plum in store for him. The
Yorkshire Cricket Club promises him a benefit next year
—the most popular match of the local season (v. Lan-
cashire), commencing on the August bank holiday.
Given fine weather for the three days, the beneflciare will
get fully £l5OO. It pays better to be a first-class English
county cricketer than a “ crack ” Australian Elevener.
* * * *
Only 7000 looked on opening of N.S.W.-Victoria
cricket match in Melbourne, Saturday. Play was slow and
methodical and generally on the lines of what has been
inflicted so much this season, and was consequently
wearying. Bowling was off, as a rule, and fielding be-
came loose as the afternoon wore. Victorians operated
first on an excellent wicket, and might have heaped up a
big pile of runs, but they didn’t try much and, though
most got set, a careless or a weak attempt usually
brought about dismissal—the tired feeling effect of the
heat was upon all. Beginning looked like disaster,
M'Beth’s first ball beat Bruce badly. Two were down
for 66, McAlister occupied 1 hour 7m. getting 34, Arm-
strong 38mins. longer making 44 and Trott "(who was
shaping well) lh. 28m. putting 38 together—he was
caught low down at point by Noble. Three runs further
on Giller went—s for 132; with everything favorable to
the bat it was unaccountable. Next wicket didn’t fall
till 216 showed. Laver’s compilation was 49 in lh. 13m.
At 237 Stukey succumbed—7 for 276 at stumps draw
There was no play on Monday, owing to rain. •
* * * Jg
“B.”: The team which played for Victoria in
the Inter-State match against N.S.W. had a dug-up look.
Veteran Trott re-appeared, as did back-number Giller.
Hughie Trumble and Chas. M'Leod had both practically
retired, and Harry Stuckey, who was announced as a non-
player this season, dropped casually into the team.
* * * *
The action of N.S.W. selection committee in
choosing 11 young players to represent N.S.W. against
Queensland (for this fixture above all others offers the
opportunity) was justified after all by the runs (334) home
side accumulated in first innings, Saturday. This easily
tops the best made by either Queensland or N.S.W. in
same match on same ground last year, and is better than
any score since performance of N.S.W.’s best team at
Brisbane four years ago. Though a better combination,
and perhaps one or two more just as good, might have
been picked, it wasn’t plain to the close observer that the
State was weakly represented ; men were all good run-
getters and “fields,” and the worst bat was no worse
than two or three who readily find places among the
State’s crack lot. Things looked blue at one stage. First
wicket (Blaxland’s) was down for 48, and at 105 five men
had gone back to the pavilion. Hickson was at work
70min. for his 50, and gathered the only couple of fives of
the day. Black made his first showing in inter-State
matches, and notched 72. Fielding of visiting side wasn’t
near the thing, otherwise it wouldn’t have had to face
anything like the score set it. Aboriginal Henry
bowled very erratioallv. Left-handers Byrnes and Miller
(also Griffiths) did well, and would have done much better
but for poor fielding.
* * * •
Sid Gregory’s 152 against South Australia, last
week, occupied but 3hrs., and included 24 fours. Noble’s
147, compiled in 3hr. 22m., embraced one five and 18 fours.
Looks as if Gregory is picking up his old form, and isany-
thing but the “ has been ” alleged. Four big matches to
time of writing have seen 25, 57, 23, 43 and 152 to his
credit; besides, in the field, he is class enough for any
combination. At 33 (provided he looks after himself)
“Sid” should be good enough for forefront cricket
throughout several seasons yet.
* * * *
Australian old man cricketer George Giffen has
surely lagged superfluous as a bowler. So far, present
season, his bag is one wicket at a cost of 263 runs. Last six
innings which saw him opposed to N.S.W. he secured
two for 522.
* 9 * *
The wicket used for N.S.W.-Vic. match, pro-
gressing as The Bulletin goes to press, is made of Bulli
(N.S.W.) soil, specially taken over because the locally-
obtained article has shown considerable falling off
during past few years.
* * * *
The idea of providing an international tinpot
to hold the imaginary “ ashes ” in connection with
Australia v. England test matches, has met the deserving
fate of a knock-out blow. Already there’s enough trouble
over a couple of cricket trophies in Australia—which have
mostly to be kept locked up on account of their value ;
and the inauguration of a rich Gup would only be another
source of woe. Besides, what a deal of heartburning it
would cause as to which State should have the right of
hiding it!
• * * *
“Euroa”: Re Hugh Stone’s par (8., 17/12703).
In my cricketing days the umpire always carried a bat.
It was a recognised thing for him to have a strike while
awaiting the coming of another batsman, or during any
interval whatever; and, besides, a bat was a tower of
strength to the umpire who gave a decision which did not
meet with the approval of the other side’s barrackers.
Still, I recognise that a machine-gun would be better.

* * * *
Although the Victorians complain of the lack
of new blood in their State team, they showed very little
interest in the doings of 18 Juniors versus the English-
men. The M.O.C. pavilion and grand-stand were empty
when the “ boys ” began their batting, and such applause
as they received from the meagre sixpenny crowd wa 8
mostly satirical. Yet in one respect the local talene
seemed to have a pull over the visitors. Their bowling
W. A . TRENWITH ,
who headed the Senate poll in Victoria.
was on the wicket. Six of the Englishmen were legiti-
mately beaten by a ball that hit the stumps, whereas only
three out of 14 Juniors (three were run out) succumbed
to a clean bowl in their first innings.

* Hs * *
The general remark in Adelaide concerning
cricket is: If Noble and co. can’t get our second-best
batsmen out for a reasonable figure, how on earth is
England ever to be got out at all—unless Hugh Trumble
is to do all the work? All the same, S.A. cricket pro-
mises better now than for some years past, and wagers
are being made that the State gets four men in the
1905 Australian Eleven for England.
* * * *
Gate (£4274 10s.) in connection with first test
mateh was cut up thus: Marylebone O.C. and N.S.W.
Cricket Association £1521195. each, and Sydney Cricket
Ground £1230 lls. Association ha 3 to pay £650 for
ground exes, etc., out of its whack. At moment of
writing, trustees of the ground and the State’s cricket
governing body are at outs again through former handing
Marylebone C.C.’s portion of the spoil direct to the team’s
manager instead of to the Association, which, ’tis insisted,
has full control of the N.S.W. portion of the tour. Looks
as if there’ll now always be friction between ground and
Association people, and the fact won’t benefit cricket
much. Sooner the whole thing is handed over to the
N.S.W.O.A. the better it will be for all and everything
concerned.
* * * *
A walking record has been put up by Regnet in
a match from Bordeaux to Paris, 375 miles. He covered
the distance in 114 hrs. 42 min.—averaging about 80 milch
a day.
* * 9 *
Recently deceased, at the age of 47, Queensland
sport, Mr. Ernest Winter, was most prominent in rowing,
whereat he first, competed 24 years ago, and, last, early
this year. Mr. Winter figured successfully in State
champion fours and eights, and stroked first Q. crew (got
together by himself) in inter-State eight-oar race 18 years
since, and eight years later filled a similar position.
He was also stroke of Queensland four in Victorian cham-
pion event on the Yarra, November 9,1889, and had con-
siderable to do with the big sculling carnival held in
Brisbane in 1888, when world-famed Searle, Beach, Kemp,
Stanbury, Neil Matterson and co. competed.
* * * *
Details of recent match (in London) between
“ Russian Lion ” George Hackenschmidt and “ Terrible
Greek ” Pierri, for world’s ’rastling supremacy, show the
contest to have been quite a fashionable affair. Ladies
mustered largely, and swellest families in England were
represented. Twenty - four - year - old Hackenschmidt
proved too tough and had too much “stay’’for his 17
years senior Pierri, whose wiles kept him going longer
than did his physical powers. Russian proved a veritable
Hercules. Hackenschmidt won the first fall (Grseco-
Roman) in I7min. llsec., and second (cateh-as-catch-can)
in 15min. 25sec.
* * * *
“X.”: Major Taylor hasn’t got the huge picnic
on that many people imagine. His legs and riding capa-
bilities are sold to a syndicate which negotiates for him ;
and he cannot compete in a race without the sanction of
this syndicate. Therein he has a great pull over Yankee
visitors Lawson, McFarland and Downing. It doesn’t
matter so much to Taylor whether he is defeated or not
in bike races, he scoops in the “ appearance ” money ;
whilst the other Yankees have to depend mainly on the
prizes. By the way, Taylor, being a “colored” person,
should be doubly challenged by the Australian Govern-
ment laws—as colored alien and immigrant under con-
tract.
* * * *
The oft-chimed “splendid condition” theory
didn’t pan out correctly in connection with Major Taylor
at Christchurch (M.L.) meeting last week. The cable
told that he had kept himself in splendid condition, and
with a ride or so on the track would be as well as ever.
However, as Taylor was defeated by Sutherland in a two-
mile scratch race, his form doesn’t seem too good, since
the M.L. champion has never shown any speed out of the
ordinary. Taylor will certainly improve, but the
“ splendid condition” preliminary advt. should be used
with more discretion.
* * * *
On his initial appearance at M.L. last week
Major Taylor began his systematic grumbling re being
“ blocked.” Somehow, these alleged great riders always
resort to the same paltry excuse when beaten ; and the
quibble grows sickening. Lawson (another Yankee)
paraded a similar grievance at Coolgardie t’other
Saturday. Amongst Australian riders the “blocked”
grievance is seldom heard—each rider being satisfied to
take the luck as it comes. Taylor, in particular, should
be the last to grumble, since his mode of riding when
bunched leads either to smash or success.
* * * *
R. Arnst, winner of this year’s M:L. Wheel
Race, is one of the Arnst brothers, who are always so
formidable in long-distance road racing. There were 44
starters, and he had a handicap of 110yds. Starting from
scratch later in the day, he dead-heated with Watson in
another two-mile handicap.
» * * *
How are the mighty fallen ! At Sydney Golden
Gate A. 0., Saturday night, one-time crack lightweight,
“Friday” O’Neill (hog-fat, listless, and without the
smallest outward indication of having done anything in
the way of preparation), proved a soft snap for black Joe
Walcott—little better than a novice. Though O’Neill had
a decided pull up to then, one punch on the jaw in the
third round caused him to quit in his corner, where he
was tended on by another light of years ago (Mick
Dooley), who has also let himself down with a run.
Dooley was the stepping-stone to world’s fame for Jack-
son, Slavin and Goddard, and was only second in point
of skill to the first-named when that past-master was at
his top.
* * * *
“M‘G. An institution of interest to tech-
nical education enthusiasts, ethnologists, and followers
of the ring has been established at Fremantle by three
members of the famous pugilistic Duggan farmly, erst-
while of South Melbourne. The Duggans pitched their
training quarters close to the aboriginal camp ac
Fremantle, and the nigs began to display such an
interest in the game that they were one day permitted,
for purposes of gentle leg-pulling, to put ’em on. noon
the whole tribe was hard at it acquiring the upper-cut,
the half-arm jolt and straight jab, and out of a mass of
chaff the Duggans winnowed two grains of pug'hstic
wheat in the persons of Jacky and Wandi, full-blooded
blackpfellers. Jacky and Wandi, who are in the light-
weight division, were initiated into the mysteries of the
jaw and the solar plexus, and they were entered for their
pugilistic matriculation a couple of weeks back, when
successively matched against Dido Plumb. Jacky failed
to uphold the honor of Australia, but showed smartness,
hitting power, and—most important of all—gameness and
capacity to take punishment. Wandi, regarded as "he
better of the pair, is now out to avenge his brudder s
defeat. The Duggans aver they have some excellent raw
material among these aborigines, and threaten to turn
out within six months a heavyweight capable of downing
Peter Felix. But the exquisite Peter would probably
draw the color line, on the ground that a cultured colored
gemman with a frock coat and a belltopper could never
demean himself by entering the ring with a common
savage.
* * * *
Continent’s champion welter weight (10.7) pug
George Johns (still only a youngster), finding his occupa-
tion completely gone through being carefully avoided by
all fighters within half a stone of him, has determined to
retire and concentrate his muscular power on beer-pump
pulling. Johns was a Sydney waterman till he took to
the fighting business, at which he punched his way into
fame very quickly. He remained idle, and challenged
frequently but vainly, for a whole year. He is among
the cleverest, most aggressive, and best stayers at his
poundage Australia has known. A pity that such a man
should have to retire for lack of chopping-blocks, with
years more of good in him. Probability is that he’ll lie
by for months, get rusty, and come out again and be
badly licked.
* * * *
Biggest bruising thing Sydney has had for some
time takes place at National S.C., Tuesday next, with
globe-trotter Dan Creedon and Australian bandsman,
“Soldier Thompson” (his name is Yercoe), as principals.
Oreedon is much above t’other man in science, apd will
probably serve out heaps of punishment in first few
rounds, and perhaps “ get him ” ; but Thompson’s
chance isn’t at all bad, for, barring a sufficiently forceful
blow on the right spot, he will stand punishment and
last too, which Oreedon may not, if the thing goes over
half-a-dozen rounds. Oreedon, a few years ago, would
have found Thompson decidedly soft, but Creedon, as he
is to-day, may find him a very stiff task. Match is
creating widespread interest, and promises excitement.
* * * *
Right between crack lightweights Tim Hegarty
and Charlie Frost, at Sydney National 5.0., was vigorous
throughout. Each went for the other with contempt for
possibilities that often surprised, and, despite Hegarty’s
exceptional footwork and skilful head-shifting, Frost
frequently managed to jab home a straight left that
wasn’t over clever. Hegarty rarely blocked when he
countered, and generally treated the enemy with scant
respect. In the eighth round a right on the jaw made
Frost bump the boards and stay there 9secs. Later same
man was spreadeagled again and was “ out” but for “cor-
ners." Next round brought the end early, a right hook
on the jaw rendering Frost oblivious to surroundings,
and a fair amount of coin was gathered in by the
Hegarty party, which laid 2 to 1 on. Manner in which one
of Frost's seconds (quite new at the game) picked his
principal up and dragged him along to his chair at gong
sound after the eighth round helped materially towards
the end.
* * * »
A slab of ’Murkan reporting. New York
Journal’s tribute to word’s champion : “James
Jeffries, of Los Angelos, Cal., is unquestionably monarch
of the brute creation. The hairy-breasted gorilla of the
Pacific Slope has twice beaten the trained chimpanzee
Corbett to submission ; has twice hammered the speckled
kangaroo Fitzsimmons to a condition of admiring meek-
ness ; has twice fractured the dorsal fin of the marine
broncho Sharkey ; has caved in the slats of the human
turnip Ruhlin, and reduced lesser brutes to various con-
ditions of physical wreck. Jeffries is in exactly the
pitiable condition of Alexander the Great, who grieved
himself to death because there were no more worlds to
conquer. Jeff, won’t grieve himself to death. He will
use alcohol as a substitute for grief, and the effects will
be even more deadly. A year or so before he is
thoroughly pickled by the rum treatment Jef. will meet
some gawk from Squashville, who never tasted anything
stronger than sweet cider. About the fourteenth round
the champion will be officially counted out.”
* * * *
A lately returned Maoriland tripper was much
impressed with the way they ran races at Moscow. The
starter is the most important man on the track. Togged
out in a gorgeous livery, he drives to the post in state,
flanked by an official on either side. The starting gate is'
used, but in a way undreamt of out here. The neddies
are taken a couple of hundred yards from the machine
and sent away. The hindmost are given a chance to get
even with the leaders ere they get to the barrier, which
is released on their approach. The race is then con-
sidered started. When the starter thinks himself com-
petent to do without the barrier the send-off usually re-
sembles that of a menagerie race at a country sports
shivoo, and backers use long words in a thick, loud voice
to express their feelings. The best thing about the
Russian racing system relates to cronkness; the Mus-
covites go to the extent of extracting saliva from a
horse’s mouth for analysis if they suspect the animal has
been drugged. Disqualification for life from all courses
in the empire, with gaol for “ ringing in,” is their method
of flattening out the clever brigade.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.— A loud “Amen!” to the
words of “ J.F.D.” (8., 12/11/03) anent that soulless indi-
vidual, the pigeon-assassin. With him class the coursing
man, who finds an ecstatic joy in chasing the life out of
an animal that does little harm while it is alive, and
doesn’t taste nice when it is dead, though the fact that
the coursing man is to some extent tolerated by his dog
possibly shows that he is a little higher in the human
scale than the other. Few pigeon-killers have a physique
that a self-respecting sparrow would care to own ; and to
see a p.k. slouching home from a match with his hands
in his pockets, while a boy follows in the rear with his
gun and mangled birds, is to realise what vertical worms
some clothes do hide.— Grant Hervey.
* * * *
An up-country parson in S.A. has introduced,
for the first time in Australia, the game of vigoro. Vigoro
is a mixture of rounders, tennis, and cricket—mostly
cricket. The wicket is a hoop, which sounds like croquet,
and the scoring is the same as in cricket. The bowler
bowls with a racket, the batsman (or batswoman) bats
with a racket, and the fielders have rackets also. The
ball must not be touched with the hands.

Timi Kara (“Jimmie” Carroll), M.L.
Native Minister, and himself a half-caste Maori,
woke up the other evening in the House of Reps.J
and made the finest speech of the session in an.
appeal to Parliament to “do justice to a noble
race”—to give more time and trouble to the
improvement of the Maori. “Timi,” who, when,
he makes the effort, is easily the nearest approach
an orator in the House, was as graceful as ever
and his metaphors were strikingly beautiful!
-but, lethargic for many years past, he is getting-
very tired and worn-looking now, and the recent
departure of a grizzled moustache has not changed
the impression of his weary face.
Upon present appearances the White Man
?" d “ ,e ? rown may begin to belt each other in.
the North at any moment. The sympathies of'
Australian democrats must needs go with the*
white man—however bad a lot he may be.
Australasia is a lonely outpost of the white race.’.
, 116 r ac ? may n °t have arrived here too honestly
but it is here. It is threatened by yellow men.
outside and by treacherous Tories and frantic*
booksellers from within. The great danger-
always hanging over Australia is an irruption of
tbe colored races that will overlay and finally-
obliterate our white lineage, and destroy our-
white traditions, and wipe out our present beau-
tiful facial resemblance to Edward VII. and Joe
Chamberlain. Britain may ally itself with the
Jap or the Chinaman or the baboon, but paper
and ink arrangements are temporary and this,
question is permanent.

The Bulletin is still inquiring anxiously
for that alleged Freetrader who doesn’t find his
creed too dirty for daily use. Here, for instance,
is the printed shriek of Liddell, the new Freetrade
member for the Hunter (N.S.W.): —
In announcing myself as a candidate, I do so as a sup-
porter of the Right Hon. George Reid, whom I regard as
the leader of the great Liberal party of Australia. ... I
am opposed to a tariff system which, by the operation of
protective duties, will create monopolies and lead to the
formation of a commercial aristocracy at the expense of
the poor man. . . . Nevertheless, I am a Protectionist,
namely, in the sense that would nourish and further true
colonial industries. By colonial industries I mean all the
industries of Australia—specially those great industries
connected with the soil—the agricultural, the pastoral,
the mining industries. These native industries I would,
by careful adjustment of the tariff, assist to the utmost.
In other words, Freetrader Liddell holds that
Protection is a bad, injurious thing, and promises
to damage his own constituents with it as much
as possible, and the Hunter farmers, on his pro-
mise to get them the horrors of Protection, cor-
dially voted for him on the Freetrade ticket.
For more than two months George Turner,
“the little man in the shabby brown suit and
cheap spectacles,” has run the Commonwealth of
Australia on his own. He has done it all in a quiet,
modest way which should put toshamethe pompous
swaggering of the “sound business man ” running
a shoddy shop—the man who is eternally being
pointed to by the Tory crowd as the one to whom
the Commonwealth should be handed over body
and soul. Turner is just a little solicitor with a
decent family consulting practice. He has two
dingy little rooms on the first floor of a ramshackle
old building in Collins-st. There he spends his
time from nine o’clock till about one in the after-
noon. He catches a tram to Parliament House,
has a plain lunch, and toddles across to the
Treasurer’s office. From two o’clock till six he sits
and polishes off the papers brought to him by the
heads of the Treasury, Defence, Home Affairs,
Foreign Affairs, Customs, Attorney-General’s
and Postmaster-General’s departments. “I’m a
sort of political barber saying ‘Next please,’ ” he
remarked the other day.
“ Croyez ” : I vote for Cromwell as the
name of our Commonwealth capital. Cromwell
and Commonwealth to me suggest one another.
The closing scenes of the Victorian Par-
liament were badly splashed over with bullying
Bentisms. Bent had passed an Unused Roads
Bill through the Assembly, providing for the
leasing of all unused roads in the State. The
Legislative Council banged it about until it was
as shapeless as Bent himself. Then Bent tried to
get the Council’s amendments through the
Assembly, regardless of the fact that, in one case
at least, the Council had stepped outside its legal
rights and had invaded the privileges of the
Assembly. For a wonder, Peacock and Duffy
protested against this proposal, and Bent, Peacock
and Speaker Beazley came into violent collision.
Bent became so blusterous that when Premier
Irvine rushed into the House to prevent a free
fight, Bent opposed a motion moved by Irvine to
overcome the trouble. Thomas also insulted the
Speaker, and had to apologise. At one time
Bent was Speaker himself, and was accustomed to
say, whenever his ruling was challenged on con-
stitutional grounds, “Well, that’s my ruling for
the present. It will do for to-night. ”
Few men have a better knowledge of the
working of the Lands Acts than has Land Agent
W. N. Willis, of 21 Bligh-street. Payment by
results is not only reasonable but also novel.*
Bryan (TLoghlen, who rushed into the
Senate fray in Victoria at the last minute, and
then stood quite still while the battle raged
around him, didn’t do so badly after all. He beat
George Wise and Harry Williams, although
everybody prophesied that he would lose his
deposit. As a fact, none of the Senate candidates
lost their deposits. They all polled more than
one-fifth of the number polled by the lowest suc-
cessful man. When in the Assembly, O’Loghlen
used to go about with a want-of-confidence motion
hanging out of one coat-tail and a tattered scheme
for raising a loan of 40 or 50 millions bulging out
of the other.

“ Kati-Kati ” writes to the wife about
“ The Husband ”: —
Now that you have got him, he is no longer your lover
—he is your husband.
And you will have to make the best of him.
For the best will be none too good.
He is only human now.
And is very fond of comfort.
Hid you ever keep pigs?
Did you ever notice how contented they are when they
are full ?
Man is the same.
Treat him as a pig.
Fill him up.
Before you caught him, he was like the lark carolling
in the sky.
You heard the song, but you did not feed the singer.
Now you have him in your hands he needs a lot of
looking after, and there is not much singing.
But never mind.
You were determined to have him, or another very
much like him.
If you have one at all, which you have is a matter ot
indifference.
You. would not have been happy without one.
You will not be happy with one.
But your destiny had to be fulfilled.
Now you have him, keep him.
And do not talk him to death.
You are a woman, and must talk.
Or burst.
But let him talk too.
Let him air his ignorance.
He has plenty of it.
Enough to sink a ship.
Be thou content with three-quarters of the conversation.
And the last word. j
Aiwa vs get the last word.
Nothing will make a man realise his inferiority sooner
than a defeat in the struggle for the last word.
It is the best of arguments.
In some ways treat him as though you were still trying
to catch him.
You used to remember his birthdays.
Do not forget them now.
Get him something which will give him pleasure.
He used to say that the greatest pleasure of his life was
to see you happy.
Get iiim a new bonnet for yourself.
He will see you happy, and enjoy one of the greatest
pleasures of his life.
Remember that he is only a grown-up baby.
And that he has not changed much in the growing-up.
Do not forget to treat him as one.
He does not need feeding so often.
But he needs more at a time.
That is the main difference.
Let him have what he wants.
But do not let him want anything that you do not
want him to have. .
It is likely that to have the idea that he is running the
show will give him pleasure.
Let him have the idea while you have the rest.
He wants to smoke in the house.
Then let him do so.
If you wanted a house like an old maid’s, you should
have remained single, or married a woman.
But you have married a man.
Who is three-quarters animal, and one-quarter baby.
And a baby is one-quarter animal and three-quarters
howl.
Then do not expect him to act as a lady.
If he did the house would be unbearable with the
chatter of the two of you.
Let him smoke in the house—and you do the talking.
You are always well able.
Except when you are dead.
He enjoys smoking in the house.
Let him have some enjoyment while you are alive.

A hash-lady’s moan :
A few weeks ago there was a par in The Bulletin to the
effect that boarding-house-keepers object to children in
the house. They DO. Married people who board pay for
themselves, and expect the kid’s board and lodging to be
thrown in gratis. The married lydy who boards is
usually a lazy, vain woman, who won’t look after her
husband arid child. She breakfasts in bed, and at 11.80
she appears painted and peroxided for the day. The
husband is a hunted wretch, loaded with debt; he
boards because his goods and chattels have been sold-for
the benefit of his creditors. He can’t pay people to wait
on his wife and mirid his child; so his idea is that the
hash-house can serve as a sort of unpaid parent to his
off pring. When mamma takes the offspring out she
thumps it and gives it instructions to call her “Alice,”
so that people will take her for its elder sister. The
boarding-house-keeper is usually a widow with a family,
and when she finds herself perpetually falling over a
strange infant, whose painted mother is in bed, it gets on
her nerves.
“ Marat ” : Think the Government should
stop the erection of the pill-box cottages —two
rooms, kitchen and bath—which are being run up
by speculative builders in Sydney suburbs. They
have much to do with the decreasing birth-rate.
Newly-married couples take them, and are afraid
to raise a family on account of not having room
to swing an infant. One “pill-box” occupant
informed me once that they hadn’t room for any
more children, “and the cottage is such a dear
little place, we couldn’t leave it.” If Shak-
speare’s parents had lived in a two-roomed house
under the same conditions there would have been
no Shakespere. Think of a landlord being
allowed to build cottages in which there is no
room for Shakspeare !
A Sydney bootmarr recently had on view
a battered boot of his manufacture, which he
alleged had just been taken from the stomach of
a captured shark. He proved by the date of
the owner’s disappearance, that the splendidly
durable article must have withstood the shark’s
efforts to digest it for fully six months. The
opposition bootmaker, three doors away, imme-
diately responded to the new move. He p*it a
large placard in his front window (red letters on
a green background), and the passers-by rushed
it. It read:
Our boots are so easy and comfortable that a purchaser
does not commit suicide to escape the torture which an
ill-fitting boot brings to its unfortunate wearer. We are
not sorry, therefore, that we cannot show samples of
our footgear taken from the stomachs of sharks. None
of our customers have as yet been driven to throw them-
selves overboard through the pain in their feet.
And the other man gave a mighty cur.-e and
heaved the show boot into the backyard.
“ C.A.R.0.” tells of a cheap sale :
Last week I had some odd pennies left out of an attenu-
ated allowance, and, being possessed of superabundant
energy, decided to make a day of it, with- a crony, at a
certain cheap sale. I donned a short skirt, flannel blouse,
and hat well inured to crushes, and caught an early boat
Met my companion in “ fairing,” and looked at her
askance. A trailing skirt, daintiest of blouses, and won-
drous hat look beautiful, but —— We hesitated on the
threshold where the flaunting advertisements were, but
were swept in to where there was semi-darkness and a
heaving of countless women.and many breaths. Despair-
ingly I went with the tide. Presently, wedged between
a corpulent Irish woman and a weary assistant, I tried to
get up some interest in the soiled, dragged and crushed
remnants on the counter. I was shaking out a crumpled
length, when a hand flashed before me, a sharp nail
scored deeply across my wrist, and the silk vanished in
the clutches of a female grimly triumphant. I meditated
many wicked thoughts. Noticed a table of light-colored,
woollen scarves, mostly torn. “ Moths,” I queried.
“ No-o,” said a tired girl, “ they are dragged so by the
ladies.” I drifted hopelessly, elbowed, hustled and
trodden on till the lust of war rose within me, and then I
battled manfully with a cadaverous female for a packet
of rusty hair pins, and conquered, even as Scipio did
when he licked Hannibal. At last, in a back wash, I was
swept near a doorway, and after a last wild charge over
children’s spades and buckets and a shop walker, I
gained the street. There I found my companion weeping
and dishevelled and with no purchases. After using
most of my hard-won hair-pins in repairing damages, we
came home gingerly, and by many by-paths.

South New Moon, Bendigo’s best mine, has now
had a2£ years’ run of dividends—an unusually, long spell
for a Bendigo mine nowadays. In that time it has paid
away £171,200, or £5 7s. per share, besides spending
£12,185 on machinery. Past half-year’s record has been
£52,800, or £ll3s. per share. This latter is the result of
13,980 tons of stone, averaging (pyrites, etc., included)
loz. 3dwt, s£gr., worth £64,043. The total costs on the
mine were £lo,234,'equal to about 14s. Bd. per ton. The
co. has just decided to pay back to the Government £6OO
which was advanced from the prospecting vote 17 years
ago. It paid big dividends for 2£ years before this
little matter occurred to it.
* * * *
“ Revert ”: Nearly everyone who writes on
Anakie (Q.) and its gems seems to be unavoidably more
or less of an Ananias. Fitful paragraphs appear in Rock-
hampton and other papers booming and boosting Anakie
stones, with the only result of sending up more consign-
ments of derelicts to fossick for a few weeks or months,
and then drift away in disgust. Your correspondent
(8., 17/12/’O3) says : “ Till within the last two years our
gems only fetched corundum prices.” When I was there,
over two years ago, the price given for corundum was
about 2s. 6d. per pound ; and sapphires were bought at
from about ss. to 12s. per oz., on an average. The pick
of the blue stones were fetching £3 per oz. —any odd
'weights or half-ounces were almost invariably “ of little
or no value,” to quote the buyer’s memo. ; and
if you wouldn’t accept his terms for the bulk, he would
send the whole consignment back. Want of expert
knowledge in classing and pricing the stones places the
diggers in the clutch of the buyers, who won’t give
a price good- enough to let men make wages year in and
year out. Nevertheless the oldest hand told me (and as
far as I knew him he was reliable) that he made £l5 per
week for some time, years ago, and that it still pays him
to cart and wash dirt for loz. to the load. But he has
spent much time and money in finding his own buyers
for particular stones, and keeps their names to himself.
Also he knows the country. There is nothing to guide
the digger, except a certain boulder which sometimes
occurs where there is sapphire wash, and sometimes
forgets to occur, and sometimes occurs where there is no
wash.

According to Lond. Economist’s figures, Aus-
tralasia has eight of the 20 most prolific gold mines in the
world at present. The list (the figures refer to the value
of the output per month) :
Homestake (S. Dakota) .. .. £85,000
Simmer and Jack (Rand) .. .. 78,000
Boulder Perseverance (Australia) .. 63,000
Robinson (Rand) .. .. 62,000
G. Horseshoe (Australia) .. .. 60,000
Champion Reef (India) ~ 60,000
Rose Deep (Rand) 58,000
Mysore (India) 55,000
Waihi (Maoriland) .. ~ 51,000
Geldenhuis (Rand) .. .. .. 51,000
Mt. Morgan (Australia) .. .. 50,000
Portland (Colorado) .. .. 50,000
Great Fingall (Australia) 48,000

Crown Deep (Rand) .. .. 46,000
City and Sub. (Rand) .. .. 46,000
Boulder (Australia) 45,000
Oroya-Brown Hill (Australia) .. 44,000
Ivanhoe (Australia) 44,000
The Waihi’s position will probably surprise most people.
As a matter of fact, though, on the co’s. last year’s
figures, it" hardly stands so high ; its output for that 12
months averaged just under £44,000. Still, it has been a
great producer and a great dividend payer. In four
years 553,203 tons of stone yielded £1,603,206 worth of
bullion, and dividends of 38s. per share, or in all £764,326,
were paid, free of income tax ; the tax amounted to
£48,757. The Waihi bullion is worth about 15s. 6d.
per oz. : it contains nearly 6oz. of silver to loz. of gold.
* * * *
General manager Schlenzig, of the mining con-
cessions held by the New Guinea Co. and Huon Gulf
Syndicate, German New Guinea, has come south on the
way to Europe ; it looks as though the trip were taken
partly to help push off some smaller co.’s to work sluicing
ground. Sohlenzig’s co.’s were formed in 1885, and, so
far, have given most attention to growing kapok, cocoa-
nuts and coffee, on which they have 5000 natives working.
They are not satisfied with the local labor supply, though,
and want to recruit in the South Sea islands ; if they
can’t do that they will go for Chinese coolies. Nothing
much has, so far, beendone in mining; but £lO,OOO is
now being spent in small sluicingplants. Early this year
20 Australian miners were taken over to prospect the
watershed of the Ramil river, but the results were not
what the co. had hoped for. Every stream flowing from
the Bismarck Ranges is, however, known to carry gold ;
and the country round the headwaters of the Fly River
is believed to be rich, but this is not within the New
Guinea co.’s'grab. The New Guinea co. works on a basis
of returning to the Government 10 per cent, of its
revenue ; while the Iluon Gulf syndicate has undertaken
to spend £500,000 on its 6000 square mile patch.
* * * *
It is estimated that for the first time Westralia’s
wheat-harvest will be sufficient to feed the State until
the next one is due. But there will be considerable
flour-trade .westward, nevertheless ; there is so much
prejudice against the local product that in Perth it can
only be sold at £1 to £2 less than Adelaide flour.

“Ed.” writes. At Redcastle (Vic.) near the
old M’lvor diggings, are two heaps of tailings containing
a few thousand tons of sand. Two companies have tried
to treat them by eyaniding. One lost £l2OO ; the other
£4OO. One man experimented with a small plant and
dropped £l5O. Last year another enterprising man
erected a concentrating and c.vaniding plant at a cost of
£4OOO, and sold out for £lOOO. Now a fifth party is
tackling it. , He is just obtaining enough gold to make a
loss. The sand assays well, some giving as high as Bdwt.,
hut when "treated in the bulk will not give up the gold.
The sand, besides gold, contains arsenic, copper, and a
high percentage of antimony. The antimony decomposes
the potassium cyanide, which is then not effective in
dissolving the goid. Up to date more sovereigns have
been sunk in the sand than ever can come out, These
tailings are Itedcastle’s salvation. All other shows are
shut down, but somebody is always struggling with the
sand,
A * * * *
Tlie scare about a possible early shortage of fuel
and timber for the W.A. mints has been allayed by forest
ranger Kelson. He reckons that the total yearly con-
sumption is 470,000 tons, of which the Kalgoorlie mines
take 380,000 tons. Within the present zone of operations
he estimates that there is eight years’ supply in sight at
current price of 13s. a'ton landed on mines. By extend-
ing the tramway system, another 12 years’ supply can be
got at 13s. to 14s. a ton. And 20 years is quite as far as
any mining field can he expected to look.

“Hix”: “Chasselas” (Bulletin, 17/12/U3) is
badly informed concerning the Victorian export trade in
agricultural implements. He quotes 14 per week as
being the biggest out-turn of any manufacturer of har-
vesters. One factory (at Ballarat) makes and delivers 48
of these machined per week, has been doing so for many
months, employing, over 300 men and working night anil
day. “ Chasselas ” :br quite wrong in his assertion that
malleable castings ate not made locally. There are two
(if not more) malleable foundries in Melbourne and one in
Ballarat. The local implement maker uses locally-
produced malleables entirely. He also uses a very
large quantity of locally-rolled bar, angle and tee-iron ;
sufficient to keep two fair-sized rolling-mills (one in
North Melbourne, the other in South Melbourne) running
most of the year. Unfortunately the raw material for
the founder and the ironmaster is not produced locally—
the one uses Scotch pig-iron, the other German or Ameri-
can “ dumped ” billets, in addition to such “ scrap ” as
can be obtained locally. The implement-makers employ
a lot of labor at a good wage. They need more protec-
tion against the cute Yank and Canuck, who ship
samples of their harvesters, &c., and makeup a better-
lookiiig machine at a lower price—by using more paint
and less metal. Note that rolling-mills and implement-
works commenced in a very small way, have expanded,
and will grow immensely if protected as in U.S.A. ; but
the production of pig-iron and billets is never likely tc
commence until the Federal Parliament declares for a
bonus. "

The latest singer to take a place-name for stage use is
Ben Digo (otherwise A. O. Bottoms), a baritone from
Bendigo, who appeared at the operatic matinee given by
Marshall-Hall students recently.
Ethel Turner ha 3 been done into drama—for the first
time, so far as writer knows. Journalist J. J. Utting, of
Hobart, who was the first man, or thereabouts, to put
Ned Kelly into four acts, has done the same service for
the Sydney writer. “ The Wonder Child ” wag the piece
chosen, and it was played at the Town Hall for sweet
charity’s sake.
George Musgrove has contradicted the rumor that lie
is abandoning theatrical management in Australia.
G.M. declares that he is travelling in search of new goods
for presentation at the theatres which he will continue to
lease, but he “ prefers to close the Princess’s when an
attraction worthy of the intellect of Melbourne or the.
traditions of the theatre cannot be obtained.” How
about the amateur companies who lately catered for Mel-
bourne’s intellect at the Princess’s ?
“0.P.”: The average song set down as a test in the
majority of Victoria’s musical competitions is of the kind
which, even if sung by a Crossley, weuld inspire the
audience to start out and look for the author with a club.
This probably accounts for the wild enthusiasm displayed
by some of the music warehouses in offering valuable
prizes and otherwise booming the show, which inci-
dentally gives them the opportunity of working off old
and unsaleable stock.
Miss Julia St. George died in England the other day,
aged 80. She was the foremost performer in the London
burlesques, extravaganzas, and spectacles of half a century
ago.
Thus a member of the Frawley co. to San Francisco
Dramatic Review: “The Frawley co., after giving
Sydney playgoers a glimpse of ‘ Arizona,’ ‘ Mizzouri ’ and
‘ Paradise,’ has gone to Queensland. Some critics object
to the 1 American twang’ where English gentlemen, as in
‘ Brother Officers,’ are represented. It is difficult to see,
however, how the situation could be improved by
substituting the mongrel cockney accent and twang of
the colonies, which prevails on both sides of the foot-
lights, and chills the marrow of every well-bred Ameri-
can when he finds himself in the company of equally well-
bred, but lamentably un-English Australians.”
Somebody protests that musicians are arrogantly im-
posing upon the public’s acquiescence in the rule of
concert management by which late-comers are kept out-
side in the corridors cooling their heels, whilst a musical
number” is proceeding. It was a good enough rule as
long as the programmes began with one or two short
numbers—songs for choice—but it is now carried to a
tyrannical point. A pianist starts his recital with a
sonata lasting 40 minutes, or a “popular” concert is
inaugurated with a2O minutes violin solo. All this time
the people who missed their trains and arrived at the hall
two minutes after the doors were shut are compelled to
do full penance. On the other hand no sort of penalty is
enforced against late-comers at theatres. The same
overture to “ Tannhaiiser,” say, which mustn’t be
disturbed during the performance in a concert hall, is
open to any amount of intrusion when it precedes the
opera at a theatre. Also the vocalist whose solo gets no
special consideration when it forms part of a big show at
a Williamson or Musgrove theatre, insists upon having
the same theme preserved from sacrilegious interruption
when it happens in a concert programme.
Melbourne police-court is finishing up the
old year with an interesting case. Some weeks
ago Melen Myers, jeweller, reported a burglary of
£3200 worth of goods from his shop in Elizabeth-
street, which had, apparently, been entered by
way of a hole cut through the ceiling from an
office above the shop. Myers alleges that about
half of the stolen property was discovered by him
(through the police) in the possession of Moss
Aarons, a Carlton pawnbroker, whom he had not
previously met. Aarons now stands charged with
the burglary, and the cross-examination of Melen
Myers by prisoner’s counsel promises to be in-
teresting.
Frank Tudor, who licked his Tory
opponent for the Yarra (Victoria) seat in the
House of Reps., was a fellow-debater of Holman,
M.L.A. (of N.S.W.), in the Richmond Noncon-
formist Debating Society, some years ago.
Anarchist poet Andrews was a member of the
society at the same time. Tudor is an earnest
young man with a great lack of humor, and it is
said that, in those days, when he failed to appre-
ciate a joke, he used to write it down, take it
home, and think it out carefully. A man who
will worry out a joke like that has courage and
perseverance.
A station laborer named Macdonald has been
tried three times for what is known as the Mooney
Mooney (Gundagai, N.S.W.) poisoning case, the
jury disagreeing on each occasion. At Young,
where the third trial took place, the accused was
remanded on bail (two sureties in £5OO each and
self in £500) to appear next sitting at Darling-
hurst, or such other court as the Attorney-General
may direct. Prohibitive, surely ? What station-
hand can provide £lOOO bail ?
Justice Hood, of Victoria, who has been
appointed a Royal Commission to inquire into
the charges of attempted bribery brought against
Dr. Montague, of Canada, in regard to the
introduction of assessment life assurance in
Australia, gets appointments of that kind
largely on account of his nose. It is a long
smelling talon darting out from beneath a big
forehead—the inquiring nose par excellence. And
Mr, Hood lives up to his nose.
Joe Panton, Victoria’s very ancient police
magistrate, got a shock during the hearing of an
assault case in connection with the sale of some
fat. After the defendant had given his evidence
Panton took a hand at cross-examining him.
“What are you?” asked Panton. “I buy fat,”
was the reply. “How long have you been a fat
merchant ? ” snarled Panton. “ Oh, a long time,”
came the answer. And then the defendant added
with a chuckle, “ I bought stuff off you, years
ago.” Panton was staggered for a minute, and
then roared, “You never did anything of the
kind. I have never been a butcher.” Defendant’s
solicitor tried to smooth things out a bit and re-
marked, “ I think, sir, he is telling the truth. He
told me the same thing last night.” “ But I
never sold fat in my life,” said the indignant
Panton. Then the defendant cleared up the
trouble in a sentence : “It wasn’t fat I bought off
you, sir. It was bottles, sir.” Panton squirmed
while the whole court rang with laughter.
Last week a man was discovered lying in
the sand within the ladies’ swimming bath enclo-
sure at Albert Park (Melb.) The cheek of inno-
cence had no occasion to blush on account of the
intruder. He was dead before a wave carried
him over the fence—probably the first dead man
that ever got into a sanctum for females without
human assistance.
