THE STORY OF A GREAT AUSTRALIAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ William M’Millan Reminiscent.—Personal and Poli-
tical Life Storv of a Successful Man of Affairs.— A Course
of Reading for Young Politicians.”— Headlines in Sydney
paper.
“My youthful life ? Oh, yes, of course; I’m very pleased
to give
My whole career, to show the young the way they ought
to live.
It may some embryonic ‘ Mac ’ induce to emulate,
And then—who knows?—he may become a Freetrade
leader great.
•• As near as memory can recall, my spirit always craved
To be the man of whom 'tis said, *He has his country
saved.’
Alas 1 I left my country young, to sail across the sea.
(Don’t smile, young man, I disapprove of all such levity.)
“My youth was spent in Derry, where they tint their
politics,
And I, just like a hatter, constituted one of six.
I’d all the lore of classic bards stored up within my head—
(No, young man, you are far too young to hear what Ovid
said.)
“ I went in strong for literature, and anything of Scott’s
Or Gowper’s or Macaulay’s or good old Dr. Watts’
I straightway held in memory, and even now I see
Before my mind those thrilling lines, ‘ The Little Busy
Bee.'
“ Oh 1 happy days of romance, when my soul's poetic
flow
Saw a sonnet in each bulky roll of unbleached calico,
When towels suggested triolets, and as I checked the sox,
I praised in simple melody my lady’s silken locks.
“ But still I felt my ardent soul was cribbed and sore con*
fined,
For dress-goods were material—and I was mostly mind.
I joined a famed debating class within the School of Arts,
With many other famous men who since have played their
parts.
“The joys of local politics now held me in their thrall,
And as an untried candidate I opened up the ball.
I came, I saw, I conquered (East Sydney was in luck),
And I upon the ladder rose above the common ruck.
“ My weight was felt in politics (I scaled at ten-stone-
two),
You recollect that Thistles Act I ably carried through?
An Export Act for Bandicoots, an Act of Lunacy,
And many other useful laws were introduced by me.
“ There’s nothing more of interest, except to say that I
Have left my leader, Mr. Reid, to keep the poodle dry.
I’ve taken now from Parliament, a temporary rest,
’Twas pocket vertus politics—and pocket came off best.
“ Now, do not hesitate at all to look me up again ;
I hold that famous men should make their whole life-
story plain.
Well, au revoir / you’re welcome quite. Don’t trouble
me to thank—
Ehl ‘what about that circular?”’ (Twelve asterisks
and a blank.)
JASON.
Heavy Damages,
An Irishman recently had the misfortune to be
in a railway accident, when both his legs and one
of his arms were broken. “Of course you will
sue the Company for damages,” observed his
sympathising friend, “Sue the Company for
damages, is it, y’re talking of?” inquired the
mutilated one. “Sure I’ve more than enough
damages already, I’m thinking ; but I’ll sue them
for repairs, or I’m not Mike Donovan from
Mayo.” . .
Now it is an unfortunate fact, constantly within
the range of everybody’s observation, that damages
are easily incurred, while repairs are nearly always
difficult to make, and in some cases impossible.
This is true of myriads of things, but specially is
t true of men and women.
What is more easy than to acquire indigestion by
following some sedentary occupation and eating
unsuitable food? Not but there are vast numbers
of people suffering from that complaint who are
at a loss how to account for it. But however
acquired or arising, what is more difficult than to
obtain relief from indigestion ? Nothing, surely.
Unlike money, it is easy to obtain and hard to get
rid of. Of the many things said to be a cure for
it, there is but one that is really so—Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, a compound of fruits, roots, and
herbs, for thirty-five years known and esteemed
throughout the world.
“ For four years indigestion blotted all bright-
ness out of my existence. It was in 1896 that I
fell a victim to that dreadful malady. My suffer-
ings were intense. Food, which should have
brought strength, was to me only a source of
distress.” Thus writes Mrs. Rose Marie, of 26
Foster-street, near the Haymarket, Sydney,
N.S.W. “Delicacies were out of the question,
and the little I ate had to be of the plainest,” con-
tinues Mrs. Marie. “Sleepless by night, I was
very subject to exhausting fits of vomiting on
rising in the morning. I was treated at different
stages of my complaint by several doctors, but
they did me no good whatever ; neither did I get
any benefit by the change of climate in going from
Adelaide to Melbourne. I took a great number
of patent medicines and tonics, but all to no
purpose, and every day grew feebler and more
despondent. In July, 1900, I was advised to try
Mother Seigel s Curative Syrup, and found the
effect remarkable. Before I had emptied two
bottles I felt like a different beiug. The sleepless-
ness, morning sickness, and other distressing
symptoms, disappeared, and in eight weeks I was
perfectly cured.”
A case of more rapid and successful repair we
think would be hard to find.*

worked. The general public, in spite of the spread of
technical education, remains densely ignorant, and seems

hold an outstretched hand. The spectator slipped his
liana into No. 4, and gazed with awe at what he thought
was a radiograph of his ow n bones. The exhibition was
a success until it struck somebody as strange that the
apparatus didn’t buzz, and he wanted me to supply the
buzzing part. I did so, and quite a number of shows
were equipped and sent round the country, coining
money while the novelty lasted.
Another man once offered me a half share not to burst
up an elaborate swindle that he had on hand. He pro-
fessed to brilliantly light a large shop with the current
from a small battery not capable of supplying the
illumination of a cigarette end. In reality a dynamo
driven by a 10 h.p. engine was 100 yards away in a back
yard, churning out current for all it was worth, and con-
veying the same to the lamps through concealed wires.
He was forming a syndicate (vendor £5OOO cash, etc.)
when a would-be shareholder got me to inspect the thing
for him. The shindykit didn’t eventuate.
Electric belts are perhaps the most profitable dodges
of all. They mostly consist of a series of metallic discs
fastened to a strip of thick flannel, or similar material.
Some consist of nothing else. Others are fitted with a
sulall medical coil and battery that give a genuine and
quite harmless current. Whether irritating the skin
with the tingling sensation such an arrangement gives
has a beneficial effect I cannot say, but should
not think it was of the least use. What really
does effect some of the widely - advertised cures
is the warmth of the flannel over the kidneys.
Prices range up to £2O for belts that do not contain 20s.
worth of work and material. I was once shown an
“ electric ’’ chair, sold for £3O by one of these sharks to a
poor paralytic, who gave up the savings of half a lifetime
on the promise of a certain cure. The chair cost 10s.,
the induction coil 155., the battery 45., and the work
done on it 2s. 6d. The sum total should have been worth
twelve months’ “ hard ” to the inventor.
“ Petramos ” tells how the bailiff buzzed :
I know every bad ’un in the city. There was a job I
’ad with Florrie Bradley—you know Florrie?—fine, big
girl down in Dash-street, used to live where Polly 'All is
now—you know Polly 'All? Florrie and the boys kept
the doors barred again me for seven weeks. We ’ad no
chance to get in for rent. Must be in ’tween six and six
for that. Finally, at 6 one morning, I paid a kid to go to
the door and holler “ Fire ! ” One of me men waited by
the front door, and when Flo came an’ opened it my man
jumped in. “Atlarst!” he says. Florrie was joined in
the ’allwav by the rest of the girls in their early mornin’
gowns, with their ’air all ’angin’ down their necks. They
let the door slide, and I slipped in too. The excitement
was tremenjous, and the things they throw’d at us was
numerous. I’m goin’ down to turn Milly Frazer out now.
You know 'er?—short, fine-made girl. Oh, well, I
s’pose you can’t remember ’em all.
Queensland’s judicial position just now is
remarkable. It has four Higher Court Judges,
but two of them —stationed in the Central and
Northern divisions—are expressly shut out from
sitting on the Full Court. There used to be three
Judges in Brisbane; but as nobody has been
appointed to succeed Griffith, although Pope
Cooper has been made Chief, the Full Court
consists of only Cooper and Real. The latter has
publicly announced that he reckons himself a
better man than the new Chief, and that he ought
to have had the job ; but as he hasn’t got the job
and Pope Cooper has, the latter is practically the
Full Court, being Real’s Chief. And as Real
doesn’t regard Pope Cooper as being large enough
to sit in judgment upon his judgments, he won’t
send cases to the Full Court, except for the
express purpose of having them sent on to the
Privy Council or the High Court. Real has been
a prickle on the Bench for years, and his recent
disappointment makes him more prickly than
ever.
“ Anglo-American ” in the North Ameri-
can Review :
I feel sure that if one could follow the workings of the
caste system into its uttermost details one would find
that the hopelessness and servility bred by it are respon-
sible for, perhaps, half the commercial inefficiency and un-
progressiveness of England. It makes for stagnation as
certainly as it makes for that class rancor which gives to
English trades-unionism its particularly bitter strength.
At one point in the social scale you may find its fruits in
the worship of externals and appearances, in an over-
valuation of the purely decorative, non-productive
elements of life. At another it will be repressing and
circumscribing the ability of the “ vulgar " in favor of
genteel incompetence ; at a third you will see it spouting
in geysers of flunkeyism. Between King Edward VII. on
his Royal Throne and the London “ floor-walker ” who
makes you shiver with the abjectness of his bowings and
scrapings the connection of cause and effect may not at
first be apparent. It is there, disastrously there, all the
same; and the caste spirit is the link. When the
monarchy sets the example of governing, rewarding, be-
having with a single eye to merit, there is no room and
no temptation on the lower strata for slimy servility.
When the Royal influence, however, tends palpably in
the other direction, it will breed flunkeys as the New
Jersey marshes breed mosquitoes.
Sometimes I say in an hour
supreme that the steak is dash good, or that the
green peas discover me where I reside ; whereupon
my helpmate smiles compassionately at me like a
lady in a corset advt. I think that woman is
jealous of man’s stomach, and, if she could get
the chance, would take his digestive machinery
away from him and fill the gap with a secondary
heart, Man, on the contrary, admires a woman
who feeds herself—so long as she takes him as well
as the food.
The relative importance of God and King
Ned, as seen by one of them. The quote is from
the London Da,ily Chronicle report of the laying
of the foundation-stone of a sanatorium :
The King himself set his subjects an excellent example
by wearing his hat during the whole of the brief religious
service in the open air, and raising it only when the
National Anthem was played.
Edward didn’t take off his hat when religion was
mentioned, but when his own name was uttered —
well, that was different.

Choose
Your Future
At Fifty,
Which Will
You
Look Like?
Do
It
Now
SUFFERING MEN.
You daily stand before the
Mirror of Life. Yearly you see
yourselves grow older, stronger, or weaker. You
know whether you are degenerating or other-
wise. If at twenty you are feeble in health and
puny in stature, you can guess what you will be
like at fifty. Look at the faces in these Mirrors!
WHAT WILL YOU BE LIKE AT FIFTY?
This is the Question, d® you mean to get the
_ best of the world, or will
you let the world get the best of you? Sooner or later you must understand
yourselves, and decide whether you will climb up hill to success, or slide down
hill to failure. No one can stand still. Now is the time for you to choose your
future. Do not dodge the question, procrastinate, or allow yourself to aimlessly
drift towards premature decay and Death. DECIDE TO LIVE—to rise to the
top of your chosen profession.
Good Health is Necessary.
To be successful in Life
you must be healthy.
If you are weak in Vital Strength, Puny, Tired, Afraid to Venture, if you have lost
Hope, Ambition, Energy, you will not succeed. You will leave the world worse than
you entered it. If you are ill, nerve-shattered, incompetent to perform Life’s duties,
Tired of Life, if your Kidneys are deranged, your Stomach disordered, your Heart
degenerated from its healthy standard, if you have specks and cobwebs before your eyes (a
sure sign of Kidney Disease), if you have Presentiments of Coming Evil, and mentally live
under a cloud of impending disaster, and have fear every moment of sudden death, you are
in a bad way. Go to a Medical Expert at once while you have time. There are none better
practising in Australia than the SPECIALISTS of the Medical Staff of
The Freeman & Wallace
MEDICAL INSTITUTE
who have cured more men of their troubles than any similar association of medical men in
Australasia. Each expert connected with the Institute is a graduate of famous English and American
Colleges, and have practised in Australasia for over a Quarter of a Century.
If you are ill you must go to the Fountain Head of Science. It offers you a chance to
regain Health. Why allow yourself to go on from day to day regardless of your Health and
Strength ? Why make life miserable when you can have Health and Happiness 1 You can obtain
this without any inconvenience, regain your Vitality, and find a joy in living and your day’s duties a
pleasure No man need be sick when these world-famed Specialists may be consulted. Diseases long
classed as “ INCURABLE ” are their Specialities. They particularly desire to treat those diseases
others have aggravated by their tampering and failed to cure. Thousands of men have testified to the
wonderful cures made by the Medical Staff in cases where medical men have entirely failed to relieve.
The Proverb says: “He
who hesitates is lost.”
Do not hesitate if you
suffer. Men are buried
daily who thought they
would call on a Special-
ist, and always kept de-
laying the main chance.
In order to meet the great growth of public patronage
accorded the Institute by suffering men from all parts of
the Commonwealth, a branch of this great medical centre
has been opened at Bourke and Elizabeth Streets, Mel-
bourne, under efficient professional and correspondence
staffs, where patients in the Southern and Western States
of Australia may consult the most eminent Specialists
and save the long journey to Sydney. All correspondence
to this Branch will be effectively replied to.
Remember! Consulta-
tion by litter or person-
ally costs you nothing.
You wbuld not throw
away a grain of gold.
The aid of a Specialist is
worth more than gold
to you if you but knew it.
AFTER fIANY YEARS.
Mr. JAMES LANE, farmer, of Rose Hill, Orange, was
a martyr for over THIRTY YEARS to Sciatica. No
doctor could cure him. They told him he was “ IN-
CURABLE.” His wife says of his recovery £-
“ Mr. Lane was in bed during; last winter, could
only walk with the aid of a stick. After adopting;
your treatment he could ride round his -farm for
his cows, i think everyone should know of his
cure, for he had tried many doctors, and had not
obtained relief. He is now well and strong;."
CHRONIC DYSPEPSIA CURED.
Mr. CHARLES BROWN, Newcomen-street, New-
castle, who suffered from Indigestion and Dyspepsia for
many years, and who scarcely knew what it was to eat
a hearty meal, writes: —
“ I cannot praise your treatment too much. At
one time I could not eat a meal with good
appetite. Your systems of treating nerve
diseases has done me good, for I now eat and
sleep well. That is more than I have done for
many years. I am quite healthy to-day.”
HAD GIVEN UP HOPE.
Mrs. JULIA CLEMENTS, of Stawell, Victoria, who
was physically a wreck, and who had given up hope in
every way, and who had consulted many leading doctors
fruitlessly declares:—
“No one knows the value of your Electro-
Medical methods as I do. I was a wreck and
utterly hopeless, but through your treatment I
am now a strong woman. I do not suffer as I
did from the fearful pains in the back, which I
thought would never leave me.”

“ H.P.” : Re the birth-rate problem, if
there is one. lam just recovering from an
attack of Sairey Gampism. Believe that “ pusson”
is responsible for much of the declining birth-rate
trouble. Instead of trying to encourage “trade,”
Mrs. Gamp appears to take a savage delight in
making the conditions of life so awful during her
reign that the thing doesn’t happen again. During
our recent infliction, there were five distinct
Gamps in the house before we were through.
No. 1 came along with the medical man.
While assisting him Bhe fainted, and had to be
laid flat in the passage with her head sticking out
of the open door to catch the breeze, until she
recovered sufficiently to have some refreshments.
Then she went home. Costs, 10s. and the value
of the “refreshments.” No. 2 came. She had
sore feet, and left home without her specially-
manufactured foot-wear. She got the necessary
articles after a time, but too late to save her (ana
us). She would not do anything, but stayed on
until we got another nurse, “just to oblige us.”
Item, one sore-footed Gamp for three days, 255.
No. 3 was a huge, unwieldy monster in a lilao
print dress, and with a wall eye. She was of the
“born tired” kind. After tne trying ordeal of
getting her patient a cup of tea, she required an
hour’s rest. She never put anything in its place
after using it. Everything went into the bath-
room. That room is only about 6xB, but the
amount of bric-a-brac that woman stowed into
it clearly indicated that she had missed her
calling. She should have been a furniture-
packer. We stood the frenzied glare of her wall-
eye for about ten days. Then, fortunately, the
bathroom got full up, and having to walk further
with the rest of our household effects caused her
to go “bung” in the legs, and she retired.
Luckily, her hands remained sound, or we would
have had to send a messenger to carry home her
feet. Price of this megatharium, 50s. No. 4
came at great personal inconvenience, and only on
condition that she slept at home. We began to
miss sundry goods and chattels out of the house
about this time. We did not worry much, how-
ever, but remarked, “Oh ! it will turn up when
we dig the bathroom out.” But, in many instances,
it didn’t. We think we know something now.
Never engage a nurse who “ must go home every
night.” Never engage a nurse who has a wall-eye
and a lilac dress. Never engage one with sore
feet, or one who faints. In short, never require
one at all. No. 4 cost 40s. Total cost of Gamp,
£6 ss. in cash, and about as much more on account
of incompetence, going home at night, &c.

The mangle, in an emergency, makes a good
weapon of defence, ranking for effectiveness with
the mitrailleuse, life-preserver, tram-car, battle-
ship and judgment summons. Recently a posse
of bailiffs attacked the residence of a Melbourne
gentleman, who lived on the second floor of a
flat-building; and were repulsed, with great loss,
by means of the said laundry implement. Stand-
ing at the head of the stairs, the man and his
wife, without showing themselves, pushed the
mass of ironmongery over, and it did the rest,
falling on the enemy like a Papal excommunica-
tion. When the chief writtist arose slowly, with
the upper portion of the clothes-stiffening machine
around his neck, he renounced the bailiff profes-
sion in a solemn voice for ever. Then he and his
minions crept silently out of the building, leaving
the unserved document and the disrupted mangle
on the field of battle. They hadn’t even the
satisfaction of being able to charge anybody
definitely with assault. No one dared to go up
and identify the foe for fear of more mangles.

The Justice of Mandhar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A shobt man — brown and grizzled and sailor-
like—he sat on a gin-case and smoked and said
nothing, till the trader, having solaced himself
with an inch or two of rum, handed over the
bottle.
“Have a tot, old man, and wake up ! Blest if
you’re any better company than that stone chap
up there.”
Micklejohn looked up at the little black image.
“That’s Siva!” he said—“ Siva, the Destroyer.
Seen him before many a time. India ! Where’d
you get him ? ”
“Bought him —’n’half -a - dozen other gods,
’n’devils, ’n’things—from a Javani chap that came
over here from Madura with Runjal Sooda last
trip.”
Micklejohn jumped off his gin-case. “ A Java-
man ? Where’s he now ? What’s he like ? What
brought him to Borneo ? ”
“What brought him here? Dunno! What
he’s like y’can see for y’self, for here he comes ! ”
Smash went the little man’s clay on the floor,
as he darted round the hardware-cases. “Dark’s
the word, Fleming ; I want to have a look at this
chap before he sees me ! ”
“Right! ” said the trader, squinting under the
counter to see that his shooter was handy. He
had been too long in “ Malarky ” * to be surprised
at anything.
The Javani camer^in —short and bandy-legged,
but long of arm and square as a Bugi buckler.
His white cotton jacket lay open on his burly
chest; in his scarlet waistband was the inevitable
kris, with his hand upon the hilt. This Fleming
noticed, and marked, too, how the eyes glanced
Bwiftly here and there from beneath the rim of
the rattan hat.
“ Micklejohn’s man, for a tael! ” he said to
himself. “ Hard case, too. High old shine
directly ! ”
The customer wanted a revolver—a good one.
“ A self-cocker! ” he explained, in Malay, and
Fleming brought down a case. “ This is the sort
of thing you want. Good pistol, but dear!
Twenty-five dol Great Sc Hold on, man !
hold on to his wrists, till I collar his sticker ! ”
then Fleming was across the counter, and the
three rolled over and under and round about—
Micklejohn fast upon the Javani’s wrists and
Fleming snatching vainly at the kris, till from
the little crowd about the door a Dyak boy
rushed in and jerked it from the scabbard.
“ What’n blazes have we got hold of, anyhow ? ”
said the panting trader, as he sat upon the
trussed-up enemy. “The little brute’s dam near
as strong as me ! —’n’ that’s more’n any man in
Dobang can say.”
The captive’s jacket had been torn off in the
scrimmage. Micklejohn pointed to the muscle-
ridges on the back and shoulder. “ Ever hear of
Samatang ? ”
“Let’s see! Oh yes!—course I have. Sama-
tang, the wrestler! But, didn’t he get into
trouble a month or two back ? Killed somebody,
didn’t he, over the other side ? At Sourabaya, I
think it was.”
“Yes! There hadn’t been a man in the port
could wrestle him till I was there in the Tantallon
Castle. Then my mate, Tom Ballantyne, took
him in hand and made a holy show of him.
So this chap waits his chance, and the very
night before we were to sail he lets Tom have it,
over the shoulder, from behind —you know the
style.”
“Ay, I know! Killed him?”
“Lived half-an-hour ! So I swore I’d fix the
fellow for it, and I gives the old Tantallon the
slip, and sets to work to hunt him up. Found
he’d bolted over here, and followed him ; but I
didn’t think I’d drop across him so soon.”
“ Sure of your man ? ”
“Ay ! —and lie’s sure of me, too ! Look at his
face ! Salamat pagi, my friend —I’ve got you at
last ! ” ,
“Hum!” said Fleming. “ You ve got him,
eert’nly—but what you’re going to do with him’s
another matter.”
“Do with him? Get him tried, of course!
Won’t the Dutchies try him forme?”
Fleming cackled. “ Some of you sailor men’s
too bloomin’ innocent to go about without a dry-
nurse ! Why, you haven’t got a single witness
except yourself. You don’t s’pose the Mynheers’d
hang him on your bare word, do you?—even if
they tried him ? But they won’t. They let the
Raja of Dobang captain his own ship. Pity you
didn’t wait, ’n’ put a bullet through Mr. Sama-
tang on the quiet ? ”
“ Murder, that would be ! What I want is
justice /” _ )
“Not much difference, sometimes! Matters
out of your hands now, in any case; here come
the Raja’s men to collar the pair of you. I’ll go,
too. I know Mandhar Alam a bit, and I can
patter Malay with the best of ’em.”
* » * *
When the Hollanders —because they couldn’t
help it —“ conceded ” to Mandhar Alam the right
to manage his own people in his own way, they
said it was because he was a wise man and a just.
At which notion Mandhar had been hugely
tickled, for wise he certainly was not, except in
knowing that he wasn’t; while, as for his “jus-
tice,” it was at any man’s disposal—upon the
most unreasonable terms. But he had a rough-
and-ready way of settling cases in which no coin
was to be extracted from either party, and, said
he, in re Micklejohn versus Samatang—
“Let them be cast together unto the other
Raja. Both he cannot seize at once, and, while
he devoureth one, let the other be taken out
again!—it will be the finger of Allah.”
The “other Raja” had, many a year before,
been placed—a six-foot infant—in an ancient
tank, wherein—sustained with dog, and pig, and
goat, and now and then with man—he had grown
to monstrous size and super-saurian sagacity.
* * * *
A great day in Dobang ! From Sura —from the
villages of the Jamir—even across from Moda
Island, the people had flocked to see crocodilus
dine once more upon homo.
The tank, of antique Javani workmanship, was
faced with stone throughout its square of 80 yards
a-side, save at the spot where, forced aside by the
roots of a giant umbrella tree, the wall gave access
to a huge sub-aqueous cavity—the sanctum of the
scaly Highness.
The two-legged Highness spoke. God was
great, and he, the Raja, was just. Let God
decide, and the people be satisfied. “ There is no
God but God !—and Mahomet is His prophet!
Bring forth the men ! ” ’
Naked, both—with girded waists and coir
lines attached. The white man dazed and
unrealising; the yellow man—realising to the full
—fatalistically indifferent. In the middle of the
tank, just peeping from the oily surface, a rugged
ridge of grey showed where the mighty lizard
waited. He knew ! It was not the first time his
hideous, bone-roofed eyes had looked upon a crowd
like this.
“ I will count,” said Mandhar, when the cap-
tives had been placed upon opposite sides of the
tank, nearly equidistant from the scaly horror.
“ At the word ‘ five ’ —push ! ”
“ Satu !” he began— “ Dual — Tiga ! — Ampatl”
—the fifth word was lost in the splash, as both
men struck the water at the same instant.
Into vigorous life at the sound started the log-
like brute in the middle, and over he came with a
steam-launch ripple towards the yellow man. The
crowd held its breath —then broke into shrill
Asiatic acclamation as the reptile’s wide-sweeping
tail brought him round again and urged him for-
wards upon the white man. But then again he
turned, embarrassed with his riches, and, as he
hung hesitating and churning up the water, there
was another splash. Mandhar Alam—swearing
roundly by the Koran that here was the rarest
sport he had ever seen—had missed his footing and
furnished the undecided reptile with yet another
possibility.
Pandemonium, then, of shriekings and swear-
ings and rushings. The captives’ waist-lines were
let go, and then came plunge upon plunge, as the
pressure from behind sent half-a-dozen of his sub-
jects to keep company with their floundering
ruler, whose heavy gold embroidery would soon
have sunk him had not Micklejohn swum over
and gripped him. Then somebody hooked up
the white man’s waist-line, and the pair were
hauled out together.
Said the potentate, when he had got his breath
—“ God is great! and thou art Mandhar’s brother!
Let the Javani pig be taken out, bound hand and
foot, and cast in again.”
But Samatang—noting that the babel of shouts
and shots had scared the astounded crocodile back
to his private apartment —had swiftly climbed
the broken stonework above it, and was gone !
* * » *
“ Saved him ! Ay ! but it was only to save
myself. I’d a deal sooner have shoved the cruel-
hearted dog under water. Called me his brother,
too—l’d as soon be Samatang’s ! S’pose the
tigers ’ll get him / ”
“No tigers in Borneo, old man.”
“Plenty, Fleming—on two legs. Curse the
place ! I’ll clear!"
ALEX. MONTGOMEBY.
* The Malay Archipelago.
“ E.” : Wandered into a cafd, kept by one
Mahmoud, attracted by the Turks ranged round
the counter. Expected a thrill of Asia—
divans, marble fountains, iced syrups, real coffee,
choice confectionery, an elaborate narghile, or at
least exotic cigarettes. The Turks had nothing.
They sold simply ice cream, lemon squashes, shakes,
bad lollies—and me. Melbourne has contracted
a bad habit of reducing everything to dull, drab-
colored, prosaic uniformity. There are touches of
Bohemianism in Sydney, none in Melbourne.
French dinners, Eastern confectionery, American
drinks, Greek cafes—one seeks for them in vain.
Everybody seems to eat, drink, and smoke the
same. Nobody tries to be original. The swell
establishments are all built on the same heavy
English lines. And even on the Flat, on race
days, in the “Hawkers’ Reserve,” about 10 acres of
it, one can procure nothing except eternal squash
and saveloy-and-roll. In time all Melbourne citi-
zens will wear the one-colored tie, eat the one
jam, swear the one swear, and be buried in the
one grave. The place is Desolation. lam going to
Geelong for new sensations.
The Rouseabout.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a humble hut, on a scrubby flat,
Near the land of the setting sun,
Li ved a simple but honest rouseabout,
Who rejoiced in the name of Dunn.
He could warble as sweet as a bandicoot,
He could dance like a kangaroo,
His age, it was just about four feet-ten,
And his height about thirty-two.
He worshipped a beautiful female maid
Who lived on a distant plain ;
Whose husband had gone to a far-off land,
And had never come back again.
She had bright blue hair j she had rosy eyes,
And her cheeks were of golden hue.
So Tommy set off, as the sun went down,
To tell her he loved her true.
He traversed the hills and the mountain peaks,
He climbed up a rugged plain ;
He swam through the beds of the dried-up creeks
And he tramped o’er the raging main.
He saw not the wind on the distant hills;
He heard not the rising moon ;
For his soul was dead, and his burning head
Was as calm as a big monsoon.
His eye, like a hurricane, roared aloud;
His voice, like the lightning, flashed ;
The blustering blizzard it boomed and burst
As on through the dust he splashed.
He rode on a flea-bitten chestnut mare,
With a patent pneumatic tyre;
And the sparks from the feet of his flying steed
Set Billabong Creek on fire.
He leapt from the train at the half-way house,
And stood at the maiden’s door;
He wept at the sight of that dear old spot
Which he never had seen before;
He stood on his head at the maiden’s feet,
And he begged her his lot to share,
Then, brushing a tear from his glist’ning ear,
He spoke of his dumb despair.
“ See ! see ! ” he exclaimed to the winsome maid,
In syllables tall and sweet,
“ The whole of my expectations I cast
At thy beautiful, blushing feet.
For you I would live—through Eternity !
Say ‘ yes ’ —for my own sweet sake,
And without a murmur I’ll sacrifice
All the millions I hope to make.”
Then the maiden rested her blushing nose
For a moment on Tommy’s chest,
And she said, as she cuddled his crumpled form
To her soft and capacious breast,
“ As I have been true in the years to come,
I’ll be true in the past,” said she.
And she winked her ear at a native bear
That was perched on a pumpkin tree.
N.S.W.

Cinderella.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My dear, our honeymoon
Will soon be ended —
The bills came in too soon—
But it was splendid.
Ghosts, in this moonlight rare,
Talk lawyer’s Latin;
Yet you sit smiling there
In olive satin.
You have indeed the fine,
Expensive graces;
And like a dream divine
Your proud, white face is.
That jewelled fan you swing
To scare off sorrow—
I wonder what ’twill bring
When pawned to-morrow.
My dear, I know that you
Could conquer Hades,
And, with a fashion new,
Charm fair, dead ladies.
Gudrun, who was a snare
For heroes olden,
Would envy you your hair—
So long, so golden.
Helen, with finer wit,
Would be too glad to
Steal shining strands from it
Her own to add to.
Venus herself would leave
Her own bright planet
From your moist lips to thieve
Their red pomegranate.
Your beauty and your grace
The gods might fetter—
Yet they cannot efface
A lawyer’s letter.
The stars shine glorious
As if they’d planned a •
Last Lantern Show for us
On God s verandah.
Sweet, while with joy and woe
We sit here juggling,
There is a Soul below
With beetles struggling.
While you gazed on the scene,
In your own rare way,
Her head you might have seen
Shine on the stairway.
Around her eyes are dear.
Brown little freckles —
To one with eyesight clear
. Worth many shekels.
She is a Rhodope—
And you can trust a
Small thing like this to me,
My dear Augusta.
You know our prospects, Gus
(God save the marker !),
Are dark as Erebus—
Her hair is darker.
And she has eyes that shine —
Swift-sparkling, stellar—
Like jewels in a mine,
And light her cellar.
Her head is full of plans,
And she is rich in
Dreams, while she scours the pans,
And scrubs the kitchen.
To her there come at dawn
Four crystal coaches,
And they, my dear, are drawn
By gold cockroaches.
The first coach holds the Prince —
Of German brood is
He, and his clothes evince
His credit good is.
Dear, we know—l and you—
And I could tell a
Tale beautiful and true
To Cftiderella.
One heart she has to give,
And once to give it,
One life she has to live,
And once to live it.
Here comes the Landlord ... So
He will not trust a
Week longer. We must go,
My dear Augusta.
N.S.W.
V.J.D.
A Bush Tragedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A couple- of children sat on the wooden step of
a galvanised iron hut. The elder, a boy of ten,
had found the day long and uninteresting, and
wished heartily for his mother’s return, for
the sister of three, left in his charge, bored him
considerably. “Oh, go and chivey ’oppers yer-
self,” he ejaculated, impatiently, at last, as she
resumed her insatiable demand for fresh supplies
of grasshoppers. He threw himself back on the
steps, his bare, sun-browned legs waving in the
air, his old hat well over his eyes.
Time sped away quickly, and he was not con-
scious of its flight till a wild, terrified cry recalled
him to his senses. He started to his feet and
ran through the tussocks towards the creek
whence the lamentation came. The baby stood
knee-deep in the long grass, her arms extended
towards him, and her little face convulsed with
crying. .
“ Billie, it bit me —a snake bit me.”
“Stop that row! Wot’s the good o’ hollerin ? ’
said Billie, practical always. “ W’ere did it bite
yer ? ” The child twisted round a plump, grimy
arm.
“By cripes!” said Billie, “ it’s a pisoner, all
right.” There were two small punctures on her
wrist. Billie knew the marks.
“ Billie, don’t let me die.” The baby mind had
partly realised the horror. They had always told
her, “ If a snake bites yer, yer’ll die.”
One had bitten Collie and he died. It was only
last month, and the baby remembered it all.
, ‘‘By gum, no,” said Billie, thoughtfully.
Maybe I’d best cut the bit out.”
Such a comforting suggestion could hardly fail
to draw forth another shriek of terror.
“By gum,” said Billie, “you’re a coward—
worse’n a Chow.” He thought hard. Perhaps
he could go for the doctor. He remembered one
case when the doctor had been sent for, and the
patient recovered. He began to feel quite
relieved.
“Yer most likely won’t feel nothin’, if the
doctor cuts it out; I’d best fetch ’im.”
Billie, in home ways, was old with the pre-
cocity of a bush-born boy; a mere baby in others.
“ ’Urry up, quick, an’ come along inside.” he said.
“ I want my ’oppers, Bill.” She pulled away
fro in him, and running back hastily picked up an
old fish tin. “ A lot o’ my ’oppers has gone,” she
wailed, looking into the tin; “ Oh, Bill! ”
“ Come on,” said Billie, “ never mind the old
’oppers. Wot odds about them? Yer’ve got to
stop in the kitching, mind that, an’ I’m goin’ to
catch the brown pony and fetch the doctor.”
“ Don’t go way, Billie.”
“ Stop hollerin’, I say ! I must! Yer don’t
want to die, do yer ? ”
Women had to be spoken to like that, Billie
knew. They couldn’t have so much sense as a
man. He lifted his sister into a box which stood
on the floor, while she clung tightly to the tin
with its few remaining “hoppers.”
“Now,” he said, “you’ve got to stop in there,
mind that.” He rushed to the cupboard and cut
a large slice of bread, which he spread with a
plentiful supply of dripping, and brought it to
the child. It is significant that he did not think
of himself. Bush men are like that. He 'then
securely fastened the window, and going out did
the same to the door on the outside.
The brown pony proved troublesome to catch.
Billie was very hot and tired from running before
he managed to slip a broken bridle over its head.
An old sugar bag served for a saddle, and with
his short legs reaching a very little way on either
side of his steed, Billie set out for a ten-mile
ride. Once the pony got going he was hard to
stop. Billie, having no stirrups, found it difficult
to get a pull on the bridle. The pony threw
down his head, and a dozen times Billie was
nearly over his ears. It grew dark before he
reached the township, and the boy found it hard
to keep to the track. Then the moon rose, the
glorious, Australian, summer moon, and by its
light the way became clear. It was eight o’clock
when the doctor, standing by his little gate to
get a breath of cool air, saw Billie ride furiously
up to the pub opposite. The child slipped to the
ground, and with the air of a man flung his bridle
to the nearest idler.
“Come quick; our young un’s bit with a
snake,” he said, simply. A few minutes later, a
light buggy was spinning quickly over the road
Billie had ridden. Billie occupied a place on the
seat, and kept up the speed by incessant urging,
though the road was bad, and more than once
they narrowly escaped a capsize.
In time, Billie explained how it was that, his
mother having gone for rations, he alone had been
in charge. Then he added, earnestly, “You’ve
got to drive quicker’n yer doin’, or the kid’ll be
stiff’nin’ afore we gets there.” The doctor gasped,
and applied the whip again.
At last, the drive was over. At the sound of
wheels, a long-limbed woman came to the door-
way. The light behind her showed all her angles.
She held a child close in her arms, and called out,
“ Who’s that ? ” in a voice that matched the un-
graciousness of her figure.
“Oh, Lord, Lord l ” she moaned, when the
doctor had explained the circumstances. “No
sooner is one trouble over but another begins.
Oh, Lord ! ”
The doctor followed her in, and examined the
child by the light of a tallow candle. The heavy,
lethargic sleep which follows snake-bite had
already the little one in its clutches. The lips
were blue and slightly swollen, the eyelids heavy
when he tried to raise them with his fingers. He
feared ihe case was beyond his skill. Neverthe-
less,, he drew his syringe, and injected strychnine.
Then, by his orders, the mother put the child into
a tub of hot water, and they forced stimulants
between her lips. Billy had, unaided, taken the
horse out of the shafts, and stood by, an interested
spectator.
But as the night wore on weariness overpowered
the boy, and he fell asleep on a bench in the
corner. The room was hot and airless, though the
doctor had caused door and windows to be opened
vyide. The child had not regained consciousness,
and every effort to rouse her seemed cruel as well
as useless.
Towards 3 o’clock a curlew called dismally ;
then for the first time the woman broke down.
“ It’s the last girl I ’ad left,” she sobbed bitterly,
“and to-morrow there’ll be another grave to dig.”
Her thin body was shaken by sobs. The doctor
tried t<3 cheer her, but he was young, and at a loss
how to act in presence of such grief.
When Billie awoke it was daylight; the doctor
had gone.
A large box which stood under the window had
been turned on its side, and covered with a white
sheet. There was something on the box. Billie
would not look; he dared not go near, but shrank
back on his bench.
_ “Billie, dear, come and get yer breakfast,” said
his mother’s voice. There was a difference in the
voice—Billie knew. He slid from the bench and
moved slowly to the table. His mother had cut
large slices of bread and dripping, but, in spite of
his supperless evening, he could not eat. At last
he turned away and went drearily towards the
fireplace, where he picked up an old fish-tin, at
the bottom of which were still some half-dead
grasshoppers. Then he vaguely felt that there
was a last duty owing to the sister he had bullied
and loved. Billie scarcely knew he had loved her
for that matter.. He carried the tin disconsolately
out into the sunshinp. Down by the bank of the
creek he found a soft place in the ground, where
he dug hard with a blunt stick, and buried the
tin and the grasshoppers deep in the hole. Then
he sat down beside it and looked dully at the
creek,, and wondered why God made snakes.
N. CAMPBELL.
A tip for N.S.W. Education Minister
Perry :
When a single male teacher is sent to a bush school the
mothers of the locality who have eligible daughters
tumble over each other to offer him board. Successful
caterer always tries to make out that he is smitten with
the charms of her ’Melia Ann, and wants to marry her.
Others get jealous, and poor pedagogue suffers. This is
responsible for so many removals among the male teachers
in charge of small schools.

At Poverty Point.
“F.” : I saw a politician listening eagerly at the lates
musical comedy, penoil in hand. “ I’m looking for gooc
gags to work off on the electors,” he explained. Coming
out that politician had a disappointed look. The show
hadn’t panned out well.
“E.”: Rickards’show is poor, very poor, at present.
Too many “ clever” turns. I want somebody to sing a
good comic or character song. That’s all. But unfortu-
nately you can get a hundred to do what seems the
impossible to one who can do what appears dead easy.
Simplicity is one of the rarest qualities of art. And you
find tons of Albertos, who can turn beer into water and a
green handkerchief into a grand old rag, to a solitary
Peggy Pryde who simply tells you about the lodger who
took Smith’s missus to the seaside.
“ Weib.” : We have plenty of amateur talent. The
Shakspeare Society has just been evoking it in a remark-
able fashion at a soiree. -Yet the dramatic clubs do
nothing but rot. .In the last generation, or the one
before last, Melbourne had its Histrionic Club,-which
acted only plays of a high stamp, such as Bulwer’s
“Money.” What a fine Alfred Evelyn was Wm. Levy.
H. A. Bruce, as Graves, was better than any professional
to be had. Then the Garrick Club would stage even
“Hamlet,’' with a certain excellent forgotten Batten as
the Dane, or Sam Banks, the-compositor, would do Shy-
lock. On one of Shakspeare’s birthdays this club pre-
sented a tableau of his leading characters, with architect
Billy Pitt, of the Garrick’s Head, as Shakspeare. The
■Oddfellows’ Dramatic Club went for the highest game,
too. Zox made his debut with it, and Zox was an actor
all his life. His favorite character was a benevolent Jew
with a white vest.
“ H,” : Maskelyne partly writes the article on “ Magic”
in the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” supplement. He gives
away his played-out illusions. He had an indispensable
advantage through being in exclusive occupancy of the
London Egyptian Hallr It took months to prepare cer-
tain tripks. Nobody could possibly do them with a mere
fit-up at a. travelling show. One of the simplest mysteries
was Cleopatra’s Needle. A big imitation of it was placec
on a stand. The audience was satisfied that there coulc
be nothing underneal h but the four legs, Anybody might;
come and lift the needle, which seemed to be as light as
paper. Yet Maskelyne produced three men out of it!
Quite easy. The men were there al? the time. When
anybody proceeded to lift the needle, an apparatus in
the room above pulled it up, by invisible \yires,
Another trick of a ridiculously simple kind occupied the
combined genius of Maskelyne and De Kolta for a
long time. The stage showed a big cabinet hung with
olack velvet. No possibility of mirrors, or any communi-
cation with the outside. Yet people appeared and disap-
peared inside before the very eves of the audience. All
done by drawing black velvet over them. Maskelyne
advertised £500 reward for anyone who could imitate his
box trick. A conjuror did so, but could not work with
Maskelyne’s box, on which ground Maskelyne refused to
pay. The imitator sued him at law. The first jury
disagreed. The second decided against Maskelyne, and
so did the House of Lords, on appeal. He had to pay.

George Musgrove says that altogether he made £3000
out of ‘ Tannhauser,” which Wagnerian opera is also the
biggest money-maker in the Land of Sauerkraut and
Lagerbier.
“N.”. “Sweet Nell of Old Drury" has naturally
turned many to read up history, with a glow over the
present Dukedoms of St. Albans, Richmond, and Grafton.
The dukes have been hugely endowed by the sympathetic
taxpayers. They were amid the proudest at the corona-
tion, because, you see, they had a family interest in it.
What do the bastard ducal clergy preach about ? Well,
the respect due from the lower orders to the Marquis of
Carabbas. Australia being an integral part of the Empire
with England, we must exact proper loyalty to those
dukes here. Deakin has not a proper reverence for the
Dukedoms of St. .Albans, Richmond, and Grafton, in
refusing a knighthood. We can imagine him saying in a
rebellious, if not treasonable, anti-British spirit, “ Do you
think I am going to label myself as inferior to those
bastards?” They are in the seventh heaven. An
Australian knight is only in the first. He gazes humbly
up half-a-dozen flights of back stairs to the dukes of
Charles II. Even Deakin will have to be a good deal
more illustrious before they recognise him. A Cham-
berlain is hardly good enough, though I have seen
Chamberlain and the Duke of St. Albans in one photo.
“ Melb.” : Christmas pantomime seemed an immortelle
at the Melbourne Royal. In 1855, when only half a year
old, this house had the panto, of the “Magician's
Daughter, or Harlequin King of the Golden Island." In
1856 Coppin had the Royal, where his Christmas panto,
was “ Harlequin Rule of Three and Pretty Practice.” In
1857 he gave “ Dick Whittington,” in which Fanny Mor-
gan waS a remarkable imitation of Joey Gougenheim. In
1858 the Royal gave “ Robin,,Hood” ; in 1859 “ Humpty
Dumpty," and in 1860 “ Little Jack Horner.” Sir William
and Lady Don were in the panto, of '61, “Harlequin
Valentine and Orson, or the Home of Romance and Tricks
of the Spirit of Fun.” Wybert Reeve writes, in his
recollections, that Lady Don died in aScoteh workhouse.
In 1862 Operatic Lyster, Musgrove’s uncle, had the
theatre, and his panto, was “ Harlequin Arabian Nights,”
the principal characters by Georgia Hodson and piquant
Kate Warde, who used to divide popularity on Ballarat
and Bendigo with Kate O’Reilly. In 1863 Barry Sullivan
was manager of the Royal, with the panto, of “ Lalla
Ropkh.” Rose Edouini (Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis) acted Fera-
morz, the Troubadour. Khorsanbad was alternated by
Charley Young and Dick Stewart. At Christmas, 1864.
Sullivan gave “ The Enchanted Island.” Lyster vvas at
the Royal again in ’65 with “Oberon.” In ’66 the
managers were Hoskins and Clarance Holt, with the
panto, ofGulliver's Travels.” In '67 the co-operative
management of JLambert, Vincent, Harwood, Bellalr,
Stewart, and Hennings gave “Tom the Piper’s Son and
Mary Quite Contrary.” The panto, of 1868 was “ Robin-
son Crusoe”; 1869, “Harlequin and The House that
Jack Built, or the Loves of Jack Melbourne and Little
Victoria.” 1870, “ Goody Two Shoes and Little Boy-
Blue,” by Marcus Clarke.

“ B." : Wandered into the Gaiety (Melbourne),, the
other night, expecting something good from Frank
Clark, of the one-time famous, or notorious. “ Silk Stock-
ings” company. But it was a frost. Clark, done up as a
fireman, came in and bawled out some execrable verses
about the Lion-hearted Fire Brigade.” He wasn’t funny
—he wasn’t even vulgar.
